Received 
      23rd June 2020
    First published on 19th October 2020AbstractDramatically increased CO2 concentration from several point sources is perceived to cause severe greenhouse effect towards the serious ongoing global warming with associated climate destabilization, inducing undesirable natural calamities, melting of glaciers, and extreme weather patterns. CO2 capture and utilization (CCU) has received tremendous attention due to its significant role in intensifying global warming. Considering the lack of a timely review on the state-of-the-art progress of promising CCU techniques, developing an appropriate and prompt summary of such advanced techniques with a comprehensive understanding is necessary. Thus, it is imperative to provide a timely review, given the fast growth of sophisticated CO2 capture and utilization materials and their implementation. In this work, we critically summarized and comprehensively reviewed the characteristics and performance of both liquid and solid CO2 adsorbents with possible schemes for the improvement of their CO2 capture ability and advances in CO2 utilization. Their industrial applications in pre- and post-combustion CO2 capture as well as utilization were systematically discussed and compared. With our great effort, this review would be of significant importance for academic researchers for obtaining an overall understanding of the current developments and future trends of CCU. This work is bound to benefit researchers in fields relating to CCU and facilitate the progress of significant breakthroughs in both fundamental research and commercial applications to deliver perspective views for future scientific and industrial advances in CCU.
Wanlin Gao
Wanlin Gao is currently a PhD candidate at the College of Environmental Science and Engineering, Beijing Forestry University in China under the supervision of Prof. Qiang Wang. Her research interests include the fabrication and application of intermediate-temperature solid CO2 adsorbents for sorption-enhanced water gas shift reaction and the deployment of in situ and operando analysis for CO2 capture mechanism study.
Qiang Wang
Professor Qiang Wang received BSc (2003) and MSc (2005) from Harbin Institute of Technology in China, and PhD (2009) from POSTECH in South Korea. In 2009–2011, he worked as a research fellow at the Institute of Chemical and Engineering Sciences under A*STAR, Singapore. In 2011–2012, he worked as a postdoctoral associate at the Department of Chemistry, University of Oxford. Since 2012, he holds a full professor position at the College of Environmental Science and Engineering, Beijing Forestry University. He serves as the section editor (capture, storage, and chemical conversion of carbon dioxide) of the Journal of Energy Chemistry and the editorial boards of several scientific journals. His current research interests include environmental functional nanomaterials for air pollution control and CO2 capture and utilizations (CCU).
Anne-Cecile Roger
Professor Anne-Cécile Roger obtained her PhD from the University of Strasbourg in 1995 in the field of heterogeneous catalysis (oxidative coupling of methane). She obtained her Accreditation to Direct Research in 2003 around the direct and indirect valorization of natural gas. She got her first position as an Assistant Professor in 1996 and became full professor in 2011 at the same university. She is the deputy director of ICPEES (Institute of Chemistry and Processes for Energy, Environment and Health, UMR CNRS 7515) since 2018. After a strong experience in Fischer–Tropsch synthesis for almost 10 years, she has been, since more than ten years, involved in the development of mixed oxide-type catalytic materials for CO2 hydrogenation (methane, methanol, DME, higher alcohols) and Power-to-X technologies.
Rose Amal
Professor Rose Amal is a Scientia Professor in the School of Chemical Engineering, UNSW Sydney. Her current research focuses on designing nanomaterials for solar to chemical energy conversion applications (including photo and electrocatalysis for water and air purification, water splitting, CO2 reduction) and engineering systems for solar-induced processes. Prof. Amal is a Fellow of the Australian Academy of Technological Sciences and Engineering (FTSE), a Fellow of Australian Academy of Science (FAA). She has received the nation's top civilian honour – the Companion of the Order of Australia – as part of the 2018 Queen's Birthday Honours for her eminent service to chemical engineering, and was named 2019 NSW Scientist of the Year.

Prof. Hong He is the Member of the Chinese Academy of Engineering, the Deputy Director of the Research Center for Eco-Environmental Sciences (RCEES), Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS). Prof. He received his doctorate (PhD) from the University of Tokyo in 1994. He studied and worked in Japan, the United States, and Canada for 11 years, and thereafter joined RCEES through the “Hundred Talents Program” of the CAS in 2001. Sang-Eon Park
Prof. Sang-Eon Park is the Emeritus Professor of Inha Univ., and has been the director of the Laboratory of Nano-Green Catalysis and Nano Center. He has a PhD (1981) from KAIST and experience from Chon Engrg. Co. for 6 years with a post-doc at Texas A. & M. He worked as a principal investigator in KRICT for 16 years. Now, he is the Editor-in-Chief of the Journal of CO2 Utilization and also serves as the Honorary Prof. of several Universities, the editorial boards of several scientific journals, and as an advisory member of several leading international conferences including ICCDU. He has published more than 350 scientific articles and 80 patents, with 7 industrialized projects. His current research interest involves carbon dioxide utilization as an oxidant coined as Soft Oxidant, Promoter, and Enhancer.
1. Introduction
Despite the emergence of alternative and unconventional energy resources, the world's primary energy resource is still fossil fuels and their substitution could not be developed for the coming decades.1–3 With growing fossil fuel utilization and industrial activities, the amount of CO2 emission is continuously rising, and is considered as a primary contributor to the increasingly severe climate change issue.4–7 The average concentration of atmospheric CO2 has shown an extraordinary increase, ranging from 172 to 300 ppm before the latest industrial age to 416.47 ppm on May 30, 2020 according to Earth's CO2.8 Past decades have witnessed great concerns on the catastrophic consequences resulting from ever-rising CO2 emission, for instance, climate change disaster as well as global warming and related energy and environmental issues. Many studies on the CCU scheme are therefore stimulated and proposed. The CCU scenario is regarded as a prominent method for combating anthropogenic carbon emission (Fig. 1). CO2 capture is the most economical feasible method to curb carbon emissions from industrial point sources. Subsequent utilization refers to the deployment of captured CO2 for the synthesis of value-added chemical compounds or enhanced oil recovery.9 Overall, CCU processes are of vital importance for addressing global emission issues and carbon-intensive production.
 Fig. 1  Industrial carbon dioxide capture and utilization chain scheme (Reproduced from ref. 10 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
Recent years have witnessed the achievement of great progress and rapid development in the field of promising CCU materials and their implementation for the effective reduction of CO2 emissions. Considering the lack of a timely review on the state-of-the-art progress of promising CCU techniques, especially from 2017 to date, developing an appropriate and prompt summary of these advanced techniques with a comprehensive understanding is highly necessary. This work aims to be an authoritative, comprehensive, and critical review of the sustainable development for advanced improvements in CCU (storage is not involved in this work), which has received tremendous attention due to its high potential in intensifying global warming. Herein, we summarized the characteristics and properties of both liquid and solid CO2 capturing materials under various operational conditions with possible schemes for the improvement in their CO2 capture ability and CO2 utilization advances in different fields, with their industrial applications systematically compared and discussed.
2. Industrial carbon dioxide capture
CO2 capture is widely recognized as an essential procedure to curb carbon emission from fossil fuel combustion.11,12 At least 550 Gt of CO2 should be removed by the end of this century to limit the atmospheric CO2 level below 350 ppm.13 Generally, CO2 can be trapped via three major methods: pre-combustion, post-combustion, and oxy-fuel combustion.14 The post-combustion process adsorbs CO2 generated from carbon-containing materials after combustion or from flue miasma after fossil commodities.15 Pre-combustion technology employs CO2 separation from fossil commodities prior to the burning process. The oxy-fuel combustion process reduces the quantities of N2 and utilizes pure O2 in the combustion process.16
Post-combustion carbon capture requires minimal retrofitting existing facilities and therefore is regarded as the most efficient process for carbon emission mitigation. The commonly utilized CO2 capture processes for PCC are the liquid-based CO2 capture system and low-temperature solid CO2 capture materials system. Meanwhile, for certain industries, high-temperature solid CO2 capture materials system could also be deployed. Liquid-based CO2 capture system can be divided into two dominant fields, which are liquid amine- and ionic liquid-based systems. Typical low-temperature solid CO2 capture adsorbents consist of solid-amine, carbon, zeolite, MOF, and alkali carbonate-based materials. However, the relatively low CO2 partial pressure from the flue gas remains a great challenge from the separation perspective, which causes poor driving force towards CO2 capture and a large gas volume to be treated.17
The pre-combustion process can be applied in various industrial processes (e.g., IGCC coal fired power plant, biomass hydrogen generation power plant, and natural gas hydrogen power plant). According to the operation temperature of different industrial processes, the commonly utilized solid CO2 capture materials can be divided into intermediate-temperature adsorbents (LDHs and MgO) and high-temperature CO2 sorbents (alkali silicate, CaO, and alkali titanate). However, the main problems for current pre- and post-combustion CO2 capture include the further improvement of reversible CO2 uptake, long-term regenerability, moisture effect, and the optimization of capture kinetics.
2.1. Liquid-based CO2 capture system
Considering the severe global warming issue, it is urgent to develop economic and feasible CO2 capture technologies at the industrial level. Generally, PCC is usually associated with CO2/N2 separation in exhaust flue gas derived from fossil fuel combustion. The main technologies utilized for PCC are chemical absorption, solid adsorption, and membrane separation. Benefiting from the high reactivity with CO2, chemical absorption offers a rapid absorption rate and superior capture efficiency. In addition, the absorption process using liquid-based solvent/absorbent could be easily retrofitted into existing fuel-fired power plants, which has emerged as a very promising technology to capture CO2 in short-to-medium terms. Statistics has shown that absorption-based processes take up about 60% of PCC technologies.18 Amine-based solvents, such as MEA, are the most mature absorbents due to their high absorption rate, large CO2 capacity, low viscosity, and high chemical/thermal stability.19 Nevertheless, amine-based CO2 absorption systems suffer from excessive regeneration energy, equipment corrosion, and amine degradation during operation. Extensive research interest has been devoted for the development of novel liquid-based absorbents for CO2 capture. The main technology options for liquid-based CO2 capture are illustrated in Fig. 2. A detailed overview should be focused on the recent progress in liquid-based CO2 capture systems, including liquid amine-based solvents and ionic liquids (ILs)-based materials from the perspective of molecular engineering.
 Fig. 2  Technology options for liquid-based CO2 capture and strategies to improve the CO2 capture performance. (i) Blended amine solvents; (ii) non-aqueous amine solvents; (iii) biphasic solvents (Reproduced from ref. 20 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018); (iv) catalyst-aided regeneration; (v) ionic liquids (Reproduced from ref. 21 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018); (vi) ionic liquid and amine blend. 

2.1.1 Liquid amine-based solvents. 
Aqueous amines are widely used liquid absorbents for PCC due to their high absorption rate, large CO2 capacity, low viscosity, and high chemical/thermal stability.19 A typical CO2 capture process using absorption/desorption protocol is demonstrated in Fig. 3. In conventional CO2 absorption processes, liquid-based solvents contact with the flue gas counter-currently in the absorber and absorb CO2via chemical reaction at 40–60 °C. The purified gas leaves the absorber, usually achieving a CO2 removal efficiency of 90%. Subsequently, the captured CO2 is released from the CO2-rich solution upon thermal stripping at relatively high temperatures (100–120 °C). The high selectivity of chemical absorption enables a nearly pure CO2 product at the outlet of the stripper. Owing to their high reactivity towards chemical absorption, amine-based absorbents exhibit great advantage in CO2 capture from dilute, low-pressure exhaust gas streams, making the amine scrubbing technology suitable for most existing large-scale, fixed-point CO2 emission sources. However, the reactive nature of chemical absorption poses a challenge due to the high desorption energy demand.
 Fig. 3  The typical absorption/desorption process for CO2 capture using liquid-based solvents and solution characteristics favorable for low regeneration energy consumption. 
In general, the energy consumed in the absorption/desorption process for CO2 capture includes regeneration heat needed in the reboiler, electrical power to drive the circulating pumps, and compressor work for CO2 desorption. The primary obstacle in implementing this “wet-scrubbing” technology is the large regeneration heat needed in the reboiler. The regeneration energy originates from three parts: (1) the sensible heat for solvent heating, (2) the reaction heat for CO2 desorption, and (3) the latent heat to evaporate the solvent, especially water for aqueous amine solvents. These three parts are associated with the inherent properties of amine solvents. The solution characteristics favorable for low regeneration energy consumption are listed in Fig. 3. First, high cyclic capacity (i.e., the difference in the CO2 loading in CO2-rich solution and CO2-lean solution, g CO2 kg−1 solution or mol CO2 L−1 solution), low heat capacity, and low stripping volume are favorable for low sensible heat. Usually, a solvent with large absorption capacity for absorption and superior desorption ability for desorption possesses a high cyclic capacity. Second, low reaction enthalpy for CO2 desorption is beneficial for low reaction heat. However, low reaction enthalpy might be associated with a large stripping steam requirement and a large latent heat. Third, low stripping steam and low evaporation enthalpy are conducive for obtaining low latent heat. In addition, other parameters, such as amine degradation, equipment corrosion, viscosity, reaction kinetic, cost, and environment friendliness should also be taken into account when exploring novel solvents.
2.1.1.1 Basic principle of liquid amine-based absorbents. 
As the most mature amine absorbent, MEA has been employed in industrial carbon capture for over 50 years. Besides, several other amines, e.g., DEA and N-MDEA, have also been widely studied. They absorb and desorb CO2via a reversible reaction with CO2. The corresponding CO2 absorption performance depends largely on the chemical structure of the selected amine. According to the hydrogen number bonded to the nitrogen atom, amines can be categorized into three types: primary amine (with –NH2 group), secondary amine (with –NH group), and tertiary amine (with –N group). Considering the difference in the basicity of various amines, the binding energy between CO2 and amines follows the order: primary amine > secondary amine > tertiary amine. The net reactions between various amines and CO2 are: Primary/secondary amine: CO2 + 2R1R2NH ↔ R1R2NH2+ + R1R2NCOO−(1) Tertiary amine: CO2 + H2O + R1R2R3N ↔ R1R2R3NH+ + HCO3−(2)where R1 and R3 refer to the alkyl/alkanol group, and R2 refers to the hydrogen atom for primary amine or the alkyl/alkanol substituent for secondary amine and tertiary amine.
CO2 absorption in primary and secondary amines is widely suggested to follow the zwitterion mechanism.22,23 As described in eqn (1), primary and secondary amines will form a carbamate after CO2 reaction, thus offering rapid absorption rate but high regeneration energy associated with the stronger C–N bonds generated in the carbamate.24 The enthalpy for CO2 absorption reaction is usually about 80–90 kJ mol−1 CO2 for primary amines and 70–75 kJ mol−1 CO2 for secondary amines. In contrast, CO2 is absorbed into tertiary amines according to the base-catalysed hydration mechanism, wherein tertiary amines could promote CO2 hydrolysis towards bicarbonate formation (eqn (2)), thereby providing a high CO2 capacity and low regeneration heat but slow absorption rate. Moreover, the CO2 absorption enthalpy in tertiary amine generally varies from 40 to 55 kJ mol−1 CO2. Thus, a trade-off between the absorption rate and the regeneration energy exists for CO2 capture using a single amine. Clearly, CO2 absorption using a single amine cannot achieve all the desired performances.
The absorption process using liquid amine solvents is recognized as one of the most effective methodologies for CO2 capture from power plants that burn coal or natural gas. Nevertheless, such amine-scrubbing processes face several challenges. The most important drawback is the expensive operating cost due to the energy demand for solvent regeneration, though it is considered as the cheapest PCC technology available.25 The energy-intensive regeneration process consumes above 60% of the overall energy demand during carbon capture. In particular, the regeneration energy for MEA (nearly 4.0 GJ t−1 CO2) greatly exceeds the theoretical limit and the acceptable value (0.40 and 0.72 GJ t−1 CO2, respectively). Secondly, amine degradation is also considered as a major obstacle in CO2 capture using amine-based absorbents as it increases the operation cost by 10% of the total CO2 capture cost and generates potential secondary pollutants. Also, some of the degradation products have shown to increase corrosion in both carbon steel and stainless-steel systems. Recently, to reduce the amine loss and operational cost induced by amine degradation, amines and amine blends with high degradation resistance were investigated. Also, many inhibitors can be used as radical or oxygen scavengers so as to decrease amine degradation. Thirdly, equipment corrosion is another challenge in the CO2 absorption process, which involves electrochemical reaction with wet CO2 or CO2-loaded amine solution that reduces the equipment lifetime and even causes untimely downtime of the plant and safety accidents. Amine-scrubbing apparatuses, usually made of carbon steel materials, are very prone to corrosion, especially in hot areas such as the regenerator and the heat exchanger. The corrosion rates are related to CO2 concentration, temperature, some of the degradation products, oxygen content, and the amine used.
In addition, heat stable salts (HSS) are usually formed from the irreversible reaction of amines with their degradation products or other impurities in the feed gas.26 They cannot absorb CO2 or decompose through thermal solvent regeneration. The accumulative HSS in the absorption system can lead to several operational problems including CO2 capacity reduction, excessive corrosion, as well as foaming and fouling issues.27 Generally, thermal reclaiming (distillation), electrodialysis (ED), and ion exchange are considered as the most prominent methods for the removal of dissolved HSS from the spent lean amine absorbents. Thermal reclaiming includes the distillation of the purified amine away from the bottoms containing HSS, which is limited by the large energy consumption. ED separates HSS using a direct-current (DC) electric field, which is an energy-efficient procedure with a small footprint and no additional chemicals are required.28 Nevertheless, the ED process is subjected to excessive amine loss in the DC field. Basic ion exchange resins containing hydroxide ions (NaOH or KOH) can exchange the HSS anions to hydroxide ions and restore the CO2 absorption capacity of the spent amines. However, this process consumes a large amount of NaOH or KOH, and the generated alkaline wastewater causes severe secondary pollution.29 Therefore, these limitations promote the further study of chemical absorption for CO2 capture.
2.1.1.2 Progress in liquid amine-based absorbents. 
The three major disadvantages of the amine-scrubbing process, i.e., high regeneration energy, amine degradation, and equipment corrosion, are related to the inherent properties of the selected amines, e.g. such as the low thermal stability, corrosive nature, and high enthalpy for CO2 desorption. For the high regeneration heat, in particular, the optional characteristics for energy-efficient solvents are listed in Fig. 3. On the basis of these considerations, extensive research interest has been focused on developing alternative solvents to conventional amines. In view of the good CO2 absorption performance of amine solvents, most of the research has been focused on the development of liquid amine-based absorbents. Various alternative amine-based absorbents, including blended amine solvents, non-aqueous amine-based solvents, and biphasic solvents have been fabricated to optimize the carbon capture performance of amine scrubbing so as to eliminate the drawbacks. Also, catalyst-aided regeneration is proposed as a potential approach for achieving CO2 desorption below 100 °C and for substantially decreasing the energy demand. Therefore, a focused progress review is given on these four types of liquid amine-based absorbents in the following section.
(1) Blended amine solvents. 
To address the above-mentioned limitations using a single amine for CO2 capture, a number of blends of commercial primary, secondary, and tertiary amines have been developed. The blended amine solvents can reconcile the advantages of different amines, specifically, the fast absorption rate derived from primary/secondary amines together with the low desorption reaction enthalpy and high absorption capacity derived from tertiary/sterically-hindered amines, so as to yield a new-generation of amine-based absorbents for CO2 capture. Bi-amine blend usually introduces certain primary/secondary amines to aqueous tertiary amines, obtaining an optimized performance. For instance, the blending of a small amount of MEA into aqueous MDEA helped to increase the CO2 absorption speed over MDEA and reduced the regeneration heat with respect to single MEA. In addition, increasing the MEA/MDEA ratio can also enhance the reaction kinetics and mass transfer rate. A further increase in the component number to form tri- or quad-amine blends could help to maximize the absorption/desorption speed of CO2 and to minimize the regeneration heat. Blended amine solvents containing multi-components have presented significant improvements in the overall performance such as reaction kinetics, CO2 solubility, mass transfer, stability, corrosion issue, and regeneration heat. In recent publications, a large number of screening studies on bi- and tri-amine blends have been conducted. Considering the complex composition of blended amine absorbents, the reaction mechanism of CO2 absorption is complicated. Liu introduced the blended amine concept into non-aqueous solvents and compared the reaction mechanism in TETA–AMP aqueous and non-aqueous solvents by 13C NMR.30 The carbamate was firstly formed and then CO32−/HCO3− was generated during CO2 absorption (Fig. 4). Accordingly, CO32−/HCO3− was desorbed prior to the desorption of carbamates. On the contrary, carbamate and C2H5OCO2− were formed in the non-aqueous counterpart.
 Fig. 4  NMR spectra of the CO2-loaded TETA/AMP/water blended absorbent (a) in the absorption step and (b) in the desorption step. (c) The CO2 absorption/desorption pathways in aqueous and non-aqueous solvents (Reproduced from ref. 30 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). 
Besides the lab-scaled experiments for screening blended amine solvents, a pilot plant study on carbon capture using MEA–MDEA (University of Regina), AMP–DA2MP, and PZ–MDEA has been conducted recently. 2M AMP–1.5 M DA2MP possesses increased mass transfer and CO2 absorption efficiency than that of MEA. In addition, due to the lower reaction enthalpy between CO2 and AMP–DA2MP, the regeneration energy was 13.8% lower than that in MEA. Though blended amine solvents are generally more expensive than single amines, the improved overall performance in both lab-scale studies and pilot plant tests demonstrates this type of solvents as promising alternatives for conventional single amines. Moreover, the blended amine solvents could use the typical amine absorption process without further modifications, which is expected to be commercialized in the near future. However, many other factors should be taken into account for the exploration of industrial blended amine solvents, such as viscosity, volatility, toxicity, degradation, and market price.
(2) Non-aqueous amine-based solvents. 
For a typical aqueous amine absorbent, several problems are associated with the diluent water. Its high heat capacity and high vaporization heat are not conducive for energy-efficient regeneration. In addition, equipment corrosion is certainly related to the aqueous conditions. Recently, considerable research attention has turned to novel potential non-aqueous absorbents. Several organic molecules, e.g., EG and diethylene glycol, featuring low heat capacity, evaporation enthalpy (almost half of that of water), and stripping temperature, can be alternatives to water.30 When using these non-aqueous amine-based solvents, most of the required regeneration energy is used to break the CO2-associated products, while the sensible heat and solvent vaporization heat are significantly decreased due to the relatively low heat capacity and evaporation enthalpy of the organic diluent. Therefore, non-aqueous amine solvents enable the feasibility of reducing the energy consumption while maintaining the CO2 absorption reactivity of aqueous amines.31 In addition, compared with traditional aqueous solvents, lower stripping temperatures also can reduce amine losses, thermal degradation, oxidative degradation, and equipment corrosion.32
Lately, intensive research efforts have been devoted to exploring novel non-aqueous absorbents with superior performances. For instance, the AMP–AEEA–NMP non-aqueous solvent was developed with CO2 uptake of 1.65 mmol g−1.32 The low heat capacity of NMP was conducive for low sensible heat and the low evaporation enthalpy of NMP was conducive for low latent heat, thereby resulting in a total regeneration heat down to 2.09 GJ t−1 CO2, which is approximately half of that of the aqueous 5 M MEA solution. Recently, non-aqueous 2-piperidineethanol–ethylene glycol solvent reached an absorption capacity of 0.97 mol CO2 mol−1 through the formation of carbonate species and CO2 desorption occurred at 50 °C, providing a potential candidate for conventional amine solvents.33
Barzagli et al.34,35 compared the CO2 absorption feature in several aqueous and non-aqueous amine absorbents. 13C NMR spectroscopy was found to be a valuable technique to evaluate the species distribution and reaction mechanism in the amine/CO2/solvent systems. When reacting with CO2, carbamate and bicarbonate were formed in the aqueous solution, whereas only carbamate was formed in the non-aqueous solution (Fig. 5). Thus, a higher absorption rate but lower CO2 loading was obtained in non-aqueous solutions. Lately, two kinds of glycol ethers have been used to dilute MEA instead of water.36 Similarly, only carbamate was generated in the non-aqueous solvent, whereas both carbamate and bicarbonate were detected using 13C NMR. The non-aqueous solvents exhibited a higher desorption efficiency, larger cyclic capacity (1.45 mmol g−1, Fig. 5b), and lower vaporization heat than that of aqueous 5 M MEA. Consequently, the non-aqueous solvents substantially reduce the regeneration energy by 55% compared to the aqueous 5 M MEA system (Fig. 5c). However, it is noted that the viscosity of non-aqueous amines is significantly increased with CO2 loading.
 Fig. 5  (a) 13C NMR spectra and the corresponding CO2 absorption behavior of aqueous and non-aqueous amine absorbents (Reproduced from ref. 34 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). (b) The absorption/desorption cycles of CO2 in MEA/2ME solutions.36 (c) The comparison of regeneration heat duty in aqueous and non-aqueous MEA solutions (Reproduced from ref. 36 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). (d) The construction and optimization of functionalized ethylenediamines (Reproduced from ref. 37 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2019). 
The rapid viscosity increase during CO2 absorption in non-aqueous amine-based absorbents was recognized as a crucial factor for industrial application by deteriorating the mass transfer and liquid transportation. Based on the correlation of the structure, Liu et al.37 put forward a novel strategy to reduce the viscosity of CO2-loaded non-aqueous solution. A series of ethylenediamine derivatives were constructed as non-aqueous solvents for CO2 capture (Fig. 5d). Favorably low viscosities and good regeneration in the range of 50–80 °C were achieved. In addition, the use of high volatility alcohols as organic diluents is susceptible to severe solvent loss.38 Likewise, poor regeneration performance without N2 sweeping was reported for some non-aqueous amine-based solvents. Prior to applying non-aqueous amine solvents into industrial application, several lab-scale studies are needed to explore remarkable overall performance for carbon capture.
(3) Biphasic solvents. 
Due to the advantages of low stripping volume and regeneration energy consumption, biphasic solvents have received great attention.39 Depending on the phase state of the CO2-rich phase, they are categorized into liquid–liquid and liquid–solid biphasic solvents. Initiated by CO2 absorption or temperature change, the homogenous absorbent can be separated into two single layers. One is the CO2-lean layer and the other is the CO2-rich layer containing most of the captured CO2. Only the CO2-rich phase, which is associated with a small volume flow and highly concentrated CO2, needs stripping, thereby enabling decreased sensible heat and vaporization heat as well as increased CO2 pressure. As a result, an energy-efficient regeneration process and decreased CO2 compression work would be realized.40
IFP Energies Nouvelles firstly developed a DMX process using the thermomorphic biphasic solvent DMX™. It only requires 2.1 GJ t−1 CO2 to desorb CO2, while the regeneration heat is up to 4.0 GJ t−1 CO2 for the conventional 5 M MEA system.41,42 Nowadays, mixed-amine biphasic solvents have attracted intensive attention, which usually contain a primary/secondary amine and a tertiary amine. During the CO2 absorption reaction, the primary or secondary amine with –NH2 or –NH sites, such as MEA and DETA, serves as the absorption accelerator and generates carbamate and protonated amine with high ionic strength. The unreacted tertiary amine with a certain hydrophobicity, such as N,N-dimethylcyclohexylamine and DEEA, contributes to liquid–liquid phase separation. For instance, mixed-amine solvents such as DETA/DEEA, TETA/N,N-dimethylcyclohexylamine,43 and DETA/pentamethyldiethylenetriamine exhibited phase change behavior during CO2 absorption. DEEA–MEA and DEEA–AEEA mixed-amine biphasic solvents showed a high cyclic capacity (0.64 mol CO2 mol−1), fast phase split time (few minutes), and low regeneration energy (2.56 GJ t−1 CO2).
Notably, high viscosity is usually associated with CO2-loaded biphasic solvents, especially due to the highly concentrated CO2-rich phase. The high viscosity could be regulated by the addition of AMP.44 In addition, an excessive rich phase volume ratio with high CO2 loading was found for the mixed amine biphasic solvent, which was detrimental for reducing the regeneration energy consumption.45 Later on, 1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone was used to replace water partially to advance the phase-splitting property of the mixed amine biphasic solvent.46 Decreasing the water content resulted in improved phase-splitting behavior, decreased CO2 capacity, and decreased regeneration energy. Shen et al.40 proposed a two-stage reaction mechanism between the mixed-amine biphasic solvents and CO2 (Fig. 6a). With the consumption of the unreacted amine groups, the reaction process changed from absorption kinetics control to thermodynamics control. A low temperature at the first stage and an elevated temperature at the second stage were recommended for CO2 absorption.
 Fig. 6  (a) A possible two-stage reaction mechanism between the mixed-amine biphasic solvent BDA–DMCA and CO2 (Reproduced from ref. 40 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2020). (b) CO2 absorption mechanism for the MEA–sulfolane biphasic solvent (Reproduced from ref. 20 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). (c) Volume distribution variation of CO2-loaded biphasic solvents with and without sulfolane.47 (d) CO2 loading in both the layers of CO2-loaded biphasic solvents with and without sulfolane (Reproduced from ref. 47 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). 
Recently, some organic solvents, such as sufolane and 1-propanol, have been reported to trigger the phase splitting of amine absorbents due to a difference in the hydrophilicity.48–50 The CO2 absorption mechanism for the MEA–sulfolane biphasic solvent is illustrated in Fig. 6b.20 Specifically, during CO2 absorption, sulfolane promoted the phase separation of the MEA solvent, generating a hydrophilic CO2-enriched phase together with a hydrophobic CO2-lean phase. The CO2 absorption speed was accelerated owing to the strong interaction of sulfolane and CO2. Also, the regeneration energy required for stripping such a CO2-rich phase was limited to 2.67 GJ t−1 CO2. In addition, sulfolane was chosen to tune the phase-splitting properties of the DEEA–TETA solvent (Fig. 6c and d).47 Herein, the addition of sulfolane increased the hydrophobic composition in the absorbent, thus decreasing the volume percentage of the hydrophilic CO2-enriched phase.47 The energy required for regenerating the highly concentrated CO2-rich phase was reduced to 1.81 GJ t−1 CO2. To date, developing novel biphasic solvents with low regeneration heat, fast absorption rate, large CO2 absorption capacity, low viscosity, high stability, and low volatility is still a major challenge. Moreover, the optimization of the existing CO2 absorption processes using biphasic solvents needs to be carried out to further decrease the energy consumption and operating costs for CO2 capture.
(4) Catalyst-aided regeneration. 
As a newly developed approach, catalyst-aided regeneration is still in the preliminary stage of exploration. In this scenario, solid acid catalysts, e.g., metal oxides derivatives and functional molecular sieves, have been introduced into amine absorbents to facilitate the breakdown of carbamate species and then release the absorbed CO2 at relatively low temperatures.51 Consequently, solvent regeneration at lower temperatures could be achieved, thus substantially reducing the stripping steam and the regeneration energy. In addition, several drawbacks associated with high temperature, such as the degradation and corrosion issues, would be relieved.
Several types of solid acid catalysts have been developed and evaluated for the catalytic ability to desorb CO2 at lower temperatures and to decrease the energy demand in solvent regeneration. HZSM-5 and γ-Al2O3 solid acid catalysts have been revealed to optimize CO2 desorption and decrease the regeneration energy effectively. In addition, the acidic strength and the Brønsted to Lewis acid ratio are important for the solid catalysts to facilitate CO2 desorption. Lately, the TiO(OH)2 nanocatalyst has been confirmed to increase the CO2 desorption rate from the MEA absorbent by over 4500%, thus ensuring feasible desorption at a lower temperature.52 ZrO2 and ZnO were reported to increase the CO2 desorption efficiency, CO2 cyclic capacity, and CO2 desorption rate by 32%, 56%, and 54%, respectively.53 Ag2O–Ag2CO3 exhibited superior catalytic activity in solvent regeneration, which could increase the CO2 desorption rate by 1000% at 80 °C.51 With the assistance of catalysts, a new route for CO2 desorption was developed with a lower activation energy. A possible reaction mechanism is illustrated in Fig. 7a. In the catalytic cycle, Ag2O and Ag2CO3 interconverted repeatedly.
 Fig. 7  (a) Possible desorption pathway using the Ag2O–Ag2CO3 catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 51 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). (b) The possible desorption pathway using the SZMF catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 54 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). 
Liang and Tontiwachwuthikul's group have developed several solid acid catalysts including metal oxide derivatives and metal-modified molecular sieves. The corresponding regeneration energy was reduced by up to 40%.54–57 The key catalyst parameters that determine the CO2 desorption performance are the acid and basic sites, Brønsted to Lewis acid ratio, mesopore structure, and specific surface area. Specifically, strong acidic sites and basic sites could significantly enhance the catalytic reaction. A high Brønsted to Lewis acid ratio is conducive for the catalytic process. Also, the large mesoporous SSA increases the availability of the active sites for catalytic reaction. In addition, for dual site catalysts with both acid and basic sites, such as SZMF, were reported to exhibit good catalytic activity.54,56,57 A possible desorption pathway using the SZMF catalyst was proposed (Fig. 7b).54 The zwitterion mechanism dominated the MEA regeneration process and involved two steps including the breakdown of carbamates and the deprotonation of protonated amines. On the other hand, the basic sites facilitated the deprotonation reaction by taking the protons away from the protonated amines to the carbamates, and the metal atoms (Fe, Zr) and acid sites facilitated the transformation of MEA-carbamate to MEA and CO2. The novel pathway provided an energy-efficient opportunity for low temperature CO2 desorption.
The catalyst-aid regeneration approach exhibits great potential in reducing the required regeneration heat duty. Nevertheless, as a newly-developed approach under preliminary exploration, the research on catalyst-aided regeneration is limited to few kinds of catalysts, some of which are expensive, unstable, and difficult to separate, rendering this catalyst-aided CO2 desorption barely feasible. It is crucial to design solid acid catalysts with high catalytic performance, good stability, inexpensive cost, and easy separation. Moreover, establishing a feasible absorption–desorption process with solid acid catalysts is necessary for future industrialization.
2.1.1.3 Summary and outlook of liquid amine-based absorbents. 
Exploring novel efficient absorbents and developing the CO2 capture process with suitable heat integration remain major challenges in liquid amine-based CO2 capture. Among the promising amine-based solvents under investigation, blended amine solvents are promising for use in industrial implementation in the near future as they preserve excellent CO2 absorption performance including rapid absorption speed and low regeneration heat, and the traditional amine absorption process can be used almost directly without further changes. Both lab-scale and pilot plant studies validated the feasibility of blended amine solvents as promising alternatives for conventional single amines. To explore the industrial scale blended amine solvents, not only the performance-limited factors, such as the cyclic capacity, absorption kinetics, and desorption enthalpy, but also many other factors, such as the viscosity, volatility, toxicity, degradation, corrosion, and market price, should be taken into account.
In the last decade, screening studies on biphasic solvents have been extensively conducted. They are capable of decreasing the low regeneration heat without sacrificing the CO2 capacity and absorption rate. Nevertheless, the problems of high regeneration temperature as well as high viscosity accompanied by biphasic solvents need to be carefully studied. So far, developing biphasic solvents with good overall performance including low regeneration heat, rapid absorption speed, large CO2 absorption capacity, low degradation, low viscosity, low corrosivity, and low volatility for further scale-up pilot tests is highly required. Furthermore, to achieve industrial level low-energy CO2 capture, process establishment and optimization need to be performed urgently.
Non-aqueous amine absorbents and catalyst-aid generation are still under preliminary exploration. Their feasibility should be evaluated prior to industrial utilization. Non-aqueous amine absorbents provide great opportunities to strip CO2 with decreased regeneration heat at lower temperature, thereby reducing amine loss and equipment corrosion as well. Solvent screening and the study on the CO2 absorption/desorption mechanism are still in progress. The rapid viscosity increases during CO2 absorption, high volatility when using alcohols as organic diluent, and poor regeneration performance without N2 sweeping should be paid more attention. Catalyst-aid generation is still under lab-scale exploration for the design of efficient catalysts and the study on the mechanism of catalytic desorption, which might be industrialized in the medium to long term. By now, only a few solid acid catalysts have been proposed. It is highly desirable to design efficient solid acid catalysts for low-energy CO2 desorption with good stability and low cost. In addition, dedicated processes for each technology option should be developed to drastically reduce the energy consumption and the operating cost.


2.1.2 Ionic liquid-based solvents for CO2 capture. 
A lot of research work has been conducted to explore alternative absorbents for traditional amine-based solvents. ILs are organic salts and are generally composed of designable organic cation and anion, which provide inherent structure tunability, high thermal stability, negligible volatility, low corrosion, and good CO2 solubility,58 thus enabling them as promising candidates for CO2 capture with low volatility, good stability, low degradation, low corrosivity, as well as low regeneration energy requirement. The main advantage of ILs in CO2 capture is that they can absorb CO2 by consuming lower regeneration heat than that consumed by amine-based solvents.
2.1.2.1 Basic principle of ionic liquid-based absorbents. 
According to the structure of ILs and the interaction between IL absorbents and CO2, they are typically divided into physical ILs (physical absorption) and functional ILs (chemical absorption). Initially, physical ILs with suitable cations and anions, such as imidazolium-based ILs, were proposed for CO2 capture. The CO2 solubility in physical ILs could be promoted through the fluorination of the anion and increment in the alkyl side chain length. The interaction (van der Waals forces) between the ILs and CO2 is usually weak, leading to low regeneration energy but limited CO2 absorption rate, capacity, and selectivity. In addition, due to the high viscosity of physical ILs, their CO2 absorption process is revealed to be controlled by limited mass transfer rather than by CO2 solubility.59 These drawbacks of physical ILs hinder their large scale industrial implementation.60 The mass transfer limitation in physical ILs could be settled by altering appropriate anions such as dicyanamide [DCN]− or tricyanomethanide [TCM]−,59 or by decreasing the gas–liquid resistance via supported ILs or encapsulated ILs.
Alternatively, functionalized ILs have been proposed to upgrade the CO2 capture performance by introducing reactive functional groups for chemical absorption. Owing to the structural tenability of ILs, it is feasible to design cation and anion groups for specific desired properties such as low viscosity, high CO2 capacity, and low reaction enthalpy. In particular, their absorption enthalpy can be conveniently regulated by the anion, which determines the regeneration heat of the ILs. Therefore, these functional ILs with chemisorption sites exhibited improved CO2 absorption performance, becoming potential alternatives for amine absorbents. In addition, ILs can directly capture CO2 without blending water diluent and the regeneration section was simplified due to the low vapor pressure of the ILs, thereby further reducing the regeneration energy as compared with the conventional amine-scrubbing process.60
2.1.2.2 Progress in ionic liquid-based solvents. 
To address the above-mentioned problems of physical ILs, certain structure modifications can be conducted by introducing suitable task-specific functional groups. The first functionalized IL was initially developed by functionalizing the imidazolium cation with a primary amine moiety.61 Similar to MEA, IL absorbed CO2 in a 1:2 stoichiometry and formed ammonium carbamate salts. Carboxylate-based or acetate-based ILs were proposed as pioneers and widely investigated for CO2 chemisorption. In particular, [Bmim][acetate] is usually used as the benchmark when developing new ILs. It possesses a large CO2 capacity and low reaction enthalpy associated with the reaction product of carboxylate. Nevertheless, their thermal degradation problem and poor mass transfer for high viscosity remain unsolved.
Lately, AA-ILs have received wide attention for their non-toxic biodegradability and large CO2 capacity. As reported, AA-ILs functionalized by an amine- or amino-acid could increase the CO2 solubility and reach a CO2 capacity of about 1.60 mol mol−1 of the absorbent.62 The CO2 capacity was influenced by the length of the amino-acids side chain and was strongly related to the amount of reactive groups (e.g., amine groups).63 However, AA-ILs still suffer from high viscosity due to the hydrogen bond network in the CO2-loaded AA-ILs solution, which might be mitigated by isolating the amino groups in the anion and, therefore, reduce the hydrogen bonding possibilities.64
Alternatively, AHA-ILs, primarily having 2-cyanopyrrole anions ([CNPyr]), are potential alternatives for CO2 chemisorption due to their high CO2 solubility (1:1 stoichiometry), high reaction rates, and good stability.65 Notably, the viscosity of the proposed ILs was quite low. The reaction mechanisms for the reaction between [DETAH][AHA] and CO2 are presented in Fig. 8a, wherein [DETAH]+ enabled a fast absorption rate of CO2, and [AHA]− ensured large absorption capacity and low regeneration heat. More specifically, the deprotonation of the alkyl chain was confirmed to be a key factor in CO2 absorption, which was determined by the basicity of the anion together with the alkyl chain length.
 Fig. 8  (a) Possible absorption and desorption pathways between [DETAH][AHA] and CO2 (Reproduced from ref. 65 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). (b) Phase separation diagram for IL–1-propanol–water biphasic solvent.66 (c) Thermodynamic of varied IL–1-propanol–water solutions at 393.15 K (Reproduced from ref. 66 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2020). (d) Plot of viscosity of imidazoline IL and amine blends (Reproduced from ref. 67 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). 
Despite their promising CO2 capture performance, functional ILs are still limited by the high cost and low gas–liquid mass transfer resulting from the high viscosity, which hinders their industrial application. Besides the composition modification on ILs, supporting, encapsulating, and using co-solvents are suggested to reduce the viscosity and solve the transport limitations. A biphasic solvent was developed by diluting dual-functionalized ionic liquid ([DETAH][Tz]) with 1-propanol–water co-solvent.66 The phase separation diagram for the IL–1-propanol–water biphasic solvent is shown in Fig. 8b. Good phase separation and low viscosity (2.57 mPa s) were obtained. The regeneration heat was 47.63% lower than that for MEA (Fig. 8c).
The high volatility, high energy penalty of amine, as well as the high cost and viscosity of ILs largely limit their further industrialization in carbon capture. Blending conventional amine solution with functional ILs as activators can effectively preserve the desired performance of amines and ILs. This strategy offers a promising absorbent with less regeneration penalty than that in the conventional amine process, as well as better process economics and higher CO2 absorption rates than that of ILs. Recently, Liang et al.67 studied the CO2 absorption performance in several low-viscosity binary absorbents containing imidazoline ILs and amines. The MDEA–[BEIM][BF4] blended absorbent exhibited large CO2 capacity, low viscosity (Fig. 8d), and good regenerability. Besides, the [bpy][BF4]–MEA binary solvent was reported to achieve 15% energy saving and 7.44% reduction in cost than that of the conventional MEA (30 wt%) solvent. Also, the increase in the [bpy][BF4] concentration would still further reduce the total energy and operating cost. Overall, ILs and amine blends are promising candidates, which can achieve energy-saving and cost-effective CO2 capture.
2.1.2.3 Summary and outlook of ionic liquid-based solvents. 
ILs are potential candidates for CO2 absorption due to their low volatility, good stability, low corrosivity, low degradation, and low regeneration heat. However, several obstacles, such as the high cost and excessive viscosity, limit their further application. The mass transfer problem and increased pump work for liquid transportation would be caused by the high viscosity. The design of task-specific ILs is required to optimize the viscosity, cost, and reaction enthalpy of the ILs. Molecular simulation is powerful to screen the ILs and to explore the role of various cations and anions. At the present stage, it is difficult for pure ILs to compete with conventional amine solvents. The viscosity could be reduced by blending the ILs with the co-solvent or amine solution, and the cost issue can be balanced by the decreased regeneration heat. Therefore, precise feasibility and economic assessment are needed before the industrial scale application of ILs. In addition, the pilot scale test on IL-based absorbents should be necessarily performed to evaluate their carbon capture performance.


2.1.3 Implementation and challenges in the applications. 
A large number of amine-based absorption technologies have been investigated at the pilot- and demonstration-scales. To optimize the absorption performance and to reduce the energy demand of CO2 capture from power plant emissions, some configurations and process integration, for instance, absorber intercooling, solvent split-flow, flash stripper, and stripper inter-heating, have been intensively investigated.68,69 Though higher plant efficiency would be achieved by the process of integration, the increase in the investment cost is inevitable due to the increased complexity of the process,70 which should be techno-economically evaluated prior to commercialization. The combination of liquid-based solvent and membrane provides a promising option for CO2 absorption. CO2 solvent that placed at the permeate side can immediately absorb the permeated CO2; once the CO2 molecule has diffused across the membrane, it leads to fast CO2 removal rates. Recently, many institutions and companies have performed pilot plant tests and reached a CO2 absorption capacity up to 80 t CO2 per day.71–73 URCASOL™ (DOW and ALSTOM), IHI, and RS2™ solvents exhibit significantly lower energy penalty for regeneration than that by MEA.74
Lately, demonstration scale tests have been performed to provide references for further commercial implementation. Among several technology suppliers for PCC, Shell Cansolv™ and MHI are two main technology suppliers for full-scale amine-based CO2 capture. The liquid-amine based CO2 capture approach has been implemented at the commercial demonstration scale in Boundary Dam project and Petra Nova project. The Boundary Dam project was designed to capture CO2 from a 160 MW coal-fired power plant since 2014 in Saskatchewan, Canada. The Petra Nova project was built to treat the flue gas from a 240 MW coal-fired power plant (1.4 million tons of CO2 per year) since 2017 in Texas, USA.17 These two projects produce CO2 at the semi-commercial scale and promote the further full-scale commercialization of liquid-based CCS technology. Unlike the conventional CO2 capture process using monophasic amine absorbent, few absorption processes have been built for newly developed biphasic solvents. Taking the demixing solvent process (DMX™, IFP Energies Nouvelles) as an example, a demixing unit, namely, a decanter, is installed prior to the stripper. The CO2-rich phase and the CO2-lean phase are demixed in this unit and the CO2-rich phase is sent for thermal regeneration.75 Further investigation on pilot- and demonstration-scale tests is crucial to validate the techno-economic feasibility of biphasic solvents and other novel solvents.
Overall, liquid-based CCS technology is designed to capture CO2 from fossil fuel-related plants. The demand for commercial-scale CCS technology is increasing according to the reduction targets of CO2 emissions. CCS technologies are expected to mitigate 30% of the total CO2 emission reduction by 2050. Despite the feasibility of the CCS technology, which has been industrially validated, its further commercialization is still limited due to technical and economic problems. The major obstacle is the large energy consumption associated with CO2 capture. For instance, when the state-of-the-art amine unit is installed, the thermal efficiency of the power plant would be reduced by 18–30% and the electricity prices would be increased by 45–70%,76,77 which poses a vital challenge for the advancement of CCS technology.16 Therefore, liquid-based CO2 capture technology still needs substantial improvements in solvent development and process design to decrease the energy cost and reach a commercially affordable price. Moreover, considering the excessive cost for CCS at the current stage, its full deployment cannot be achieved without suitable policy actions, such as carbon price, adequate emissions trade system, global carbon pricing tax, performance standards, and CO2 storage regulation.74

2.2. Low-temperature solid CO2 adsorbents

2.2.1 Solid amine-based adsorbents. 
Solid amine-based adsorbents are excellent CO2 capture compounds with high capacities at low CO2 partial pressure (10–15%), low regeneration temperature (<100 °C), and less corrosion of the equipment.78 CO2 molecules interact with amino groups and form carbamic acid, carbamate, and bicarbonate.79 The nature of the adsorbed species varies in terms of amine loading, amine type, amine sites’ density, the amine molecular size immobilized on the solid support, and, more importantly, the CO2 partial pressure.80 Given that the adsorptive properties of amine-enriched adsorbents are mainly based on chemisorption, the poor thermal regeneration ability remains a major drawback. CO2 retention is expected to occur with the contribution of adsorbed moisture molecules. Since then, many groups have fabricated amine-based solid adsorbents by supporting amines on porous materials including silica,81,82 carbon,83 MOFs,84,85 zeolites,86 clays,87,88 graphite/graphene89,90 and polymers91 so as to improve the CO2 adsorption capacity (Fig. 9). However, considering the complex synthesis and overall performance, its industrial widespread application is still far from fulfilment.
 Fig. 9  Promising substitutes for amine-based adsorbents associated with lower energy consumption for regeneration, including zeolites (Reproduced from ref. 81 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018), MOF (Reproduced from ref. 84 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018), silica (Reproduced from ref. 78 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019), polymers (Reproduced from ref. 91 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019), graphite/graphene (Reproduced from ref. 89 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2018), and clays (Reproduced from ref. 87 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). 
2.2.1.1 Effect of impurities. 
Amine-based adsorbents are vulnerable to deterioration through a variety of chemical pathways, such as irreversible SO2 adsorption, oxidative degradation of amines, steam-induced degradation of porous supports, and CO2-rich atmospheric urea formation. The amines undergo rapid oxidative degradation in the presence of O2 (3–4%), which is the greatest obstacle in industrial application. In 2018, Min and coworkers synthesized an oxidation-stable modified PEI/silica that displays extraordinarily superior oxidative stability with a minor loss of 8.5% in CO2 uptake after one month of aging in the flue gas containing oxygen at 110 °C (Fig. 10a).92 Despite the fairly low SO2 concentration (500–2000 ppm) in the flue gas, it is much more acidic than CO2 and can still cause irreversible poisoning effect and the gradual deactivation of the basic sites contained in the amine-based adsorbents. Consequently, the development of highly SO2-resistant amine-based adsorbents can substantially reduce the desulfurization cost. Primary and secondary amines generate thermally stable salts through interaction with SO2 (stable up to 200 °C), which cannot be decomposed back to free amines at the typical regeneration temperatures. In contrast, tertiary amines react with SO2 to generate a moderately stable adduct, which can be regenerated below 130 °C. However, due to the shortage of free protons at the amine sites, tertiary amines fail to form carbamate species and are therefore not favorable for CO2 adsorption. Recently, Kim's group synthesized an amine-based CO2 adsorbent with high SO2-resistance, exhibiting only a slight CO2 uptake decay (8.52%) with 50 ppm SO2 over 1000 CO2 adsorption/desorption cycles, compared with conventional PEI/SiO2 (65.1%), as depicted in Fig. 10b. A surface protection layer enriched with tertiary amines is introduced to primarily and reversibly capture SO2 in the flue gas, whereas desulfurized CO2 is freely trapped on primary and secondary amines beneath the layer.93
 Fig. 10  (a) Synthesis of oxidation-stable CO2 adsorbent. PEI was functionalized with EB, which generates tethered 2-hydroxybutyl groups,92 (b) SO2-resistant amine-containing CO2 adsorbent with a surface protection layer (Reproduced from ref. 93 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). 
2.2.1.2 Summary and outlook of solid amine-based adsorbents. 
For commercial implementation, the CO2 capture capacity should be stable over a long-period of repeated adsorption/desorption cycles. Generally, lower regenerability requires continuous replenishment with fresh adsorbents, causing a significant increase in the material cost towards CO2 capture. Recently, Ouyang and coworkers prepared low-cost millimeter-sized TEPA functionalized palygorskite spheres for industrial-scale CO2 capture with high capture capacity as well as excellent reusability characteristics. The uniform particle size, good sphericity, and excellent mechanical properties made the spheres highly favorable for the direct utilization and easy regeneration in large-scale industrial applications.88 Amine-based adsorbents have been proved to be viable for industrialization to capture CO2 in the post-combustion process, albeit with serious shortcomings including high regeneration energy consumption and equipment corrosion. Besides, the repeatability of CO2 capture performance for these adsorbents is somehow inferior and inaccurate as the amino groups generated between the organic amines and the support with a mesoporous structure are difficult to manipulate. In addition, organic amines with poor thermal stability are vulnerable to deactivation and decomposition under a high temperature environment, which restricts the operational conditions of amine-based adsorbents. The SOx and NOx impurities that co-exist in the flue gas harm the regenerability of the adsorbents; therefore, the units operated for such adsorbents should be set behind the desulfurization and denitrification processes in industrial applications.


2.2.2 Carbon-based adsorbents. 
Carbon-based CO2 adsorbents are a kind of promising candidates for CO2 adsorption due to their high specific surface area, low price, excellent chemical stability, hydrophobicity, controllable textural properties, and low energy requirements for regeneration. However, as mentioned in previous papers,94 CO2 adsorption on carbon adsorbents is “physical” and weak, making them sensitive to the reaction temperature with low CO2 selectivity. In the temperature range of the power plant flue gas (50–120 °C), the CO2 adsorption capacity drops dramatically. Therefore, to solve this problem, the adsorbate/adsorbent interaction as well as CO2 selectivity must be improved. Currently three main methodologies, including (1) tuning the textural properties of carbon, (2) chemical modification of carbon surfaces, and (3) preparation of carbon-based hybrid composites, are being adopted to improve carbon-based CO2 capture performance (Fig. 11).
 Fig. 11  Different methods to improve the performance of carbon-based CO2 adsorbents95–100 (Reproduced from ref. 95 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2012; reproduced from ref. 96 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017; reproduced from ref. 98 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017; reproduced from ref. 99 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019; reproduced from ref. 100 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2013). 
2.2.2.1 Tuning the textural properties of carbon. 
As reported, the pore structure and SSA greatly influence the CO2 adsorption performance of carbon-based materials.101 In particular, porous carbons with high porosity have been considered as ideal candidates for CO2 adsorption due to their fast kinetics, good reversibility, and high recyclability, which is attributed to the presence of abundant pores generated during carbonization using activating agents. Several approaches have been adopted to increase the SSA and to regulate the pore structure, such as using different precursors and chemical modifications (KOH, NaOH, ZnCl2, H3PO4, H2O2, and K2CO3) to form porous carbons. Typically, KOH is one of the most commonly used activation reagents for obtaining porous carbons. High SSA and well-developed microporous structures of carbon adsorbents can be obtained by KOH, which could give rise to a high SSA of ∼2500–3000 m2 g−1 and a desired pore size distribution. Besides, the CO2 uptake of KOH-activated porous carbon is mostly higher than 4 mmol g−1 at 25 °C. Sevilla et al.102 fabricated a sustainable porous carbon material from hydrothermally-treated polysaccharides and biomass activated by KOH at different temperatures. The narrow micropores play an essential role for achieving the good CO2 capture performance of 4.8 mmol g−1 at 25 °C.
However, activation with KOH suffers from two main problems: one is that KOH requires a high temperature (≥600 °C) during the activation process, which is not energy efficient. Besides, KOH is highly corrosive and the corrosivity increases significantly with elevated activation temperatures, which greatly restricts the practical applications. Therefore, Sevilla et al.103 put forward a chemical activation method by using the less corrosive and toxic potassium oxalate. The direct activation of glucose with potassium oxalate showed excellent CO2 uptake in the range of 4.2–4.5 mmol g−1. When combined with melamine-mediated activation together, the micro-/mesoporous structure carbons could be obtained and at a relatively high pressure, the CO2 uptake could reach 32–33 and 44–49 mmol g−1 at 30 bar and 50 bar, respectively. Potassium oxalate as an activation agent can also achieve good CO2 capture performance. Moreover, the narrow micropores are beneficial for CO2 adsorption at pressures <1 bar and micropores/mesopores are favorable for CO2 adsorption at high pressures (Fig. 12a). The narrow micropore (<1 nm) presented an important factor in the high CO2 adsorption capacity of the carbon materials at the ambient conditions. Qi et al.104 studied the underlying mechanism of carbon capture capacity and CO2 selectivity by first-principles simulation, in which the adsorbed carbon dioxide poses a stronger thermal effect in all the micropores, resulting in higher CO2 selectivity. As the pore size decreases, the pores become more and more sensitive to the distinction of CO2, indicating that CO2 adsorption depends on ultra-micropores to a large extent (Fig. 12b).
 Fig. 12  (a) Chemical activation method for preparing sustainable porous carbon (Reproduced from ref. 103 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018) and (b) relationship between adsorption heat and pore diameter calculated by first-principles (Reproduced from ref. 104 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). 
2.2.2.2 Chemical modification of carbon surfaces. 
Though the optimized porous carbons have the advantages of good reversibility and high recyclability, obtaining good CO2 adsorption and sufficient selectivity of CO2/N2 still remains a challenge. Especially for practical applications, when separating CO2 from other gas, the selectivity is even more important than the CO2 adsorption capacity. Pure carbons have a weak affinity towards CO2 due to their low polarity; thus, CO2 adsorption as well as CO2/N2 selectivity need to be improved. For instance, commercial activated carbon Norit R1 presents a very high SSA but a low IAST CO2/N2 selectivity of <5.105 However, both CO2 and N2 are linear symmetric molecules; the quadrupole moment of CO2 is slightly higher than that of N2 due to the polar CO bonds (13.4 × 10−40 cm2vs. 4.7 × 10−40 cm2). Therefore, the chemical modification of carbon surfaces, such as introducing heteroatoms (nitrogen, sulfur element, etc.) into the carbon framework or functional groups on the surface is usually adopted to increase the polarity of CO2, thus resulting in a high CO2 uptake over N2.106
Both N- and S-doping can increase the adsorbate/adsorbent electrostatic interactions as well as the heat of adsorption, which are beneficial for CO2 adsorption.107 The high CO2 uptake and selectivity of N-doped porous carbon108 were closely related to the high pore volume and nitrogen content, indicating that a suitable pore size and distribution as well as nitrogen on carbon play important roles in the selectivity performance.109 In addition, S-doping can also increase the interaction between CO2 molecules and carbonaceous materials, leading to a higher CO2 capacity. For example, Li and co-workers96 demonstrated that S doping can significantly improve the CO2 adsorption by GSP and CO2 capacity of 51 mmol mol−1 was obtained for this material, which increased by 39.85% compared to the counterpart without S-doping at 27 °C and 1 bar. The selectivity of CO2/N2 increased with increasing S content. The adsorption energy between the S-GSP surface and the CO2 molecules can be improved by S-doping, thus enhancing CO2 adsorption performance by the S-GSP material (Fig. 13).
 Fig. 13  Electron-density distributions of CO2 on the surface of graphene (a) without S-doping and (b) with S-doping. (c) Adsorption energy per CO2versus the number of CO2 molecules; (d) CO2/N2 selectivity versus the adsorption pressure at 27 °C; optimized structures for (e) 1CO2, (f) 12CO2, (g) 1CO2, and (h) 12CO2 on the surface of graphene with or without S-doping (Reproduced from ref. 96 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017). 
In addition to the doping of heteroatoms (mainly nitrogen and sulfur elements) in the carbon framework, the interaction between CO2 and the adsorbent can also be enhanced through the incorporation of amine groups into the porous carbons. Amine-functionalities can be dispersed inside the carbon pores, thus enhancing the CO2 capture performance. For example, Liu et al.110 grafted PEI onto HG and this new type of HG–PEI material exhibited a CO2 uptake of 4.13 mmol g−1 (25 °C, 1 atm), together with a good cycling stability at both low (100 °C) and high (135 °C) desorption temperatures. Except for the impregnation method, grafting with amino groups is also used for surface modification. Compared with the impregnation method, the advantages of grafted functional groups are stability and no desorption during the regeneration process. Amino incorporation in MCMs and MCNs by impregnation with PEI and grafting with EDA (Fig. 14) was carried out by Chai et al.111 The MCNs modified with PEI were preferred for CO2 capture, with a CO2 uptake of 1.97 mmol g−1 at 75 °C as well as high cyclic stability. Generally, carbon-based materials show natural CO2 affinities via physical adsorption; the addition of amino species leads to a reduction in the SSA, pore volume, and active sites of carbon, causing a loss in the CO2 adsorption capacity at low temperatures. However, the introduction of amines alters the adsorption mechanism from physical adsorption to chemisorption or combined physical and chemisorption mechanism, leading to enhanced CO2 uptake in moisture conditions or at higher temperatures, along with enhanced CO2 selectivity.
 Fig. 14  Two different methods for the amino functionalization of mesoporous carbons (Reproduced from ref. 111 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016). 
2.2.2.3 Preparation of carbon-based hybrid composites. 
Apart from tuning the textural properties or surface chemical modification of carbon, the third method for improving the CO2 uptake involves the preparation of carbon-based hybrid composites, such as carbon–MOF and carbon–carbon. For instance, HCM combined with Cu3(BTC)2 was prepared by Qian and co-workers (Fig. 15),95 exhibiting high CO2 uptake and selectivity for the CO2 molecule as well as good regeneration ability under mild conditions. Despite the excellent CO2 adsorption performance and relatively good CO2/N2 selectivity, there are still some problems to be addressed to allow the commercial application of carbon-based adsorbents, including the long-term stability to other components in the flue gas (NOx and SOx), low regeneration energy, and high reusability. The thermal stability and adsorption scale also need to be further investigated. In addition, other techniques such as DAC should also be developed to capture CO2 from a low concentration medium so as to reduce CO2 emissions.
 Fig. 15  (a) Synthetic process, (b) breakthrough curve, and (c) cycle performances of HCM–Cu3(BTC)2-x materials (Reproduced from ref. 95 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2012). 


2.2.3 Zeolite-based adsorbents. 
Among porous materials for CO2 separation, zeolites have traditionally shown promising results and have been widely used in PSA processes (<200 °C)112 due to their good availability, low cost, high specific surface area, high CO2 uptake, fast kinetics, and good chemical and thermal stability. Zeolites possess better capture capacity and higher CO2/N2 selectivity at low CO2 partial pressure (15% CO2, 85% N2) in the flue gas in contrast to activated carbons.112 The underlying mechanism contains strongly-bound carbonates via bi-coordination or CO2 physisorption via ion–dipole interaction. The CO2 selectivity towards other gases (CH4, H2O, N2, etc.) is still limited and their adsorption performances are vulnerable to the operational temperature.113 Generally, changing the framework structure and composition is proved to be an efficient methodology to improve the CO2 capture performance of such adsorbents.114
2.2.3.1 Cation exchange modifications. 
To improve the CO2/N2 selectivity in flue gases, a series of modifications based on cation exchange to effectively fabricate new zeolites has been reported. The CO2 adsorption characteristics and pore volume of zeolites can be profoundly influenced by the type of extra-framework cation. The intimate Coulombic interactions between extra-framework cations and CO2 give rise to selective capture and strong binding. The CO2 adsorption performance of zeolites highly depends on the electropositive character, cation concentration, and ionic radii.115 In 2018, Chen and co-workers employed microscopic-level molecular simulation to investigate different cation exchanges in 13X zeolite for high separation performance. The CO2 uptake and average CO2/N2 selectivity of the developed polymetallic cation-exchanged LiPdAgX zeolite were increased by 25.2% and 179.3% than those of 13X, respectively.115
2.2.3.2 Impregnation with amines. 
In addition, researchers have begun to modify the zeolites by either grafting or impregnating different amines to further enhance the CO2 uptake, which is closely correlated with the surface amine density, amine ligand basicity, and porous silica matrix dimensionality. For instance, Zeleňák and co-workers modified SBA-15 with three amine ligands (AP, DA, and TA), with mono-, di-, and tri-amine atoms, respectively. At low CO2 pressures, the adsorptive capacity highly depends on the amine center numbers and the surface density of the ligand. Driven by physisorption at higher pressures, the adsorptive capacity largely depends on the pore volume of SBA-15 and the entropic factor.116 Mohamedali and coworkers prepared ZIF-8 zeolites with the impregnation of ionic liquid, which exhibited substantially better CO2/N2 selectivity and higher CO2 uptake at 30–50 °C than that of conventional ZIF-8.117
2.2.3.3 Preparation of zeolite-based hybrid materials. 
Preparing zeolite-based hybrid materials to appropriately tune the final properties is another research direction in this field. For instance, Song's group successfully deposited an ultrathin microporous TiO2 layer on the 5A zeolite surface using the MLD technique to succeed in fine-tuning of the pore entrance towards the kinetic diameter between the N2 and CO2 molecules. The modified zeolite composite exhibited superior CO2/N2 selectivity (∼70) and a greatly improved CO2 capture capacity of 1.62 mmol g−1.118 Organic–inorganic composites have become a hot interest for various gas separation processes due to their beneficial synergistic effect. Recently, Minelli and coworkers developed novel geopolymer–zeolite composite monoliths, which exhibited a high CO2 adsorption capability of 1.1 mmol g−1.119
3D-printing technique has emerged as a promising approach for precisely and rapidly manufacturing materials with desirable configurations. Thakkar and coworkers fabricated novel zeolite-incorporated Torlon polymer composite monoliths via 3D printing technique for the first time, with outstanding mechanical strength, favorable textural properties, rapid phase separation performance, and superior CO2 uptake (1.83 mmol g−1).120 Furthermore, Wang's group proposed a versatile scheme to fabricate mechanically vigorous binder-free zeolite monoliths (Fig. 16). The material with hierarchical structures exhibits outstanding mechanical properties and a high equilibrium CO2 capture capability of 5.58 mmol g−1.121
 Fig. 16  Schematic diagram of the fabrication procedure of 3D-printed mechanically robust binder-free zeolite monoliths.121 
2.2.3.4 Summary and outlook of zeolite-based adsorbents. 
Overall, the research hot topics in zeolite-based CO2 materials are briefly summarized in Fig. 17. Fine-tuning the interior pores of zeolites with enhanced adsorption characteristics has emerged as an efficient method to improve their gas separation properties. The extra framework cation type exhibits a profound influence on the CO2 capture capacity and the pore volume of zeolites. The functionalization of amine sites with electron-withdrawing or bulky groups leads to very low adsorption heats. The methodology of pore confinement makes the fabrication of scalable and efficient zeolite adsorbents possible so that they can be applied in size-selective separations. The trade-off between the adsorption capacity, diffusion kinetics, and mechanical stability of zeolites remains a great challenge in their industrial applications. Furthermore, the existence of moisture greatly influences the CO2 capture performance of zeolites-based materials as they are not useful at higher operation temperatures with low CO2 partial pressure due to the physical adsorption mechanism.
 Fig. 17  A brief summary of the research activities on zeolite-based CO2 adsorbents12,115–119,122 (Reproduced from ref. 117 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 118 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 122 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019; reproduced from ref. 116 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 119 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 


2.2.4 MOF-based adsorbents. 
Metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) are a novel category of crystalline nano-porous materials,123,124 which are composed of transition metal ions connected by bridging organic ligands to generate porous structures, with various physical and chemical properties, such as ultrahigh specific surface areas and large porosities. The wide-ranging alternative possibilities of organic linkers and transition metals lead to the development of various MOFs with tunable adsorption sites so as to enable selective and energy-efficient CO2 capture. MOFs have particularly attracted attention due to their typically high pore volume and specific surface area, which offer abundant void space and numerous adsorption sites to host CO2. The exceptional tunability of MOFs provides chemically and structurally tailorable nanopores as they possess optimal adsorption sites with careful positioning towards selective CO2 physisorption (<200 °C).125 CO2 adsorption is expected to be critically influenced by both the topology and chemistry of the MOF pores. To enhance the CO2 selectivity and affinity, Anderson and coworkers investigated the role of a variety of topological and pore chemical features of MOFs for the enhancement of CO2 capture.
