The CO2 capture capacity of the parent MOFs is often improved via functionalization with amino, nitro, hydroxyl, cyano-, or thiol-related chemistries but the efficacy depends strongly on the pore topology-related descriptors.125,126
Generally, MOFs beat zeolites in terms of the working capacities, energy consumptions, and efficiencies.113 Their performance can be further improved by post-synthetic modification,127 functionalization of organic linkers,128–130 and doping nanomaterials117,131 into their frameworks.132,133 A stronger CO2 molecule interaction with MOF was achieved by post-synthetic rapid heat treatment, giving rise to drastically improved adsorption performance. The temperature-activated transport confirmed the lattice stiffening.134 The introduction of a tertiary amine linker yields a higher affinity for CO2, leading to increased overall CO2 adsorption capacity.129 Less polar or non-polar solvents have more benefits than more polar ones towards alkylamine incorporation into MOFs. Alkylamine molecules dispersed in less polar solvents possess higher chemical potential, driving more alkylamine molecules to be integrated into the framework so as to form modified MOFs with drastically improved CO2 capture properties.85 The immobilization of unsaturated alkali metal sites in the MOFs via a tetrazole-based motif can greatly boost the gas affinity.135
2.2.4.1 Integration with functional components. 
The rational synergetic integration of multiple functional components such as combining ionic liquid with Lewis base sites and MOF with Lewis acid sites into a bifunctional composite material polyILs@MIL-101 could lead to significantly improved activity towards CO2 capture. The significantly enhanced activity of polyILs@MIL-101 is ascribed to the synergy effect among the Lewis base sites in the polyILs, Lewis acid sites in the MOF, and the good CO2 enrichment capacity.136 MOFs offer various chemical environments, resulting in different and complex structure-adsorbate chemical interactions towards CO2 separation. A novel bi-porous MOF, SION-8, endowed with chemically distinct hydrophobic and hydrophilic structural pores, was obtained by judiciously altered activation conditions. The intrinsic structural flexibility, primarily along the b-axis, correlated with the structural adjustments, which allowed for the accommodation of gas molecules and guest solvent (Fig. 18).137 In particular, isoreticular synthesis via the fine-tuning of inorganic moieties remains a great challenge.138 Jiang and co-workers successfully adjusted the channels in NJU-Bai35, achieving significantly high CO2 uptake (7.2%) by upgrading the inorganic clusters’ symmetry in isoreticular MOFs as well as the CO2 selectivity.139
 Fig. 18  (a) A chain of alternating Ca–O atoms running along [100]. (b) Arrangement of the TBAPy4− ligands relative to the Ca–O chains. (c) A dihedral angle between the planes of the TBAPy4− ligands bound to two subsequent Ca atoms of 80.06°. (d) Tubular structural voids extending along the [100] direction, marked as gold surfaces, occupying 24.8% of the unit-cell volume (Reproduced from ref. 137 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). 
2.2.4.2 Intrinsic property regulation. 
The uniqueness of MOFs is that the intrinsic functionalities and properties could be finely regulated to address diverse applications. In particular, the co-existence of CO2 and H2S in natural gas and biogas causes severe corrosion problems for pipeline transportation and influences the quality standards in a variety of energy-related applications.140 The formation of impurities such as COS should be carefully avoided by selectively manipulating the concentration of CO2 and H2S. The high degree regulation of MOFs shows attractive potential in the selective removal of H2S and CO2 with different efficiencies. Through the integration of favorable sites for CO2 and H2S capture, Belmabkhout et al.141 reported the distinctive structural features offered by a fluorinated AlFFIVE-1-Ni, allowing the simultaneous removal of H2S and CO2. Interestingly, N-rich organic linkers are superior for the design of functional MOFs for CO2 and H2S separation due to their high affinity.142
2.2.4.3 Summary and outlook of MOF-based adsorbents. 
The thermal stability of MOF, particularly, their stability towards moisture, is a primary consideration for practical utilization.143 Developing MOFs with water-resistant features is a big challenge for practical CO2 capture. The hydroxo-functional groups are of paramount importance for enhancing the CO2 capture performance under humid conditions.144 The μ3-O groups interact strongly with the adsorbed H2O inside the pores due to the strong polarity of water molecules, which allows more CO2 to be efficiently trapped due to the confinement effect.144 González and co-workers reported an environment-friendly Mg(II)-based MOF, which exhibited high storage capabilities to reversibly adsorb H2S and CO2 with an unusual thermal stability (>500 °C).145
For the evaluation of the possibility of deploying MOFs towards CO2 separation in large-scale implementation, further pilot adsorption installations and investigations of functionalized compounds at the bench-scale are highly necessary. In 2018, Olajire published a systematic review on the synthetic chemistry of MOF for CO2 capture and conversion towards future sustainable energy development.132 Ghanbari and co-workers discussed the CO2 adsorption properties of MOFs, with an emphasis on the MOF structure design and adsorption mechanism in another review in 2020.146 The research interests on MOF-based CO2 adsorbents are briefly presented in Fig. 19. The introduction of Zn–OH groups with stronger nucleophilic properties could provide MOFs with enhanced CO2 uptake under low pressure. Functionalized mesoporous polymer supports play a critical role in the effective regulation of the crystal growth of MOFs on a localized surface, while the enhancement in the intimate interaction gives rise to synergistic properties. Unsaturated alkaline metal ions embedded into MOFs have proven to be another relatively straightforward method for boosting their CO2 affinity and the corresponding adsorption performance. The introduction of functional groups (tertiary amides or amines) to tune the inherent moisture adsorption properties of the MOFs could further improve the CO2 capture performance.129,147 Surface coating with polydimethylsiloxane dramatically enhances the moisture stability, which proves to be a facile methodology for stabilizing moisture-unstable MOFs with desirable CO2 capture capabilities under humid flue gas conditions.148 The use of MOF-based adsorbents also faces several challenges, for instance, low mechanical and thermal stabilities as well as poor moisture resistance under harsh conditions. Therefore, the development of functionalized MOFs with high mechanical and thermal stabilities is of vital importance, which needs further attention and in-depth studies.146 In addition, the cost of MOF-based adsorbents has to be significantly reduced to achieve further large-scale application.
 Fig. 19  A brief summary of the research activities on MOF-based CO2 adsorbents123,126,128,135,144,146,149 (Reproduced from ref. 149 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 126 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 144 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 123 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 146 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2020; reproduced from ref. 135 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2019). 


2.2.5 Alkali carbonate-based adsorbents. 
Alkali-metal carbonate-based adsorbents (mainly K2CO3 and Na2CO3) have been widely investigated for CO2 capture due to their high uptake, low cost, and low working temperatures (adsorption at 50–80 °C and desorption at <200 °C), which show great potential in application for fossil fuel power plants. Alkali metal carbonates can react with CO2 to form alkali metal hydrogen carbonate and the basic principle for CO2 adsorption is presented as follows: M2CO3(s) + H2O(g) + CO2(g) ⇔ 2MHCO3(s)(3)where M is potassium or sodium. The regeneration of Na2CO3/K2CO3 normally occurs at temperatures over 150 °C, which demands much energy during the process. Besides, there are some other problems including slow carbonation reaction rate and poor durability with the unmodified Na2CO3/K2CO3 adsorbents, thus restricting their industrial applications. Therefore, alkali-metal carbonates are always dispersed on stable and porous supports such as metal oxides (Al2O3, TiO2, SiO2, MgO), active carbon, and zeolites to improve their performance. Al2O3-supported Na2CO3 adsorbent showed good CO2 adsorption at relatively low temperatures. With the addition of MgO, both the CO2 adsorption capacity and the adsorption rate could be increased compared to that of the Na2CO3/Al2O3 adsorbent.
In terms of both the CO2 adsorption capacity and the adsorption kinetics, K2CO3 generally performs better than Na2CO3. The adsorption process of K2CO3 as a CO2 adsorbent is divided into three steps: (i) the formation of K2CO3·1.5H2O, (ii) the transformation into K4H2(CO3)3·1.5H2O, and (iii) the further slow conversion into KHCO3. In particular, K2CO3/Al2O3 with high porosity and high mechanical strength is an ideal adsorbent for CO2 capture. The pathway of the Na2CO3 carbonation process was proposed by Cai and co-workers (Fig. 20),150 which is controlled by diffusion limitation rather than the reaction kinetics on the surface. One of the possible rate-limiting reaction pathways related to the diffusion of the reactants is that the CO2 and H2O molecules diffuse through the NaHCO3 layer and react at the interface of NaHCO3 and Na2CO3. Another possible reaction pathway is due to the Na+ diffusion from Na2CO3 and H+ counter diffusion from NaHCO3 to their interface, thus promoting the reaction.
 Fig. 20  (a) Scheme of the Na2CO3 carbonation reaction with surface carbonation and diffusion-control and (b and c) two kinds of possible pathways for Na2CO3 carbonation (Reproduced from ref. 150 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). 


2.2.6 Implementation and challenges in post-combustion applications. 
Despite the consensus that CCS could make a great difference in achieving the 2 °C target proposed by IPCC, the implementation of commercial-scale CCS projects has made slow progress. In 2019, there were fifty-one large-scale CCS facilities globally, with nineteen at work, four under construction, and the remainder under various stages of development.151 According to the classification of the nine-point TRL system, among all CO2 capture technologies, only the chemical absorption using amine solutions technology has reached a commercial scale (TRL 9). Adsorption-based processes, which are discussed in this section and in Section 2.4.4, are still at the demonstration stage (TRL 7).152
Post-combustion processes separate CO2 from the flue gas released by fossil fuel combustion in power plants or industrial processes including coking, iron, and steel as well as cement and oil refining. Post-combustion CO2 capture operates at atmospheric pressures and low temperatures (<200 °C) with CO2 concentrations varying from 5% to 20%, which depend on the carbon sources. The impurities in the flue gases (NOx, SOx, solid particles) are removed before passing through the CO2 capture facilities. Since first being considered in the 1990s,153 adsorption processes for post-combustion CO2 capture have undergone rapid development as a substitute for the chemical absorption processes due to their avoidance of the hydrodynamics, volatility, and degradation problems associated with the use of chemisorption solvents. Research efforts towards post-combustion CO2 capture based on adsorption technologies have been directed at three aspects: (1) low-temperature adsorbents with larger CO2 working capacities, faster kinetics, higher selectivity, and better stability; (2) efficient cyclic processes with energy-saving regeneration methods; and (3) novel adsorbent structures and capture devices with low gas-solid mass transfer resistance.
VPSA or VSA is regarded as a relatively mature technology for post-combustion CO2 capture, which is based on physisorbents such as zeolites or activated carbon with pressurized feed gas.154–157 The regeneration pressure for a single VSA unit packed with zeolite 13X should be lower than 10 kPa to obtain a CP of 95%. Although the use of novel adsorbents, such as MOFs, to reduce the vacuum requirements is possible, these adsorbents are not currently available in large amounts. Therefore, VSA systems commonly adopt multi-stage configurations, allowing flexible adsorbent packing and process sequencing. Dry flue gas is commonly required in a VSA system to avoid the performance degradation of physisorbents, which causes additional capture cost. The energy consumption of the VSA process is between 1.5 and 3 MJe kg−1, which is equal to 4.5–9 MJth kg−1.
TSA is a more attractive option for the separation of CO2 from dilute gases. One obvious advantage of the TSA process based on chemisorbents is avoiding the requirement of pre-drying the flue gas, which accounts for 2–3 MJth kg−1 of additional energy consumption. In the TSA process, it is preferable to implement low-pressure steam for regeneration. This requires the adsorbents to be water-tolerant. A fluidized bed configuration is commonly adopted in TSA to enhance the heat transfer during heating and cooling.158–160
In early 2014, the Korea Institute of Energy Research installed the largest adsorbent-based post-combustion CO2 capture demonstration system, the 10 MWe-scale KIERDARY® plant, which is fed with 35000 N m3 h−1 flue gas from the Hadong coal-fired power plant and has the ability to capture 200 t CO2 d−1.158 The pilot plant used a two fluidized bed configuration, where the sorbent KEP-CO2P2 (K2CO3-based sorbent) captures and releases CO2 at 40–80 °C and 140–200 °C, respectively. The simulation studies indicated that the energy consumption of the plant was required to reach an 80% CCR and 95% CP is 4–5 MJth kg−1. Furthermore, the ADAsorb process, with a three-stage fluidized bed reactor, was used to develop a 1 MWe post-combustion CO2 capture installation for a coal-fired electricity generation plant in Alabama, USA.161 In addition to power generation sources, RTI International developed a fluidized bed-based TSA process to trap CO2 from the Brevik cement plant via a polyamine-based solid adsorbent at operating temperatures of about 60–70 °C and 110–120 °C.159 The estimated cost for RTI's TSA is between 38 and 46 € t−1.
The moving bed avoids the use of multiple reactors for the fixed bed system in a large-scale plant and is thus regarded as another candidate for TSA separation applications.162–164 The KCC system is a typical post-combustion CO2 capture pilot plant based on the moving bed reactor.162 The solid sorbent, a polyamine-impregnated mesocellular silica foam provided by the Research Institute of Innovative Technology for the Earth, flows into the top of the reactor and passes successively through the absorption, regeneration, and adsorbent dryer zones before being collected at the bottom using a circulating conveyer. The bench-test plant captures 5.5 t CO2 d−1 from the flue gas containing 13% CO2, and waste heat is used to generate steam (60 °C) for regeneration. The next plan for the Kawasaki system is to scale up this technology to 40 t CO2 d−1. In another moving bed process, SRI, Linde, and ATMI developed an advanced carbon sorbent technique based on carbon beads for CO2 capture from a 0.5 MWe slipstream of a coal-fired electricity generation plant.164 The estimated capture cost of SRI's ACS is $45 t−1 CO2. TDA Research, Inc., is also planning to build a 0.5 MWe-scale post-combustion CO2 capture demonstration system with a simulated moving bed process using alkalized alumina.163
One of the main challenges of the moving bed process arises from the low gas velocity that is required to avoid the fluidization of the adsorbents, which leads to intolerantly large equipment dimensions. The use of structured sorbents can solve this problem. An example is the VeloxoTherm™ process developed by Svante Inc., in Canada,165 which consists of a rotary adsorption unit divided by three gas paths for flue gas (adsorption), steam (regeneration), and air cooling. This technology uses structured silica gel functionalized with amine, which allows a fast adsorption–desorption process in 60 s with almost no pressure drop and high mass and heat transfer. It was reported that Svante launched a demonstration plant capturing 30 t CO2 d−1 from a natural gas steam boiler in Saskatchewan and will build a facility of the same size with funding by Chevron Technology Ventures.
Post-combustion CO2 capture technologies based on adsorbents offer alternatives to amine-based absorption processes but their further development is needed to reach higher TRLs. Table 1 summarizes the current status of adsorption-based post-combustion CO2 capture facilities. High-throughput simulation is required to evaluate the large number of adsorbent candidates to meet the multi-objective optimization of the adsorption performance (e.g., CO2 uptake, kinetics, selectivity, and stability) and the process performance (e.g., CCR, CP, energy consumption, abd productivity).166 When the TSA process is used in post-combustion CO2 capture, the adsorption bed should be designed carefully to achieve better heat integration as well as a good balance between the gas–solid mass transfer and the pressure drop. A fully comprehensive comparison between the adsorption and absorption processes pertaining to energy consumption and operating cost is still lacking. In addition, more pilot-scale post-combustion CO2 capture facilities should be implemented to acquire engineering experience with adsorption technologies.
