2.3. Intermediate-temperature solid CO2 adsorbents
Pre-combustion technologies have received tremendous attention for industrial hydrogen production due to their small-scale capturing system, low energy consumption, and high adsorption efficiency. Fossil fuels transform into CO2 and H2 through combustion, resulting in high gas pressure and CO2 concentration, which is favorable for CO2 capture. Syngas (CO + H2O) is converted into a gas mixture containing CO2 and H2 through the water gas shift reactor at 200–400 °C. The introduction of in situ CO2 adsorption materials could effectively achieve the integration of WGS and the gas separation process in one reactor. Layered double hydroxide (LDH)-derived mixed metal oxides (LDOs) and magnesium oxide (MgO) have excellent affinity for acidic CO2 molecules in the intermediate temperature range of 200–400 °C and thus possess good CO2 capturing potential for the SEWGS process.

2.3.1 LDH-based adsorbents. 
Layered double hydroxides (LDHs) with precisely controlled chemical composition and tunable properties have been well studied in many fields (Fig. 21a).167 Though LDHs have been deployed as adsorbents for decades, their first application in CO2 capture appeared in 2001.168 The LDOs capture CO2 at intermediate-temperature (200–450 °C), and thus can be applied in the SEWGS reaction. The research of LDH on CO2 capture started with its tunable chemical composition,169 followed by the synthetic method, doping with alkali metals, LDH-based composite, and the influence of the other gases as well as the adsorption mechanism.
 Fig. 21  (a) Schematic structure of the LDHs,167 (b) CO2 capture performance of different Mg3X–CO3 (X = Al, Fe, Ga, and Mn) LDHs as a function of the calcination temperature (Reproduced from ref. 170 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2010), (c) the diagram of the structural change in the Mg3Al–stearate LDH and Mg3Al–CO3 LDH after calcination at 400 °C, (d) the CO2 uptake of Mg3Al–stearate LDH and Mg3Al–CO3 LDH.171 
2.3.1.1 The effect of chemical composition and fabrication method of LDHs. 
The CO2 capture performance of LDOs has been found to be highly dependent on the divalent and trivalent cations within the brucite-like layers and the interlayer anions. The influence of divalent cations including Mg2+, Co2+, Ca2+, and Cu2+ was studied by Wang et al.,169 where the Ca–Co–Al LDH system displayed the highest CO2 adsorption capacity (1.39 mmol g−1). Wang et al.170 revealed that the trivalent cations (Al3+, Fe3+, Mn3+, and Ga3+) can affect the dehydration and the dehydroxylation/decarbonation processes, and different LDHs (with different trivalent cations) possess different optimal calcination temperatures (Fig. 21b). Among them, MgAl LDH displayed the highest CO2 adsorption capacity and excellent thermal stability. Moreover, due to the good affinity of lithium-containing complex oxides with CO2, LiAl2 LDH, as a unique monovalent cation containing LDH for CO2 capture, was first reported by Huang et al.172 The basic Li–O and lattice O2− sites were formed after thermal treatment, which was beneficial for CO2 capture. In short, the trivalent cations in LDHs may influence the layer charge density, whereas the divalent cations in LDH can provide the basic sites and affect the optimal adsorption temperature.169,170,172
Different optimal divalent/trivalent ratios also depend on the type of interlayer anions. Therefore, the effect of interlay anions (CO32−, NO3−, Cl−, ClO4−, Fe(CN6)4−, SO42−, and HCO3−) in LDHs on the CO2 capture performance has also been investigated. Among them, Mg/Al–CO3 LDH exhibited the greatest advantages in the CO2 capture performance with the highest specific surface area.173 The anions in LDHs can affect its thermal stability, morphology, and SSA, thus affecting its CO2 capture ability. Based on the above results, Wang et al.171 first fabricated an organic anion stearate (C17H35COO−) intercalated LDH for CO2 capture. By replacing CO32− with long carbon-chained C17H35COO−, its CO2 uptake was significantly enhanced from 0.5 to 1.25 mmol g−1 (Fig. 21d). The structural changes in the Mg–Al–CO3 and Mg–Al–stearate LDHs are comparatively illustrated in Fig. 21c. Compared with CO32−, the decomposition of C17H35COO− could generate more surface basic sites. The obtained LDOs possess lower degree of crystallinity and more stable structures, which are beneficial for capturing CO2. Qin et al.174 further studied the influence of the carbon-chain length on the CO2 capture performance of organo-LDHs. The CO2 uptake increased significantly when the carbon chain increased from 10 to 16. Therefore, it was concluded that larger interlayer spacing, higher layer charge density, and greater number of basic sites are more favorable for capturing CO2 over LDOs.
Moreover, the synthetic method could result in LDHs with different morphologies including “stone-like”, “platelet-like”, and “sand rose” morphologies, consequently influencing the CO2 capture performance. Wang et al.175 employed the IEP theory to fabricate nano-sized spherical LDH for CO2 adsorption. The obtained LDHs showed mesoporous structures and large pores. The average particle size of the obtained LDHs was about 20 nm, which is favorable for the dispersion of alkali metals.
2.3.1.2 LDH-based composites. 
In recent years, LDH-based composites have become very promising as CO2 adsorbents. To enhance the adsorption ability and cycling stability of LDO adsorbents, different carbon materials (CNF, GO, and CNT) have been studied as supports for LDHs.176–178 In general, three synthetic methods are often employed, namely, direct co-precipitation, electrostatically driven self-assembly, and hydrothermal urea reaction. Garcia-Gallastegui et al.178,179 prepared LDH/MWCNT and LDH/GO composites by direct co-precipitation (Fig. 22a) and proved that the CO2 capture performance of the LDH were enhanced both in terms of the adsorption ability and cycling stability. The enhanced interactions between the gas and the adsorbent were attributed to the increased effective SSA and the disordered structure of the composites. By utilizing the electrostatically driven self-assembly process, Wang et al.180 prepared highly dispersed LDH/GO composites from two exfoliated LDH and GO solutions (Fig. 22b). The obtained LDH/GO composite with 6.54 wt% GO resulted in maximum CO2 uptake, which was two times higher than that of pure LDH without GO due to the dispersion of exfoliated LDH and the stabilization caused by GO. Qiao et al.181 fabricated multilayer LDH/MgCO3 hybrids on aluminum foil/mesh using the urea hydrolysis method (Fig. 22c). The aluminum foil/mesh, which functioned as a substrate and the sole source of aluminum, could effectively prevent the sintering and pasting problems. More importantly, the Al-supported LDH thin film can be further made into any shape for practical applications.
 Fig. 22  (a) The schematic structure of the LDH/GO composite synthesized by the direct co-precipitation method,179 (b) the photographs of the electrostatically driven self-assembly process of exfoliated LDH and GO (Reproduced from ref. 180 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2015), (c) the synthetic diagram of the LDH/MgCO3 hybrids.181 
2.3.1.3 Doping with alkali metals. 
Doping alkali metal carbonates on LDH is another effective method to promote its CO2 capture performance.182 Numerous studies have been focused on the use of K2CO3 as a promoter since K2CO3 impregnation can increase the amount of active sites on LDH, thus enhancing the adsorption capacity, thermal stability, and cycling stability of the parent LDH. The level of adsorption depends on the doping amount of K2CO3 on the LDH. 10–30 wt% of K2CO3 was proved to be the optimum loading amount, considering that the overload of K2CO3 may block the pores of LDH. Wang et al.173 found that organic solvents may result in better dispersion of K2CO3 than inorganic solvents during K impregnation, thus enhancing the promotion of CO2 capture capacity. Long carbon chains as anions can enlarge the interlayer distance for promoting more cation exchange on the external surface or the internal surface of each of its layers. Therefore, the K2CO3-promoted stearate-pillared LDH exhibited a superior CO2 uptake of 1.93 mmol g−1 at 300 °C, which is much higher than that of K2CO3-promoted carbonate intercalated LDHs (1.11 mmol g−1). Likewise, Meis et al.176 compared the CO2 adsorption performance of supported LDHs and unsupported LDH with K2CO3 doping and found that K2CO3 loaded on the supported LDH showed higher adsorption capacity than that of the unsupported LDH due to the existence of K+ on the surface of the supported LDH after thermal treatment, which caused a higher concentration of defects on the K2CO3-loaded supported LDHs (Fig. 23). The higher occurrence of low-coordinated sites (corners and edges) was considered to be of significant importance for CO2 capture. Therefore, K2CO3 was further used to improve the CO2 uptake of the LDH-based composite adsorbents by Wang and Zhu et al.177,180,183
 Fig. 23  The schematic representation of the addition of K2CO3 to supported/unsupported LDHs (the red ball represents oxygen, the light gray ball represents magnesium, and the dark gray ball represents potassium) (Reproduced from ref. 176 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2010). 
Commercially available LDHs commonly exhibit stone-like morphologies with highly stacked structures, which lead to low SSA for doping alkali metal ions. Though it is reported that LDH layers are possible to be exfoliated into nanosheets through “top down” or “bottom up” methods,184 they are easy to re-stack during the drying process due to their high surface charges and hydrophilic properties. Recently, O’Hare's group successfully synthesized a series of Zn2Al–borate LDH nanosheets with high SSA (459 m2 g−1) and large pore volumes (2.15 cm3 g−1) using a facile exfoliation method called AMOST.185 AMOST refers to the washing of the LDH wet cake with AMO solvents in the co-precipitation process; thus, the interlayer water is removed. The replaced AMO solvent molecules are then evaporated during the following drying process, which makes the LDH layers easy to slide. Zhu et al.186 reported flower-like Mg3Al–CO3 LDH after acetone washing, which leads to more uniform dispersion of the impregnated K+ ions, exhibiting increased CO2 uptake by about 22.9% at 400 °C compared to that of commercial K-MG70 (Fig. 24). A subsequent work screened six impregnation solvents and proved that ethanediol is more effective for dispersing the doped K2CO3 than water on the surface of the LDH prepared by the AMOST method.187
 Fig. 24  Scheme of AMOST and the characterization of K-promoted LDH before and after acetone washing (Reproduced from ref. 186 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). 
In addition to alkali metal carbonates, alkali metal nitrates have also been studied as promoters for LDH-based adsorbents. For instance, the CO2 uptake of LDH with a Mg/Al molar ratio of 20 can reach a relatively high value of 9.27 mmol g−1 with the existence of NaNO3 at 240 °C.182 Qin et al.174 verified that ternary alkali nitrates (Li–Na–K)NO3 can also enhance the CO2 capture performance of a carboxylic anion-intercalated LDH. Lara-García et al.188 investigated the mechanism of (Li–Na–K)NO3 promoted Mg–Al–palmitate LDH by in situ DRIFTS and confirmed that two reaction processes took place for nitrates for enhancing the CO2 adsorption ability. In the first process, CO2 combined with the melted NO3−, resulting in the generation of NO2− and CO32−. Then, the CO32− reacted with the calcined LDH material to form MgCO3. In the second process, O2− was released and reacted with nitrite, thus recovering the initial NO3− species.
2.3.1.4 Adsorption mechanism. 
LDH itself does not have alkaline sites, while its derived metal oxides after thermal treatment contain abundant alkaline sites. According to the CO2 adsorption mechanism over LDH-derived adsorbents, the Mg–O species are the active sites for CO2 capture.169,189 Gao et al.190 proposed two possible active Mg–O species formation mechanisms for LDH. The Mg–O species were achieved by replacing Mg2+ with Al3+ at the octahedral positions in the MgO lattice, resulting in Mg vacancies, or by the diffusion of Al3+ out of the octahedral brucite layers, leaving behind Al vacancies. Recently, Zhu et al.191 applied in situ techniques to illustrate the CO2 adsorption mechanism of K-promoted MgxAl–CO3 LDH with different Mg/Al ratios. They proved that there is a synergistic effect between the K+ ions and the Mg/Al ratio. After adsorbing CO2, bidentate carbonates are formed on the surface of the K-promoted LDH. However, when the Mg/Al ratio is less than two, unidentate carbonates with stronger binding force are also formed. In practice, the actual mechanism of CO2 adsorption and desorption on LDHs is complex.
2.3.1.5 Summary and outlook of LDH-based adsorbents. 
To the best of our knowledge, for intermediate-temperature CO2 capture, there are only two promising adsorbent candidates so far, namely, LDH and MgO. MgO has attracted a great interest in recent years due to the extraordinary progress achieved by doping molten salts. However, Zhu et al.192 found that K2CO3-promoted MgO showed lower CO2 uptake with trace CO2, whereas K2CO3-promoted LDH exhibited higher uptake with relatively low Mg/Al ratio under the same conditions. They proposed that the introduction of Al3+ to Mg2+ combined with K2CO3 may create more CO2 adsorption sites. Meanwhile, unidentate carbonates were formed in K2CO3-promoted LDH with the lowest Mg/Al ratio, making it possible to capture extremely low concentration CO2.
The service life of the adsorbents has significant economic implications on any commercial-scale operations. The introduction of carbon materials as supports can help to minimize the pulverization of LDH. However, there are few studies that have reported the uses of LDH-based composites for long-time cycling tests. Although doping alkali metals can enhance the CO2 capture capacity of LDH-derived CO2 adsorbents, the relatively low adsorption capacity and poor cycling stability are still a challenge. The adsorption mechanism and the alkali metal promotion mechanism for CO2 adsorption over LDH-derived adsorbents are still controversial, which need more in-depth investigations. It was suggested that the molecular models and force fields should be developed to investigate the adsorption mechanism of LDH-derived CO2 adsorbents based on the actual flue gas conditions. LDH-derived CO2 adsorbents have displayed significant advantages in the adsorption of low concentration CO2 but the intrinsic reason remains unclear. In addition, the influence of the impurities in the flue gas, including H2O, SO2, and H2S, on the CO2 uptake of LDH needs to be further investigated. Finally, parameters relevant to cost analysis, lifetime of the adsorbent, and kinetics under the industrial operating conditions should also be taken into account.


2.3.2 MgO-based adsorbents. 
Magnesium oxide (MgO), which is abundant on Earth, exhibits promising potential for CO2 capture due to its high theoretical adsorption capability, lack of toxicity, low cost, and relatively low regeneration temperature.193,194 It can capture CO2 in the temperature range of 200–400 °C and can be easily regenerated at a moderate temperature at 450 °C.195 However, the main obstacle in MgO adsorbents towards the practical application of CO2 capture lies in its low actual CO2 uptake and poor adsorption kinetics due to the intrinsically high lattice enthalpy and barren basic active sites.196 It is commonly accepted that MgO displays reduced uptake tendency during high-temperature exposure in CO2 capture, resulting from the decline in SSA due to sintering.193 The low-coordination Mg2+–O2− sites located at the corner/edge exhibit favorable interaction towards CO2, resulting in inherently limited adsorption performance. Recently, the “negative-sintering” hypothesis was challenged by Mutch and co-workers using both spectroscopic and computational methods, who first claimed necessary high-temperature conditions for facet-dependent surface activation to unleash the capability of the MgO polar (111) facet.197
2.3.2.1 Fabrication with mesoporous structure. 
Mesoporous materials as heterogeneous adsorbents, which possess narrow pore size distributions, provide a combination of a certain degree of shape- as well as size- selectivity and large SSAs. Therefore, Gao and co-workers fabricated mesoporous MgO adsorbents possessing abundant basic sites, which exhibited a superior CO2 capture capacity of 2 mmol g−1. During the interaction with CO2, a variety of carbonate surface species including monodentate and bidentate carbonates as well as bicarbonate were generated on the surface of MgO.193 Using small-sized MgO with more corner- and edge-located basic O2− capturing active sites in the O2−–Mg2+ bonds for acidic CO2 molecule could achieve higher capturing efficiency for MgO. Meanwhile, combination with a support can further enhance the regenerability of the MgO species, thus preventing its agglomeration over multiple cycles.198 Inspired by this, Li's group dispersed ultrafine MgO nanocrystallites with full exposure of the active sites on well-defined dual supports (amorphous C and rGO sheets as protectants). The novel hierarchical rGO@MgO/C nanocomposite system possessing unique physicochemical properties (large SSA, sheet-on-sheet architecture, and abundant nanopores) exhibits remarkable CO2 uptake (1.36 mmol g−1), fast adsorption kinetics, as well as superior process durability.199
2.3.2.2 Molten salts modification. 
Notably, Gao and co-workers published a review in 2017, which summarized the fabrication and property advances of molten salt-modified MgO-based CO2 adsorbents, which has emerged as a promising approach to enhance the capturing performance of MgO adsorbents.200,201 The CO2 uptake for pure MgO is far from satisfactory, even with the optimization by the above-mentioned promotion methods (<2 mmol g−1). In 2015, Harada and coworkers reported the remarkable effect of molten salts on the CO2 uptake of highly regenerable MgO (>10.2 mmol g−1) at ∼300 °C. The generation of a CO2-impermeable monodentate carbonate rigid layer on the MgO surface is effectively prevented by the molten salts and CO2 capture capability with a high rate is promoted by the rapid generation of carbonate ions (Fig. 25).202,203 Inspired by the promotion effect of the molten salts, Qiao and co-workers further improved the CO2 uptake of MgO to 16 mmol g−1 by optimizing the alkali metal ratio (Li/Na/K) of the nitrates.204 In 2018, Zhao's group fabricated NaNO3/NaNO2 mixture-promoted mesoporous MgO, which exhibited a superior CO2 uptake of 19.8 mmol g−1 at 350 °C.205 The significant improvement achieved by the introduction of molten salts for MgO-based adsorbents makes it a research hot topic for SEWGS. However, the detailed reaction mechanism for molten salt-promoted MgO is not yet fully understood.205,206
 Fig. 25  Schematic illustration of the possible reaction occurring in the molten alkali metal nitrate-coated MgO (Reproduced from ref. 202 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015). 
The durability of molten salt-promoted MgO adsorbents during multiple sorption-regeneration cycles has become a major concern for their real applications in SEWGS. Trivino and co-workers utilized commercially available LDHs as stabilizers to improve the cyclic stability of NaNO3-promoted MgO adsorbents. Restricting the formation of non-uniform and large product crystallites, the LDHs effectively act as templates for MgCO3 and regulate the basic sites for their high interaction with both NaNO3 and MgO (Fig. 26).198 In 2019, the same group went a step further to apply this novel MgO-based adsorbent to the SEWGS process for 30 cycles. The superior CO2 capture performance resulted in greatly increased H2 purity (99.4% in dry basis) at 300 °C, 12 atm in the 1st cycle, which remained stable over ten consecutive cycles, thus indicating its excellent regenerability (Fig. 27).207
 Fig. 26  Formation of smaller MgCO3 crystals, exposure of stabilized sites, and higher MgO-stablizer-NaNO3 interactions (Reproduced from ref. 198 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). 
 Fig. 27  Sorption-enhanced water gas shift reaction by in situ CO2 capture on an alkali metal salt-promoted MgO–CaCO3 adsorbent (Reproduced from ref. 207 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). 
2.3.2.3 Summary and outlook of MgO-based adsorbents. 
Overall, the research strategies for improving the CO2 adsorption properties of MgO-based adsorbents are briefly summarized in Fig. 28. A timely review covering deeper and broader advancements of MgO adsorbents has been provided by Hu and co-workers in 2019.196 However, the development of MgO adsorbent granulation techniques and the enhancement of granule performance are quite urgent for realistic large-scale applications with excellent mechanical and chemical properties.208 The ease of granulation operation and energy consumption of the manufacturing process should be taken into consideration for industrial implementation. Future tests need to be focused on the evaluation of the adsorption performance under realistic conditions and targeted apparatus to verify the techno-economic consumption of MgO-based CO2 adsorbents for implementation.
 Fig. 28  A brief summary of the research activities on MgO-based CO2 adsorbents (Reproduced from ref. 208 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019; reproduced from ref. 193 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 200 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017). 

2.4. High-temperature solid CO2 sorbents
Methane-reforming is a highly efficient, economical, and widely used process for industrial H2 production at high temperatures (600–900 °C). The sorption enhanced methane-reforming (SEMR) process requires high-temperature solid CO2 sorbents such as calcium oxide, alkali silicate, and alkali titanate, which can effectively capture CO2 in the relatively high temperature range of 600–900 °C. The deployment of such sorbents could successfully inhibit large heat consumption, simplify the whole process, and further improve the conversion efficiency of CH4 to form H2 with simultaneously captured CO2 of high purity.

2.4.1 CaO-based sorbents. 
2.4.1.1 Basic principle of CaO-based adsorbents. 
Calcium oxide (CaO)-based materials have been widely studied as high temperature CO2 sorbents since 1933.209 Owing to its high theoretical sorption capacity and availability, CaO has shown great potential applications in CO2 capture. With decades of research, more and more CaO-based CO2 sorbents with high-performance have been reported, driving this research forward from the foundation towards real applications. For practical applications, the sorption capacity is one of the dominating parameters for determining the cost and efficiency. However, the sorption capacity of CaO is poisoned by the CaCO3 layer formed outside the CaO sorbent. Since CaCO3 formation is inevitable, the capacity for CO2 sorption on CaO, which is highly dependent on the particle size, decreases rapidly due to the sintering of the sorbent particles over multiple cycles. A widely accepted explanation suggests that it is the low Tammann temperature of CaCO3 (533 °C) that leads to sintering.210 The powder sorbents need to be treated before implementation as the CaO powder in the reactor is prone to pressure drop and elutriation. In order to solve the above problems, several efforts have been devoted to the following areas over the last three years, including (1) CO2 uptake enhancement, (2) sintering-resistance improvement, (3) powder granulation, and (4) practical application exploration.
2.4.1.2 Enhancement of CO2 capture performance. 
To enhance the CO2 sorption capacity of CaO, a large amount of work has been carried out on particle size reduction, SSA increment, and surface modification. A variety of methods, such as hydration, acid treatment, precursor selection, physical grinding, and preparation process control, have been studied thoroughly in the early stages. On top of that, there has been a lot of original work in the last three years, which is especially focused on precursor modification and doping. Zhao et al.211 proposed a surfactant-free method to prepare morphology-controlled CaCO3 precursors by the reaction of Ca(NO3)2 with the CO2 storage material (CO2 bubbled ethylenediamine + ethylene glycol) and obtained nano CaO by roasting the CaCO3 precursors. The synthetic CaO particle size is about 480 nm × 255 nm, which facilitates a high sorption capacity (17.18 mmol g−1, 92% of the theoretical sorption capacity). Besides CaCO3, Ca(OH)2 is also commonly used as a CaO precursor.
Apart from the above-mentioned progress, doping with an addition agent is also an effective way to improve the sorption performance. Among all the additives, the most representative additives are metal oxides. Though metal oxides are often used to enhance the stability, they can be utilized to improve the sorption performance of CaO. In terms of energy, Liu et al.212 explored the effect of metal additives on the reactivity of CaO carbonation by DFT, indicating that the sorption energy on various promoted CaO surfaces followed the order Zr > Al > Mg > Na. The higher sorption energy demonstrated that the addition of this substance facilitates the carbonation of CaO. The CaO carbonation process involves CO2 chemisorption, surface reaction with O2− to form CO32−, and finally, the generation of CaCO3.
The diffusion of CO2 and mobility of O2− are key factors for CO2 capture. Based on these, Guo et al.213 reported a (Zr–Ce)-additive with facilitated CO2 capture of the CaO-based sorbent. Due to the formation of Ce2Zr2O7, oxygen vacancies were created to facilitate the diffusion of CO2 and O2− mobility. Similarly, Huang et al.214 developed a low melting point oxy-salt eutectic doped CaO. By loading CaO with alkali carbonate molten salt, its CO2 uptake could be significantly improved from 3.26 to 10.94 mmol g−1 at the first carbonation process (Fig. 29a), which can be ascribed to the high O2− concentration and O2− conductivity of the molten salt (Fig. 29b).215
 Fig. 29  (a) CO2 sorption/desorption cycling performance of CaO and molten salt-promoted CaO, (b) schematic diagrams of the reaction with CO2 for low melting point oxy-salt eutectic coated CaO particle214,215 (Reproduced from ref. 214 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2017). 
2.4.1.3 Improvement in sintering-resistance. 
Capacity loss is recognized as the most serious problem for CaO-based sorbents. In this regard, extensive work has been devoted to preventing the sintering effect and to improving the cyclic stability. One of the most feasible options is to incorporate CaO particles with inert materials. In earlier studies, supporting materials including Al2O3, MgO, Y2O3, CuO, CeO2, TiO2, KMnO4, SiO2, CexZryOz, La2O3, LaAlxMgyO3, and CaZrO3 have been thoroughly investigated and reviewed but the state-of-the-art works still need to be highlighted to reveal some new trends. Based on previous research, the study of CaO-based materials lays more emphasis on the performance and economy. Additives such as Al2O3, MgO, ZrO2, and their mixtures are preferred due to the high stability of their derivatives such as Ca12Al14O33, Ca9Al6O18, MgAl2O4, and CaZrO3. For stabilized CaO sorbents, the dispersion degree of inert supports is a determining factor for sintering resistance. Hence, the research emphasis aims to achieve a good dispersion of the supporting materials in the calcium precursor or CaO by adapting various synthetic methods, such as coprecipitation, sol–gel, hydrothermal, milling, spray/freeze drying, and flame spray pyrolysis. Different precursors prepared by the aforementioned methods allow to obtain stabilized CaO with specific morphology, structure, and mixing degree. Furthermore, Armutlulu et al.216 published an ALD strategy to prepare shell-comprising nanoparticles with Al2O3 film on the CaO nanoparticles. By wrapping hollow CaO in the thin Al2O3 layer (<3 nm), the sintering between the particles was effectively prevented (Fig. 30a). For CaO with 10 ALD cycles, CO2 uptake of 55 wt% was achieved after 30 cycles (80.5% capacity retention), which is higher than that of untreated synthesized CaO (40 wt% with 53.4% capacity retention); both the samples exhibited far more superior uptake compared to the performance of limestone (11 wt% with about 20.3% capacity retention). The high cyclic stability of Al2O3-stabilized CaO was attributed to its favorable morphological characteristics and the hollow spherical structure was extensively preserved during the cyclic operation (Fig. 30b). Calcium precursor was suggested to show an effect on the morphology and structure of CaO, especially the porous structure generated by the calcination of the organic precursor. Therefore, organic calcium has been selected as the CaO precursor in many mixing methods, for instance, calcium acetate. Huang et al.217 employed a facile solvent/non-solvent method to prepare MgO/CaO using calcium acetate as the precursor. The MgO/CaO composite was obtained at a nano-meter mixing level (Fig. 31), exhibiting a CO2 uptake of 59.2 wt% after 7 cycles (with 95.3% retention of its initial capacity).
 Fig. 30  (a) HR-TEM of ALD-grown Al2O3 coating CaO nanoparticles with EDX mapping; (b) SEM of Al2O3-stabilized CaO sorbents before and after carbonation over 30 cycles (Reproduced from ref. 216 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2017). 
 Fig. 31  SEM images of CaO and MgO/CaO prepared by solvent/non-solvent before and after 10 cycles (a) CaO, (b) MgO/CaO, (c) CaO after 10 cycles, and (d) MgO/CaO after 10 cycles, and (e) the schematic illustration of the possible mechanism of stability enhancement (Reproduced from ref. 217 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2018). 
2.4.1.4 CaO derived from waste resources. 
Though the synthetic stabilized CaO shows higher CO2 uptake and better stability, the high cost still inhibits its further practical application. From economic perspectives, the deployment of solid waste or minerals as calcium precursors or inert additives is being more widely applied. Common cheap calcium sources are limestone, dolomite, eggshell, seashell, and metallurgical slag. The materials used as additives such as bauxite tailings, sepiolite, fruit bunch, ash, and cement generally contain Al, Si, and Mg. The use of these cheap raw materials/scrap can not only reduce the production cost of the sorbent but also render the management of solid waste with great practical value. Unlike the direct synthesis of stabilized CaO with chemical reagents, the extraction of the calcium source and dopant is required before the sorbent preparation. Generally, the calcium sources and dopant precursors are mechanically crushed, dried, and then mixed.
2.4.1.5 Powder granulation. 
The above studies are mainly focused on the preparation and characterization of CaO sorbents in the powder form. For implementation, powders cannot be applied directly due to the elutriation from the carbonator and the regenerator. To fix the CaO sorbents, granulation and coating methods are executed to prepare molding materials. However, low CaO loading greatly reduces the sorption capacity. Another strategy is to prepare molding CaO by granulation, for example, extrusion-spheronization, to make CaO into pellets by mechanical forces. However, this methodology inevitably causes the destruction of the porous microstructures of the powder materials.218 To improve the CO2 uptake of CaO-based pellets, pore-forming templates were widely used to enhance the porosity, such as cellulose,219 hemicellulose,220 plastic wastes,218 lignin,220 and urea.221 Among these, the most widely used template is cellulose. Li et al.219 modified CaO pellets with 30% microcrystalline cellulose and found that the CO2 uptake (34 wt%) was increased by 48% compared to that of the unmodified pellets after 20 cycles. They attributed this enhancement to the gas channels and cavity formation caused by gas release and the disappearance of microcrystalline cellulose during the burning of the porosity promoter.
With the gradual improvement of CaO properties and reduction in the production costs by the deployment of cheap precursors, more studies have been focused on the application of CO2 adsorption by CaO, viz., CaL. CaL was initially studied in the power sector as an alternative to amine scrubbing for post-combustion capture to reduce the costs or was used for pre-combustion in IGCC, and had already been tested in bench- and pilot-scale plants. A comprehensive review paper on pilot-plant testing of calcium looping for CO2 capture from power generation systems has been published by Hanak et al.209 In addition, due to the world's increasing demand for clean energy hydrogen, more CaO is being utilized for sorption-enhanced hydrogen production, such as methane reforming glycerol reforming,222 ethanol reforming, tar reforming, and biomass pyrolysis-gasification. These hydrogen-producing reactions usually involve two processes, SR and WGS, which are generally reversible. By extracting CO2 from the mixture, the equilibrium is shifted to the product side (Le Chatelier's principle), resulting in higher H2 yield. Furthermore, CaO carbonation is an exothermic reaction; thus, the incorporation of the CO2 capture reaction is favorable for the endothermic SR reaction. Moreover, it even turns the SEMR into an exothermic reaction. With these merits, bi-functional CaO-supported catalysts, for example Ni/CaO, were developed for sorption-enhanced hydrogen production. However, bulk CaO still suffers from sharp CO2 uptake reduction caused by sintering and the general solution is to add supports, as mentioned above. Wu223 and Arif224 also believed that the addition of CaO carrier and inert materials (alkaline earth oxides) inhibits the formation of coke, which remains the most serious problem for Ni-based catalysts as it causes a severe decay in the activity. Though noble metals such as Pt, Pd, and Rh have better catalytic activity and anti-coking properties,224 lower cost transition metals such as Ni with high catalytic activity are deemed to possess more research value. Charisiou et al.222 reported a Ni/CaO–MgO–Al2O3 (Ni/modAl) catalyst for combined catalytic glycerol steam reforming and in situ CO2 removal. The modAl material with reduced nickel species particle size of higher dispersion exhibited high resistance to coke formation and CaO sintering, leading to enhanced and efficient glycerol conversion and hydrogen production with high purity.
2.4.1.6 Summary and outlook of CaO-based adsorbents. 
To realize the industrial application of CaO-based sorbents, the activity, price, stability, form, and anti-crush strength need to be taken into full consideration, as shown in Fig. 32. Though the CO2 sorption capacity and cycling stability of synthetic CaO have made great progress, their implementation is still limited by the high cost and poor stability. The performance of CaO derived from cheap precursors is still far from satisfactory, and more importantly, the CO2 uptake of CaO suffers a serious decline after granulation. Hence, the implications of future research should be focused on the following directions: (1) the large-scale preparation technology of CaO with higher sorption capacity and stability derived from solid waste/mineral as well as catalyst-loading techniques for sorption-enhanced hydrogen production; (2) the technology of material regeneration and CO2 separation/purification; (3) direct utilization/conversion technique of CO2 fixed by CaO sorbents.
 Fig. 32  The current problems and solutions to realize the industrial application of CaO-based CO2 sorbents211,215,217,219 (Reproduced from ref. 217 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 211 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 219 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). 


2.4.2 Alkali silicate-based sorbents. 
Alkali silicates, mainly including lithium silicates and sodium silicate, can act as efficient CO2 sorbents. However, considering the technical requirements for practical applications (such as high sorption capacity, good regeneration performance, and fast sorption/desorption kinetics), not all the aforementioned sorbents are suitable. For instance, the sorption capacity of Na2SiO3 is typically less than 10 wt%. On the contrary, Li8SiO6 exhibits very high sorption capacity (about 50 wt%) and fast kinetics. Unfortunately, the poor regeneration performance hinders its application potential.225 In contrast to Na2SiO3 and Li8SiO6, Li4SiO4 not only has a high sorption capacity (36.5 wt%) but also possesses good regeneration ability.226 Therefore, as the representative of alkali silicate-based CO2 sorbents, Li4SiO4 will be highlighted in this section.86
2.4.2.1 Basic principle and current challenges of Li4SiO4. 
CO2 can react with Li4SiO4 at high temperatures (>400 °C) to produce Li2CO3 and Li2SiO3 (eqn (4)). The generally-accepted sorption theory is based on the double-shell model. As depicted in Fig. 33, the CO2 capture process includes two steps: (1) CO2 reacts with Li+ at the surface of Li4SiO4 and the products (Li2CO3 and Li2SiO3) can cover the Li4SiO4 surface to form a dense shell with a fast reaction rate; (2) CO2 and Li+ must penetrate this dense product shell in order to react with each other. However, the carbonate shell is not easy to diffuse; thus, the sorption rate in this step diminishes rapidly. As the product shell thickness increases, the reaction gradually stops for CO2 and Li+ cannot penetrate within this shell. The strategies that can improve the CO2 diffusion or Li+ and O2− ion transportation will therefore be extremely effective for CO2 sorption performance of Li4SiO4. Furthermore, the cost of the sorbents is also to be taken into account. Li4SiO4(s) + CO2(g) ↔ Li2CO3(s) + Li2SiO3(s)(4)
 Fig. 33  Double shell model for CO2 sorption over Li4SiO4 (Reproduced from ref. 227 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2016). 
Hereunder, we have aimed to provide an overview of the optimization methods and their corresponding principles. At the same time, the future application prospect of Li4SiO4 has been evaluated. We hope that this part can provide guidance for the development of alkali silicate-based sorbents. The specific strategies to improve the Li4SiO4 sorption performance are summarized in Fig. 34, including the selection of a proper silicon source, influence of the preparation method, and the addition of foreign materials. For practical applications, the prilling process for making pelletized Li4SiO4 is also of great importance.
 Fig. 34  Different strategies for improving the Li4SiO4 sorption performance226–231 (Reproduced from ref. 227 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2016; reproduced from ref. 226 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017; reproduced from ref. 228 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018; reproduced from ref. 229 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2015; reproduced from ref. 230 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2015; reproduced from ref. 231 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
2.4.2.2 Strategies for improving the performance of Li4SiO4. 
According to the aforementioned optimization strategies, there are mainly four ways to enhance the CO2 capture property of Li4SiO4: (1) proper selection of the raw material and preparation methods; (2) creation of more pores by the addition of pore forming agents, (3) surface modification of Li4SiO4 with molten salts, (4) using Si-containing minerals as precursors and utilizing the impurity phases to enhance the stability and to prevent sintering. For the traditional solid-state method, an ideal calcination temperature is typically about 950 °C. During the solid-state process, SiO2 with a high melting point (about 1650 °C) as a “core” material gradually reacts with lithium salts with lower melting points (usually below 800 °C). The reaction progresses from the outside layer of SiO2 to the inside. Hence, large SiO2 particles lead to an increase in the Li4SiO4 particle size. In addition, the large particle size of the raw materials will raise the synthesis temperature and induce a non-uniform reaction, which would undoubtedly facilitate the sintering of Li4SiO4 particles, thus raising the probability of by-product (Li2SiO3 or Li2O) formation. Therefore, an optimization of the raw silica source is crucial.
At present, Li4SiO4 sorbents can be synthesized from a large panel of silica sources, such as zeolites or fumed silica. Compared to commercial silica, these novel silica sources exhibit smaller particle sizes and high SSAs. As a consequence, the formed Li4SiO4 sorbents demonstrate outstanding CO2 capture performances. As shown in Fig. 35a, SBA-15–Li4SiO4226 achieved the highest sorption capacity of 36.3 wt%, which is very close to the maximum theoretical sorption capacity (36.7 wt%). Likewise, MCM-41–Li4SiO4 showed excellent cycling stability during 20 cycles.232 However, high cost and complex synthesis remain the main drawbacks for the practical use of these new silica sources. Hence, some waste materials or natural minerals containing silicon have also been used as raw materials to both reduce the cost and promote waste valorization. Recently, lithium precursors such as Li2CO3, LiNO3, LiOH, and few organometallic salts have also been used as Li-precursors.233 The use of LiOH as a lithium source can significantly decrease the synthesis temperature to 600 °C, thanks to its higher reactivity than that of Li2CO3 and LiNO3.233
 Fig. 35  (a) Li4SiO4 synthesis from SBA-15 zeolite (Reproduced from ref. 226 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017), (b) Li4SiO4 obtained by sol–gel method (Reproduced from ref. 234 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017), and (c) Li4SiO4 modification by adding TiO2 nanotubes and porous carbon (Reproduced from ref. 235 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017; reproduced from ref. 236 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2016). 
To decrease the preparation temperature and time, novel processing methods have been developed in recent years, including sol–gel method,237 impregnation-precipitation method, glycine-nitrate solution combustion method, and “solvo-plasma” technique.234 Nano-sized or special morphological ceramic materials could be synthesized via these methods. The fine powder or fast heating of raw materials greatly diminished the sintering. In contrast, the conventional solid-state method can only obtain micron-size ceramic particles. Recently, Li4SiO4 nanowire sorbent was firstly synthesized using the “solvo-plasma” technique (Fig. 35b).234 The fabricated sorbent demonstrated a fast sorption rate (sorption capacity reaches 25 wt% in 3 min) and a high sorption capacity (about 35 wt%) due to the high SSA of the nanowires. Unfortunately, serious sintering issues related to the nanowire morphology led to difficult regeneration.
Back-up technical solutions have been set involving ball milling or hydration-calcination method as auxiliary technologies so as to intimately mix raw materials, thus further enhancing the SSA of the as-obtained Li4SiO4 powder.238 The addition of pore forming agents is another strategy to raise the porosity and the SSA of the adsorbent. Pore forming agents generally include carbon materials, organic materials, or porous materials with excellent thermal stability at high temperatures. Carbon and, in general, organic materials become volatile at high synthesis temperature and result in pore formation. In contrast, thermally stable porous materials still remain within the samples, thus generating a secondary porous network. The presence of these pores does not only enhance the mass transfer phenomena but also increases the contact area between the reactants. For instance, TiO2 nanotubes, polyethylene, and porous carbon have been used to design Li4SiO4 sorbents (Fig. 35c).
The main reason for the limitation of the CO2 capture capacity is the dense shell composed of carbonated products (Li2CO3 and Li2SiO3) covering the unreacted core of Li4SiO4. The loading of molten salts, mainly consisting of single, binary, or ternary alkali metal salts, is an effective way to promote the diffusion of CO2 and ions. Indeed, it can form low-temperature eutectic compounds with Li2CO3 and further cause the formation of a “liquid product shell”. Compared with the solid state, the ‘molten state’ is more conducive for the diffusion of CO2 and ions. Hence, this strategy greatly improves the sorption property of Li4SiO4 at low temperatures and CO2 concentration. Zhang et al.232 proved that compared with pure Li4SiO4, the sorption capacity of 10 wt% Na2CO3-loaded Li4SiO4 was increased from 10.8 to 34.7 wt% at 550 °C under 20 vol% CO2.
The surface modification of Li4SiO4 is usually aimed to improve the diffusion capacity of CO2. However, the influence of ion migration on the property of Li4SiO4 sorbents cannot be neglected. The point defects as a result of charge compensation can be formed by the doping of ions into the crystal lattice, which would enhance the ion hopping ability. Recent studies have confirmed that a solid solution can be formed by doping Al, Fe, Na, Ti, Ge, etc., with Li4SiO4via the isomorphous substitution of Si4+ or Li+ in the Li4SiO4 crystal lattice.230,239 Among these solid solutions, Li4+xSi1−xAlxO4 has been widely studied due to its CO2 sorption capacity, compared with Li4SiO4, which can be efficiently improved at medium temperature ranges (e.g., 400–600 °C). Furthermore, a recent study showed that the solid solution of Li4SiO4 doped with Ge (Li4SixGe1−xO4) exhibited excellent sorption performance at a wider operational temperature range (150–680 °C).240
However, the mechanism of promoting the sorbent performance by the addition of other elements is complex and still under debate. For example, Al and Ca react with lithium or silicon to form by-products, such as LiAlO2 or Ca2SiO4, which can then improve the sorption capacity as the promoters in the Li4SiO4 sorbent. The addition of a certain amount of CeO2 can inhibit the aggregation of Li4SiO4 crystals and obtain foam-like Li4SiO4 with improved sorption rate and capacity. The addition of Na, Ti, or Br can also inhibit the growth at the grain boundaries and avoid changes in the morphology of Li4SiO4.230 Besides, doping with sintering-resistant materials (Y2O3 or Gd2O3) also enhances the regeneration performance of the sorbent, thanks to the higher dispersion of Li4SiO4 particles.240 Recently, silicon sources from cheap wastes or minerals has aroused intensive interest, such as blast furnace slag,241 fly ash, diatomite, rice husk ash, halloysite, sediment,242 and biomass ash. Pure SiO2 can be obtained after treatment with acids. However, acid waste solution generated by this process can cause secondary pollution. The sorption performance of the obtained Li4SiO4 is similar to the sorbent synthesized by purified SiO2 or even worse, and the regeneration performance is not significantly improved. To avoid acid pollution, Wang et al.243 used an acid solution, which was produced using the natural silicon source (vermiculite) acid treatment to synthesize Mg–Al LDH. In this new route, two kinds of CO2 sorbents were obtained (Li4SiO4 and Mg–Al LDH) while avoiding pollution due to waste acid liquids. This study also demonstrated that metal oxide impurities that exist in vermiculite as dispersants can improve the cycling performance of Li4SiO4. Furthermore, Li4SiO4 synthesized by BFS-derived silica gel exhibited a fast sorption rate and stable sorption capacity in different CO2 concentration atmospheres (5–100 vol%). The presence of Ti4+ and Al3+ in BFS can improve the CO2 uptake performance (Fig. 36a).241
 Fig. 36  (a) Schematic diagram of the mechanism (replacement of Li+ by foreign ions) (Reproduced from ref. 241 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019), (b) CO2 sorption isotherms of vermiculite–Li4SiO4 and SiO2–Li4SiO4 sorbents under different CO2 concentrations (100 vol%, 50 vol%, 20 vol%) at 550 °C, and (c) the schematic illustration of the synthesis of a Li4SiO4-based CO2 sorbent from a vermiculite precursor (Reproduced from ref. 228 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
As a natural mineral, vermiculite possesses a higher silicon content (about 40%) than fly ash, and also contains various alkaline earth metals and alkali metals (Mg, Al, K, etc.). Li4SiO4 synthesized by vermiculite exhibited high sorption capacity (20.7 wt%) and excellent sorption stability under different operating conditions. As shown in Fig. 36b, the vermiculite–Li4SiO4 sorption capacity only declined by 0.8 wt% (from 19.4 to 18.6 wt%) with the reduction of CO2 concentration from 100 to 20 vol%, whilst the traditional SiO2–Li4SiO4 capture capacity deteriorated rapidly. This result may be attributed to the porous structure and alkali metals that the adsorbent inherits from vermiculite. Though the sorption capacity of vermiculite–Li4SiO4 is still lower than that of Li4SiO4 prepared by pure SiO2 reagent, the excellent cycling performance and sorption capacity at low CO2 concentrations is warranted for industrial applications. The porous structure of vermiculite–Li4SiO4 can exhibit reduced mass transfer limitation for CO2 diffusion. Likewise, the alkali metal oxides in vermiculite not only promote the CO2 sorption capacity but also prevent the sintering of the particles (Fig. 36c).
Recently, Wang et al.244 synthesized a new nanoparticle/flaker composite silicon source, which was able to adjust the silicon content. For this silicon source, vermiculite and MCM-41 mesoporous silica were connected by the hydrothermal method. The prepared Li4SiO4 presented high SSA (6.8 m2 g−1) and high crystallinity with the even dispersion of alkali metals or alkali earth metals from vermiculite. These characteristics had a positive effect on the adsorption property of Li4SiO4. Compared with Li4SiO4 sorbents obtained from single silicon sources, (MCM-41/vermiculite)–Li4SiO4 demonstrated a higher CO2 sorption capacity at low temperature and low CO2 concentration, reaching 26.1 wt% at 550 °C under 20 vol% CO2. Furthermore, the sorption capacity was slightly diminished by about 1.4 wt% after 20 cycles. In general, a sufficiently high SiO2 content, the presence of moderate amounts of thermally stable impurities, and the presence of alkali or alkaline compounds are necessary (but probably not sufficient) features for silicon precursors to fabricate efficient Li4SiO4 sorbents.
2.4.2.3 Perspectives and outlook of alkali silicate-based sorbents. 
Recently, the demand of a high-temperature CO2 sorbent gradually increased with the SEHP process development. In this process, CO2 as the by-product, is generated simultaneously with H2, which greatly affects the H2 purity and limits the production rate when the equilibrium is gradually reached. It is therefore mandatory to introduce a suitable CO2 sorbent into the process to shift the equilibrium for improved H2 purity yield and reduced energy consumption. However, the presence of SO2, NOx, and H2O in the flue gas further raise the complexity of post-combustion CO2 capture. The post-combustion CO2 capture process by using high temperature CO2 sorbent is considered as an effective way to reduce CO2 emissions. Pacciani et al.245 proved that the existence of SO2 in the flue gas will cause the deactivation of lithium-based sorbents due to the irreversible reaction between Li4SiO4 and SO2. Therefore, removing SO2 is mandatory prior to CO2 capture. There is no clear decrease in the CO2 sorption capacity over Li4SiO4 in the 0–1000 ppm NO concentration range due to the chemical inertia of NO with Li4SiO4. In contrast, the presence of steam in flue gas sorption conditions can promote CO2 capture of Li4SiO4 for the enhanced diffusion of Li+. The industrial applications require prilling of Li4SiO4 powder, which is the last step in sample preparation after the different steps involved in the optimization of the Li4SiO4 sorbent. Though the study of granulation technology of Li4SiO4 is limited, promising results could be obtained using extrusion-spheronization, agar method, and graphite moulding pelletization method. Considering that the particle size of the sorbent increases rapidly after granulation, the addition of a pore-forming agent in the prilling process is therefore necessary to reduce the diffusion resistance of CO2. For the Li4SiO4 sorbent, the optimization of synthetic conditions and methods greatly improve the sorption capacity under pure CO2 atmosphere. However, the CO2 concentration in practical applications are varied in a wide range. Thus, improving the regeneration performance and sorption capacity at low CO2 concentration are of paramount importance. In addition, the impact of the complex compositions in the flue gas, such as fly ash, SO2, and H2O, on the Li4SiO4 sorbent performance should be assessed in a more systematic manner. In addition, the granulation of the Li4SiO4 powder needs to be considered due to the difficulties in using and recycling the powder in a machine. However, there are few reports about it at present.


2.4.3 Alkali titanate-based sorbents. 
Traditional solid chemical CO2 sorbents, such as CaO-based, alkali silica-based, and alkali zirconate-based sorbents, have been widely investigated in terms of the sorption performance and modification. However, in addition to material modification, the proposal of a new capturing material has also attracted widespread attention due to the improved cycling stability and other limitations. A variety of novel materials have been proposed for high-temperature CO2 chemisorption in recent years. Nevertheless, the sintering effect over multiple sorption-desorption processes still largely restricts the development of these materials. Meanwhile, a new promising CO2 capture material, alkali titanate-based sorbent with a similar structure to that of silicate- and zirconate-based sorbents, has shown selective CO2 sorption capacity and great potential for high-temperature CO2 capture. The titanates possessed a typical layered structure with a chemical formula of MxTiyOx/2+2y·zH2O (M = H, Li, Na, K, etc.). Similar to other layered materials, these titanates have a 2D sheet structure formed by Ti and O atoms, with cations or neutral molecules located inside the layers’ spaces. A majority of layers consist of many edge-sharing TiO6 octahedral blocks, such as H2Ti3O7 and K2Ti6O13. There are still a few titanates possessing different Ti atom- coordinated structures. For example, K2Ti2O5 exhibits a unique layered structure, which has a five-fold coordination structure and then forms the 2D (Ti2O5)2− sheets.246
2.4.3.1 Progress in alkali titanate-based sorbents. 
Alkali titanate-based materials were used for low-temperature CO2 capture at first. However, over the past decades, significant research efforts have been devoted to the development of alkali titanate-based sorbents, particularly for the SESR processes of pre-combustion CO2 capture. By overcoming the reaction equilibrium limitation, it is possible to achieve the complete conversion of CH4 or CO by the sorption by a solid CO2 sorbent. Initially, the performance of CO2 sorption and desorption capacities was investigated over Li4TiO4 at high temperatures. Li4TiO4 can adsorb CO2 from a lower temperature (250 °C) to about 900 °C and shows a significantly fast sorption rate promotion at 710 °C. The weight increase was approximately 27 wt% at 710 °C during continuous heating and carbonization, with the formation of Li2TiO3 and Li2CO3. Besides, CO2 uptake increased with the decreasing diameter of the sorbents. However, this material suffers from poor stability over repeated cycles due to increased particle size and decreased reactive SSA. Moreover, the prepared Li4TiO4 deteriorates easily when exposed to the atmosphere.
Sodium titanate is widely used in various fields, such as catalysis and wastewater treatment,247 and has been utilized as a possible CO2 sorbent in the recent years. Furthermore, potassium titanates, especially potassium dititanate (K2Ti2O5), have also been systematically investigated for their CO2 capture capacity, reaction kinetics, and regeneration ability. In contrast to other sorption materials suffering from serious sintering problems due to high temperature operation condition of multiple cycles, K2Ti2O5 showed a typical particle size “break-down” process. The morphology cracked down into K2Ti2O5 nanofibers instead of bulk particles at high temperature after 20 cycles. Thanks to the morphological changes, CO2 uptake was increased in the first few cycles and then finally reached a plateau. In addition, K2Ti2O5 showed a dramatic improvement in reversible structure switching during the CO2 sorption/desorption processes (Fig. 37). A rapid and complete release of captured CO2 can be achieved within only 6.0 min at the same temperature (750 °C).246
 Fig. 37  The proposed CO2 sorption and desorption mechanism over K2Ti2O5 caused by reversible structure switching.246 
Recently, to further enhance the CO2 capture capability and to develop advanced sorption materials, the fabrication of KNaTiO3 was carried out and its capture performance was systematically investigated based on previous studies by Wang et al.248 The weight decrease was only about 1.6 wt% (from 19.0 to 17.4 wt%) with reduced CO2 concentration from pure CO2 to 20 vol%, indicating that KNaTiO3 is very suitable for application in industry (Fig. 38a). In terms of the actual conditions, it is necessary to understand the effect of vapor on the capture properties. In the presence of steam, the mass loss in the sorption performance of KNaTiO3 under 20 and 50 vol% CO2 concentration was 0.87 and 1.38 wt% (Fig. 38b), respectively. Generally, KNaTiO3 has less dependence on CO2 concentration and has higher tolerance for the existence of water vapor in the CO2 uptake rate and the sorption capacity. Moreover, it has an outstanding thermal stability during 20 cycles under different CO2 partial pressures and its performance achieves a large step forward compared to the original research on alkali titanate-based sorbents.
 Fig. 38  (a) The influence of CO2 sorption capacity over KNaTiO3 and K2Ti2O5 under different CO2 concentrations, (b) the cycling performance of KNaTiO3 and other capturing materials (K2Ti2O5 and neat CaO) at different CO2 partial pressures (Reproduced from ref. 248 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2020). 
The CO2 chemisorption/desorption mechanism for alkali titanate-based sorbents, similar to silicate- and zirconate-based materials, is mainly ascribed to the double-shell theory. To be more specific, when the CO2 sorption process is carried out on Na-doped Li2TiO3 particles, though external eutectic molten carbonates (Li2CO3 and Na2CO3) cannot react with CO2, it can facilitate the transfer of gaseous CO2 into the inner unreacted layer. Na2TiO3 possesses different CO2 capture reaction pathways, which is heavily dependent on the capture temperature. A detailed mechanism for K2Ti2O5 was proposed and verified by DFT calculations. O2− and K+ are released from K2TiO5 and further encounter CO2, forming K2CO3 among the outer layer. The shortage of K+ resulted in the transformation of K2Ti2O5 to form K2Ti4O9 with a lower K/Ti ratio rate. After losing two K atoms by interaction with CO2, the system energy could be elevated by 13.8 eV. The structural transformation into K2Ti4O9 can result in a system energy reduction by about 50.6 eV and better stability. KNaTiO3 rapidly transformed into K2Ti6O13, Na2Ti6O13, Na2CO3, and K2CO3 after CO2 adsorption. The double-shell model was proposed by Ida et al.249 to explain the CO2 sorption-desorption mechanism on pure and modified Li2ZrO3 in 2003.
2.4.3.2 Summary and outlook for alkali titanate-based sorbents. 
So far, few studies on titanate-based sorbents have been reported, considering their enormous potential in CO2 capture performance, theoretical research, and practical application, compared with other high-temperature CO2 sorption materials. However, current studies on alkali titanate-based sorbents have mainly been confined to the fabrication of sorption materials and the characterization of their sorption properties. More work is needed to clarify the systematic research results under various conditions and detailed reaction mechanism. With respect to novel materials, it would have bright prospects in material modification, such as a change in the different titanium sources and other alkali or transition metal promotion. Besides, bulk titanate-based materials are usually prepared by solid state reactions. The SSAs of these prepared materials are small, which limits their practical sorption performance. Hence, the selection and utilization of different synthetic methods is also worthy of further discussion.
The CO2 separation and capture technology using alkali titanate-based sorbents can be applied in a variety of realistic conditions. However, in the pre-combustion capture technology, the CO2 concentration in steam (i.e., 15–60% by volume, dry basis)250 is often higher than that in other technologies. The suitability should be estimated under realistic conditions, including testing temperature, gas flow rate, various CO2 concentrations, effect of water vapor, foreign gases, and impurities.
The chemisorption–desorption kinetics is one of the key factors for evaluating the performance of sorption materials. Some studies about sorption kinetics have been investigated using various theoretical kinetic models, such as the power law model, the SCM, the Avrami–Erofe’ev model, and the double exponential model. Besides, kinetic limitation under low CO2 concentrations is recognized as the main obstacle for many sorbents. To overcome this limitation and to enhance the sorption performance, sorption kinetics analysis should be taken into consideration as one of the essential factors to evaluate the CO2 capture property in the future. The reason that the coexistence of K and Na in the KNaTiO3's crystal shows better CO2 sorption than single metal ion in K2Ti2O5 is not fully understood. Further investigation of the cooperation mechanism of binary ions would provide a theoretical support for the modification of other materials. The industrial application of alkali titanate-based sorbents for CO2 capture also demands a prolonged evolution in terms of the economic advantages in the future.


2.4.4 Implementation and challenges in pre-combustion applications. 
The pre-combustion CO2 capture process is that by which CO2 is separated from H2-rich syngas or reformed gas at high pressures (2–7 MPa) and intermediate temperatures (200–450 °C). Commonly, the capture facility is placed after the WGS reactor, where CO is catalytically converted to CO2 and H2; however, it may be integrated with WGS to shift the equilibrium conversion. For reforming processes operated at high temperatures (>450 °C), high temperature CO2 adsorbents such as CaO and Li2SiO3 are also possible to be directly added into the catalytic reactors to enhance the H2 yields by in situ CO2 capture. The PSA process is preferred in pre-combustion CO2 capture because it can take advantage of the high CO2 concentrations (e.g., 15–60%). It should be noted that pre-combustion low-temperature separation technologies are still limited to lab tests (TRL3), probably due to the complicated processes, high equipment cost, and large energy consumption. In contrast, the concept of WGC, in which CO2 in the shifted gas is directly removed at elevated temperatures without pre-cooling the shifted gas, has received wide attention.251 Weak chemisorbents with reversible CO2 capture capacities are suitable for WGC systems.
SEWGS technology is a typical WGC process for pre-combustion CO2 capture, which achieves WGS catalysis, sulfur removal, and CO2 capture in a single step. The ECN has devoted to branching out SEWGS by using commercialized K2CO3-promoted hydrotalcites as CO2 adsorbents since 2005. The technical feasibility of SEWGS was verified in the 2 year CCP.252 A co-current CO2 rinsing step after the feed and a counter-current steam purge step for regeneration was introduced into the seven-bed, ten-step PSA process so as to reduce H2 loss in the product gas. However, the deployment of the CO2 rinsing step gave rise to lower productivity and consumed the CO2 compression energy; thus, in the following European FP6 project CACHET, it was replaced by a steam rinse step.253 The European FP7 project CAESAR mainly focused on reducing steam consumption for the rinsing and purging steps via operating parameter optimization.254 It is worth noting that the steam consumption could be much lower than expected if the co-adsorption of H2O is considered during the feed and rinse steps.255 In 2013, ECN announced that the SEWGS technology was ready for scale-up.256 Currently, SEWGS is being demonstrated in the STEPWISE project for CO2 capture from BFG with the objective of achieving 85% CCR with 60% lower energy consumption and 25% lower capture cost.257
TDA developed another PSA-based WGC technology for pre-combustion CO2 capture operated at 190–260 °C by using functionalized mesoporous carbon. In the 0.1 MWe-scale field test at the National Carbon Capture Center, the 4-bed, 8-step PSA prototype underwent 1500 adsorption/desorption cycles without performance degradation.258 A recent simulation study indicated that an 8-bed, 10-step PSA based on TDA-patented adsorbents can achieve both high CCR (>90%) and CP (>99%) with low energy consumption of 0.34 MJe kg−1.259 TDA is planning to build a pilot-scale PSA plant at the Sinopec Yangzi Petrochemical Plant in China. In another project, TDA is developing a low-temperature WGS–PSA combined PSA process but with a small scale (10 kg CO2 h−1).260 Compared to the single PSA process, the integrated WGS–PSA improved the system efficiency by 0.5%.
Tsinghua University conducted basic studies on ET–PSA, which exhibits 20.3–26.6% lower energy consumption compared with the Selexol process.261 In a 5 year project (2011AA050601), a four-bed, eight-step ET–PSA prototype was built to handle 6 N m3 h−1 CO2/H2/H2S mixed gas at 400 °C and 30 bar.262 The results from 75 h of continuous operation and 1089 h of accumulative operation indicate that the lab-scale prototype achieves 95.7–98.6% CCR and more than 99% H2S removal ratio. Since 2016, research has focused on high-purity H2 production with in situ CO2 separation. As a recent achievement, a 2-train ET–PSA with reflux structures was executed to exhibit both high hydrogen purity (HP, 99.9994%) and hydrogen recovery ratio (HRR, 97.51%) from the shifted gas.263 In a project funded by the Shanxi Government (MH2015-06), an ET–PSA unit with a processing capability of 5000 N m3 h−1 has been planned to be constructed for the high-purity H2 production from a coal-to-ammonia plant by the end of 2020.
SER using high temperature CO2 adsorbents is an efficient technology to produce high purity H2 with CO2 capture from various carbon sources including methane, biomass, glycerol, bio-oil, and ethanol.264 The SER system captures CO2 at the time of its production, thus eliminating the individual reactor for WGS and the downstream H2 purification process. In addition, the coupled carbonation process enables to provide the endothermic reforming reactions with heat energy. Besides the conventional fixed bed configuration, the DFB reactor, where the saturated adsorbents are regenerated in a separate reactor with additional heat from combustion, is commonly adopted for SER. Pure O2 and recycled flue gas for cooling are required for the calcination process to concentrate CO2. In recently years, several SER pilot plants have been successfully constructed. Unfortunately, most of the pilot-scale projects used SER technologies only to regulate the H2-to-CO ratio for downstream synthetic processes and to mitigate the reforming energy consumption, where the removed CO2 was released back into the flue gas without concentrating. An exceptional example is the SER tests with oxyfuel combustion conducted in a 200 kWth DFB reactor at the IFK Stuttgart using wood gasification as the fuel gas source. It was reported that when the operation mode for calcination was switched from air to O2 combustion, the CO2 content in the flue gas increased from 26.7 vol% to up to 95 vol%.
In summary, the PSA-based WGC process is one of the most promising options for pre-combustion CO2 capture. Several pilot-scale (TRL 6) WGC plants are now being constructed worldwide. Table 2 summarizes the simulation results of adsorption-based pre-combustion CO2 capture. Meanwhile, several challenges at the adsorbent, reactor, and system scales have been exposed. Although great progress has been achieved in the development of intermediate- and high-temperature CO2 adsorbents, their working capacities are still lower than those of low-temperature adsorbents for post-combustion CO2 capture. Molten salt-promoted MgO exhibits impressive capacities through bulk phase reactions but prototypes should be fabricated to verify its performance stability in pre-combustion CO2 capture. Current WGC systems still consume a large amount of high-temperature steam to achieve both high purity and recovery. Although multi-train PSA is able to reduce the energy consumption, it leads to higher operational complexity and capital cost. In addition to CO2, the removal of impurities, such as H2S, COS, HCl, and heavy metals, at elevated temperatures also needs to be considered. Note that the WGC processes are applicable not only in IGCC but also in NGCC, biomass gasification power plants, integrated steelworks, and coal chemical industries, where CO2-rich and H2-rich streams with high pressures and elevated temperatures are generated. SER hydrogen production processes based on high temperature CO2 adsorbents with pure O2 generated by an additional air separation unit for the calcination process provide feasibility for pre-combustion CO2 capture; however, detailed techno-economic analysis should be conducted to evaluate the capture cost.
