3. Industrial carbon dioxide utilization
To combat climate change, an important strategy is to close the anthropogenic carbon cycle and the key requirement focuses on the effective combination of CO2 capture with further conversion.268–270 The utilization of CO2 to generate value-added products could reduce the net cost of removing atmospheric CO2 and mitigating the emissions.271 The import dependency of countries without fossil fuel production could be alleviated by CO2-based fuels with the diversification of energy supply and the reinforcement of energy security.272–274 In addition, other environmental impacts, for instance, particular matter and NOx emissions from fuels, could be mitigated by CCU products.275–277 The comparison with conventional chemical production processes combined with the assessment of CO2-based chemical production impacts are of significant importance.278 From a life cycle perspective, polyol production and formic acid generation via hydrogenation have emerged as the best promising conversion technologies for global warming impact mitigation.279 Recent estimates have put CO2 utilization potential at 10% for the fuels sector and 1–7% for the chemicals sector. Over 110 Mt per year CO2 have been transformed into chemicals such as urea (70 Mt CO2 used per Mt product, annual market demand 100 Mt per year), methanol (14 Mt CO2 used per Mt product, annual market demand 40 Mt per year), and inorganic carbonates (30 Mt CO2 used per Mt product, annual market demand 80 Mt per year). The deployment of various CO2 utilization technologies could potentially reduce annual CO2 emissions by 3.7 Gt, which equates to approximately 10% of the current annual CO2 emissions. With the exception of very few materials, the utilization would overwhelm the market if deployed at a large scale.280 In this section, we have systematically summarized the following aspects that are dominant research interests towards CO2 utilization, including thermochemical conversion of CO2, photochemical conversion of CO2, electrochemical conversion of CO2, photoelectrochemical conversion of CO2, and photothermal conversion of CO2 and CO2 as soft oxidants for dehydrogenation.
3.1. Thermochemical conversion of CO2
Considering that CO2 is an economical and abundant source, the thermo-catalytic transformation of CO2 into chemicals is a promising strategy for CO2 utilization that not only contributes to mitigate climate change caused by increasing CO2 emissions but also provides a sustainable means towards essential chemicals or materials production. Indeed, the thermochemical conversion of CO2 to platform chemicals and fuels could be quickly industrially implemented at a large-scale, hence further speeding up the great efforts that mankind has made to diminish CO2 emissions. In order to obtain a drastic reduction in CO2 molecules, large-scale conversion plants are going to be built for clean fuels. The rise in oil barrel prices, or at least their volatility, have led numerous researchers to focus on the electrochemical, photochemical, biological, or catalytic conversion of CO2. Another strong incentive for the conversion of CO2 to fuels is the ongoing energy transition, which aims to change the way electricity is produced by processes that emit less CO2. The increasing penetration of intermittent renewable energy sources is accompanied by difficulties in managing electricity grids, and the chemical storage of excess electricity in hydrogen and then, in combination with CO2, in fuels or chemicals (power-to-X processes) that further facilitates the development of these decarbonized energy sources in the energy mix. Hence, the direct conversion of CO2 has a strong effect on the reduction in CO2 emissions. The CO2 molecule is thermodynamically stable and kinetically inert;281 therefore, its activation acts as the rate-determining step in the transformation into value-added chemicals. Usually, another valuable reactant with high energy content is reacted with CO2 due to its efficient conversion. H2 is the most widely used reducing agent as it can be supplied from renewable energy via the relatively mature technology of water electrolysis.282
However, based on the aforementioned “apparent” chemical inertness, rather harsh conditions, such as high temperature and pressure, are required for accelerating the reaction rate or shifting the chemical equilibrium. Hence, CO2 hydrogenation reactions to form methane, CO, methanol, and several hydrocarbons appear as a sound option and will be reported in the coming sections. In consideration of the long history of these reactions and extensive works dedicated to those topics, the state of the art of the technologies and processes will be summarized herein for the in-depth understanding of the reaction mechanisms as well as the synthetic strategies of the catalysts. Due to the high cost of noble metals, special effort will be focused on the deployment of cheaper transition metals such as Fe, Co, Ni, and Cu. In addition, industrial CO2 utilization situations all over the world will also be reviewed.

3.1.1 CO2 methanation via the thermochemical route. 
Methane, the main component in natural gas, plays an important role not only in industrial processes but also acts as the main cooking and heating medium in Europe. Besides, methane can be easily liquefied and stored safely in large quantities. The presence of numerous existing infrastructures provides a tremendous convenience for storage and transportation of this high energy density alkane. The conversion of CO2 into methane, according to the CO2 methanation reaction CO2 + 4H2 → CH4 + 2H2O, the so-called Sabatier–Senderens reaction, is therefore meaningful for both the industry and consumer needs of our daily life. NASA has even investigated the process of converting carbon dioxide existing in the Martian atmosphere, where the reduction agent (H2) is generated from the H2O splitting process, to CH4 and H2O as an energy source and life-support system of astronauts.
The COx methanation reaction is industrially operated at moderate pressure (5–20 bar) and moderate temperatures in the range of 200–500 °C up to 800 °C in the Tremp process. The main difficulty in operating CO2 methanation reactors is the management of the heat release caused by the strong exothermicity of the reaction to maintain thermodynamically favorable conditions and to avoid catalyst deactivation by sintering under hydrothermal conditions. The catalytic systems concerning group VIII noble metals (i.e., Ru and Rh) supported on conventional oxide carriers (Al2O3, SiO2, ZrO2, TiO2, and CeO2) as well as their mixtures have been widely investigated for the Sabatier reaction.283 In particular, Ni-based catalysts have been recognized as the most promising materials in terms of the activity/selectivity/stability typically for CO2 methanation at relatively low cost. Different strategies have been employed to further enhance the activity or improve the methane selectivity, such as by introducing promoters at the active sites or support modification by tuning metal–support interactions.284,285 Peculiar electronic interactions between the metal and its support strongly impact the catalyst performance, which is triggered by the modification of the chemical bonding strength of the chemisorbed species. Hence, reducible supports are suggested to provide suitable active sites for CO2 activation, thanks to the oxygen vacancies generated during the reduction process.
Thus, depending on the nature of the support, different reaction mechanisms have been proposed: the “direct” methanation of CO2 through the CHOx intermediate species or the “indirect” methanation through the formation of CO. Conventional Ni/alumina catalysts promote the indirect methanation pathway. Finally, different promoters have also been evaluated and targeted for enhancing the metal reducibility, nanoparticle dispersion, CO2 adsorption, and further activation. For instance, Liu and co-workers showed that the introduction of the LaOx promoter into Ni/CNT promoted the number of basic sites that are necessary for CO2 adsorption, which resulted in not only lower CO2 activation temperature but also extremely high selectivity for methane compared with the pristine Ni catalyst.284 The 10Ni–2La/CNT catalyst with 2 wt% La-activated CO2 at 163 °C reached the full-selectivity towards CH4. The LaOx species in sub-nano clusters deposited on Ni NPs were suggested as the real (promoted) active sites. Similar results were also observed for BEA-type zeolite-supported Ni catalysts after the introduction of the La promoter.285
Reducible supports have been widely investigated in a large variety of reactions, such as WGS, CO oxidation, and CO/CO2 hydrogenation, which can be attributed to the existence of oxygen vacancies formed during the reduction process.286–288 The oxygen vacancies participate in the catalytic processes via two key roles: (i) storage and release of oxygen; (ii) promotion of dispersion and activity for the supported noble metal.289,290
Wei et al.286 revealed the reaction mechanisms of CO2 methanation with the comparison of Ru active sites’ supported on α-Al2O3 and CeO2 (Fig. 39c). Combined with operando XANES, Raman, and IR techniques, the Ce3+ sites were generated and oxygen vacancies as well as the surface hydroxyl groups were presented in the Ru/CeO2 catalyst during the catalytic reaction. By using SSITKA-type in situ DRIFT-IR, a formate route could be assessed over the Ru/CeO2 catalyst with all of the three aforementioned species getting involved. The dissociation of the formate species to methanol was found to be the rate-determining step, which was catalyzed by the presence of O-vacancies. In contrast, metallic Ru is considered to be the sole active site for the CO pathway on Ru supported on chemically inert α-Al2O3.
 Fig. 39  (a) The possible CO2 catalytic hydrogenation mechanisms. Pathways 1 and 2, in the preceding green and red boxes, indicate reverse-water–gas-shift (RWGS) mechanisms (Reproduced from ref. 282 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018). (b) Qualitative scheme of the reaction network involving RWGS, carbon monoxide methanation, and carbon dioxide (Sabatier) methanation processes (Reproduced from ref. 291 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016). (c) Schematic illustration of the formate route for CO2 methanation over the Ru/CeO2 catalyst, (1) conversion of CO2 to CO2δ−, (2) hydrogenation of CO2δ− to formate, (3) dissociation of formate to methanol, (4) hydrogenation of methanol to CH4 (Reproduced from ref. 286 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2016). 
In contrast to Fischer–Tropsch synthesis, where it has been generally accepted that the particle size strongly affects the reaction rate,292 this factor has been seldom reported for the Sabatier reaction, especially that catalyzed by Ni-based catalysts. However, Weckhuysen et al.,282 using sophisticated operando spectroscopic tools, unraveled the structure sensitivity between the Ni particles and the stability of the reaction intermediates. The CO adsorption strength, which is directly related to the formation of Ni(CO)4, diminished with an increase in the Ni particle size, thus leading to the selective formation of CO with the decrease in the Ni NPs size (Fig. 39a). Schlögl and co-workers sketched the contours of the detailed reactions that occur and the stable surface species existing on the nickel catalyst surface by using some advanced techniques (Fig. 39b).291
CO2 methanation offers multiple perspectives, for instance, the introduction of renewable energy into the energy system through SNG as a carrier (Fig. 40). Firstly, SNG benefits from the technology reserves and the existing infrastructures of the natural gas sector for efficient transportation to places for power or heat generation, or as fuels. Secondly, renewable SNG conduces to the secure energy supply with the aid of large gas storage facilities that already exist and dedicates to the reduction of the net carbon emissions when coal or biomass are employed as feedstocks. In addition, the development of novel methanation processes with an automated and safe control of intermittent operation is also required for small-scale decentralized productions. It could be associated with the delocalized production of biogas by methanation, where the separation of CO2 from methane before injection into the gas grid is inherent to the process, thus providing a source of CO2 ready to be valorized.
 Fig. 40  The key role of methanation for the supply of renewable substitute natural gas (Reproduced from ref. 293 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2014). 


3.1.2 Dry reforming of CO2 and methane via the thermochemical route. 
Methane dry reforming (DRM, CO2 + CH4 → 2CO + 2H2) represents an efficient way for CO2 utilization, allowing the production of valuable syngas. It has been reported that all syngas-producing processes are afflicted by coking. The zones of thermodynamic stability of solid carbon are illustrated in Fig. 41.294 Coke formation is especially prominent in the DRM between 550–700 °C according to the Boudouard reaction (2CO ⇔ C + CO2) and above 900 °C due to methane pyrolysis (CH4 → C + 2H2).
 Fig. 41  C–H–O phase ternary diagram (graphite, 1 bar) (Reproduced from ref. 294 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
In accordance with the principle of microreversibility, Ni catalysts have been identified as one of the most promising candidates for the DRM process owing to their relatively low price and high catalytic activity.295 Since coke formation can barely be avoided over conventional Ni based catalysts, strategies for designing sintering- and coke-resistant catalysts or even an in situ manner for coke removal are therefore optional.
Yavuz et al.296 recently synthesized a single-crystalline MgO-supported NiMo catalyst, which exhibited superior stability over 850 h with almost 100% conversion of CO2 and methane without coke formation (Fig. 42a–c). In situ TEM and SEM observations disclosed the migration of Ni–Mo nanoparticles to the high-energy step edges of crystalline MgO(111), hence forming a stable and sustained average particle size of 17 nm during activation. Thereinto, the single-crystalline MgO and Ni–Mo alloy are both necessary for conferring excellent sintering- and coke-resistant properties. This method may provide an inspiration for the preparation of catalysts with high stability and selective product formation in the near future. Qiao and co-workers297 designed single Ni atoms trapped in the CeOx-doped hydroxyapatite catalyst. In this catalyst, isolated Ni atoms could inhibit coke formation owing to the peculiar ability of the activation of only the first σ C–H bond of methane, thus preventing the consecutive decomposition to coke. The doping of Ce in HAP induces strong metal–support interactions, which stabilizes single Ni atoms. This catalyst exhibited extremely superior catalytic activity as well as stability for at least 100 h of DRM with negligible coke formation.
 Fig. 42  (a) NOSCE mechanism for the sustained catalytic activity of NiMoCat. (b) Synchrotron analysis of NiMoCat sampled at different times under reactive conditions. (c) HAADF-STEM analysis of the spent catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 296 with permission from American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2020). (d) A summary of thermodynamic properties of eligible metal candidates for participation in the CH4/CO2 redox cycle for DRM (Reproduced from ref. 298 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2020). (e) Proposed DRM mechanism using m-V2CTx MXene as the precursor, highlighting the transformation of V8C7 into V2O3 nanoparticles in-between the layers and on the surface via an in situ redox mechanism (Reproduced from ref. 299 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2020). 
To remove the carbon deposition during the DRM process, a downstream process was elaborated by McFarland et al.298 based on the coupling of methane dry reforming with the catalytic pyrolysis of methane in a molten metal bubble column reactor (Fig. 42d). A coupled reduction–oxidation process occurs, in which CO2 was reduced by the liquid metallic species and methane was partially oxidized to syngas by the produced metal oxide intermediate, thus regenerating the active metal species. Three advantages of this smart strategy were proposed: (i) the H2/CO ratio could be regulated by varying the CH4/CO2 feed ratio; (ii) this process produces syngas by consuming CO2, unlike SMR, which produces CO2; (iii) the solid carbon produced by the reaction floats to the surface of the melt, where it can be easily removed. Furthermore, each gas bubble in the bubbling column reactor is in contact with the carbon-free molten catalyst surface; hence, neither coking nor coarsening may occur. Meanwhile, the metal in the catalyst has to satisfy three main criteria at the desired operating temperature in the range of 1000–1100 °C: (i) CO2 must be able to oxidize one (or both) metal, (ii) CH4 must be able to reduce the formed metal oxide species, and (iii) the metals should not form very stable carbides under the reaction conditions.
Newly emerging materials have also aroused researchers’ interest towards potential implementation in traditional catalytic reactions. For instance, Mxene exhibits the unusual electronic property of the 2D structure and unique properties of transition metal carbides, such as metallic conductivity and hydrophilic properties of the oxygen or hydroxyl terminated surface.300 VC MXene (m-V2CTx) was reported as a precursor for an efficient oxy-carbide catalyst (V2O3 and V8C7) in DRM by Carrero and co-workers (Fig. 42e).299 The in situ redox mechanism is demonstrated, in which the V2O3 nanocrystals generated by the oxidation of CO2 were further transformed into V8C7 nanocrystals by the carbonization of CH4. This catalyst exhibits comparable activity as that of the Ni-based catalyst and more than four-fold better performance compared with the bulk analogues (VC and V2AlC MAX phase). In particular, it lessened the occurrence of side reactions, such as methane cracking, Boudouard, and RWGS, and demonstrated high stability.


3.1.3 Reverse water–gas shift (RWGS) reaction via the thermochemical route. 
The conversion of CO2 into CO through the reverse water gas shift reaction (RWGS, CO2 + H2 → CO + H2O) has been generally considered to be a suitable option since the produced CO is chemically active and represents a valuable intermediate in numerous chemical industrial processes, such as hydroformylation or carbonylation and in the synthesis of methanol, acetic acid, dimethyl ether, hydrocarbons, etc.
In general, two main mechanisms are proposed for the RWGS reaction including RRM and AM. For RRM, hydrogen acts as the reducing agent without being directly involved in the intermediate formation.301 The RRM is based on the fact that CO2 could re-oxidize the partially reduced support, thus resulting in CO formation. In contrast, AM deems that CO arises from the decomposition of formate intermediate, which is derived from the association of H2 with CO2.302 However, other researchers proposed the carbonate mechanism for the RWGS reaction. Cu-based catalysts are most widely used in this catalytic process owing to their excellent activity and selectivity. In addition, metal carbides with noble metal-like properties also stand out in this process, leading to the achievement of a high CO selectivity.303 Moreover, the tuning of properties from other active metals, such as Ni, Co, and Fe, also presents the good performance for the RWGS reaction. For example, Ni is a classical active metal in the methanation process. Reina et al.304 employed the bimetallic Ni–Cu formulation to suppress methanation so as to reach a satisfactory CO selectivity.
Cu remains an efficient active metal for RWGS, especially the Cu–oxide interface, which is recognized as the active place, while Cu NPs tend to agglomerate under working conditions, thus losing a mass of active sites, especially under high temperatures. Malmstadt and Brutchey et al.305 reported the preparation of pure phase α-MoC1−x NPs by a solution-phase synthetic method. After its deposition on carbon, the catalysts showed a two-fold improvement in the activity with respect to the bulk α-MoC1−x counterparts. Since each reaction requires its own characteristic active metal, the modulation of the active site properties can be achieved by fine tuning the product distribution. Rodriguez and co-workers designed γ-Mo2N supported cobalt clusters, in which the charge transfer from Co to Mo2N via the nitride-bonded cobalt led to the stabilization of the Co 3d levels, reaching 100% selectivity for CO.306
With the advanced development of electron microscopy in the last decade, especially with the aid of AC-STEM, the atomic arrangement of complex catalyst systems can be investigated directly to get an in-depth insight towards the relationship between the catalyst structure and the activity.307–310 In particular, in situ HRTEM technology allows the tracing of the real catalyst structure under real reaction conditions. Liu et al.311 reported a NiAu catalyst that displayed selective CO formation in the CO2 hydrogenation reaction, which manifests an intact ultra-thin Au shell that covered the Ni core before and after the reaction by ex situ techniques (Fig. 43). However, the formation of a transient re-constructed alloy surface was discovered by in situ environmental TEM during the reaction, which was re-considered as the real active site, as further proved by other in situ tools including XAS and IR. The discovery of such a reversible transformation urges us to reconsider the nature of the active sites in the dynamic state for core–shell nanoparticles as well as other well-defined nanocatalysts.311
 Fig. 43  
In situ observation of the structural transition of the NiAu NPs during the reaction. (a) In situ TEM imaging of the alloying and dealloying evolution of an individual NiAu particle during the CO2 hydrogenation reaction. Scale bar, 2 nm. (b and c) Surface atom arrangement of the NiAu NPs reconstructed from the full alloy (60 °C) (b) to the Ni@Au core–shell (400 °C) (c); the four parts from left to right in b show the HRTEM image. Scale bars, 2 nm. (d) Intensity profiles from the integration of the diffraction rings of the SAED patterns during the reaction. (e) In situ EXAFS of the Au L3 edge and the coordination number changes (inset) of the Au–Au, Au–Ni, and Ni–Ni pairs at room temperature (r.t.), 400, and 550 °C (Reproduced from ref. 311 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2020). 


3.1.4 CO2 hydrogenation to methanol via the thermochemical route. 
Methanol is known as a key product in the chemical industry, which is used as solvent, fuel additive, or as a fuel for fuel cells. Besides, methanol serves as an indispensable building block for the production of high value-added chemicals such as acetic acid, formaldehyde, anhydrides, methyl esters, and monomers such as methyl methacrylate. Its world demand is continuously expanding, which was roughly 40 million ton in 2007 and is now almost (in 2019) 100 million ton.312 The first methanol industrial plant, started with syngas as the feed mixture, was developed by BASF in 1923. Cu/Zn-based catalysts have been intensively investigated in methanol synthesis from CO2 hydrogenation with Zn as a physical spacer to promote Cu dispersion during the preparation process. Whilst the debate for ‘which species acts as the real active sites’ still remains, it has been suggested that the metallic copper surface, the Cu–ZnOx interface, or the CuZn alloys may catalyze the reaction.
Based on the investigation on the nature of active sites of industrial methanol catalysts, Behrens et al.313 pointed out that the intrinsic activity presented a linear relationship with the stacking fault probability and highlighted that the defects are of paramount importance in affecting the activity (Fig. 44). The partial coverage of the metal particles by ZnOx under a reductive atmosphere was also demonstrated by in situ XPS experiments (Fig. 44f).314 With the development of operando advanced techniques, the precise location and visualization of active sites can be achieved. For example, the graphite-like ZnO metastable layers structure can be detected via Cs-corrected TEM on the commercial Cu/ZnO/Al2O3 catalyst during the reductive activation process. Zn was also indicated to be in the ZnxO form rather than in the ZnO from under the reaction conditions according to DFT calculations, microcalorimetry, and FTIR experiments.315 The Cu–ZnO interface was further demonstrated to be the active site in the CO2 hydrogenation reaction by Rodriguez and Chen.316 XPS, kinetic Monte Carlo simulations, and DFT calculation results suggested that the surface oxidation of the ZnCu alloy model catalyst during the reaction induced the evolvement of the surface Zn species into ZnO. Except for Cu and ZnO, other structural components such as Al2O3 or ZrO2 have been introduced to achieve better dispersion.317 Based on the kinetic studies, Roger et al.317 observed that Cu/ZnO/ZrO2 exhibited high methanol selectivity during CO2 hydrogenation, thus effectively limiting the occurrence of RWGS.
 Fig. 44  (a–d) Aberration-corrected HRTEM images of the Cu particles in the conventionally prepared, most-active Cu/ZnO/Al2O3 catalyst, (d) is a close-up of the marked area in (c). (e) Relation of the intrinsic activity of Cu to the concentration of stacking faults (Reproduced from ref. 313 with permission from American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2012). (f) Relative measured methanol exit concentrations at 130 °C (temperature ramp-down) as a function of the post-reaction values of θZn. Varying values of θZn are obtained by pretreatments with H2 at different pressures and temperatures prior to the activity tests. The dashed line is a second-order polynomial fit (Reproduced from ref. 314 with permission from American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2016). 
Though Cu–ZnO catalysts have been utilized as industrial catalysts for methanol synthesis, the methanol selectivity remains limited owing to the presence of the competitive RWGS reaction. Efforts have been devoted to novel catalyst design with high methanol selectivity. For instance, In2O3 has been investigated for methanol synthesis owing to its oxygen vacancies that may favor CO2 dissociation. Pérez-Ramírez et al.318 tested ZrO2-supported In2O3 for the CO2 hydrogenation reaction and achieved methanol selectivity of 100% and excellent stability of 1000 h. The high methanol selectivity is attributed to the lower apparent activation energy for CO2 hydrogenation to methanol than that for the competitive RWGS reaction (103 vs. 117 kJ.mol−1), as disclosed for the In-based catalyst. Furthermore, DFT calculations pointed out that the oxygen vacancies surrounded by three In atoms prompt CO2 activation and hydrogen splitting heterolytically. Afterwards, Pd, an efficient H2-splitter, was introduced as a promoter to boost the catalytic performance without losing the high methanol selectivity by the controlled co-precipitation method, which displayed good stream stability of over 500 h in the methanol synthesis.319
Apart from the aforementioned catalytic systems, cobalt-based catalysts have also been explored in the CO2 hydrogenation reaction. Cobalt is renowned in Fischer–Tropsch synthesis owing to its good C–O and H–H dissociation as well as the C–C coupling ability to produce hydrocarbons. When CO2 is employed as a feedstock, methane is formed. However, on changing the microstructure property of the active phase, methanol can be obtained as a prominent product. Zeng et al.320 attempted the incorporation of N into Co nanosheets through NH3 treatment, which shifted the selectivity towards the products from methane to methanol, along with a 64-fold increase in the TOF, compared with the pristine nanosheets. Co4N was regarded as the active phase with Co4NHx formed in the presence of H2. The amido-hydrogen atoms in the Co4N nanosheets directly interacted with CO2 to yield HCOO* intermediates. Wang et al.321 designed the Co@Six catalyst, in which silica acts as a support and a ligand, modifying the nature of the cobalt species via Co–O–SiOn linkages with Co0 and Co2+ both existing in the catalyst. On metallic cobalt, *CHx tends to form according to its excellent H–H and C–O bond dissociation ability and *COOH was generated over Co2+ with a high formation rate.322,323 Hence, controlling the appropriate Co0/Co2+ ratio remains the key point for obtaining a proper balance between the methanol selectivity and catalyst activity.


3.1.5 CO2 hydrogenation to hydrocarbons via the thermochemical route. 
Hydrocarbons are key building blocks from bulk chemistry, mainly including light (C2–C4) olefins, aromatics, as well as liquid alkanes and alkenes. The latter liquids act as fuels, including gasoline (C5–C11), jet fuels (C8–C16), and diesel (C10–C20).281 In the past decades, hydrocarbons have been produced by the well-known Fischer–Tropsch synthesis catalyzed by Fe, Co, and Ru from syngas.307,324 The metallic nanoparticle surface provides the right environment and good affinity for CO and H2 dissociation as well as C–C chain growth. The selectivity control, however, remains a big challenge since the product distribution usually follows the ASF rule.
Recently, the mixture of metal oxides and zeolites has been evaluated as a bifunctional catalyst, which attracted considerable attention due to its distinct characteristics of violating the ASF distribution, which has been a challenge for decades. Hence, an oxide-zeolite catalytic system was developed, aiming to overcome the hydrocarbon selectivity restrictions found in the conventional Fischer–Tropsch synthesis. Bao et al.325 designed the ZnCrOx/MSAPO catalyst with a ZnCrOx phase to activate the CO and H2 molecules, whilst the acid sites (from MSAPO) performed the C–C coupling, yielding a selectivity for C2–C4 olefins as high as 80%. C2–C4 olefins with a selectivity of 70% were obtained over the Zr–Zn/SAPO-34 hybrid catalyst by Wang et al.326
In contrast, when CO2 is used as the carbon source, the activation follows two different paths, i.e., (i) CO2 was transformed to CO via the RWGS reaction and then CO was converted into hydrocarbons, following the ASF distribution. Sun et al.327 directly converted CO2 into gasoline over a multi-functional catalyst involving three kinds of active sites (i.e., Fe3O4, Fe5C2, and acid sites), which cooperatively promoted a tandem reaction (Fig. 45a). During this reaction, CO2 was firstly reduced to CO through RWGS on the Fe3O4 sites and CO was subsequently converted into α-olefins on the Fe5C2 sites through FT synthesis. Finally, the olefin secondary products diffused towards the zeolite acid sites, undergoing classical acid-catalyzed processes (i.e., oligomerization, isomerization, and aromatization); (ii) the second path consists of CO2 hydrogenation to methanol or DME, followed by C–C coupling reactions catalyzed by Brønsted acid sites. Zhong et al.328 developed an In2O3/HZSM-5 bifunctional catalyst for CO2 hydrogenation to yield a high selectivity of 79% towards gasoline range hydrocarbons (Fig. 45b–e). CO2 and H2 were activated on the oxygen vacancies of In2O3, forming methanol and subsequent C–C coupling reactions within the zeolite pores. Several metal oxides, including Zn–Cr oxide,329 Cu–Zn–Zr,329 Fe–Zn–Zr, ZnO–ZrO2,326 In2O3–ZrO2,330 ZnGa2O4, Cr2O3, and ZnAlOx,331 have been investigated for CO2 conversion into methanol/DME with the bifunctional catalyst. Amongst all these oxide–zeolite catalytic systems, the distance between the oxide and the zeolite particles remains a crucial factor that strongly influences the targeted product selectivity. For instance, intimate contact between the two functions may lead to the poisoning of the Brønsted acid sites, thus resulting in CH4 formation as the main product.
 Fig. 45  (a) Reaction scheme for CO2 hydrogenation towards gasoline-range hydrocarbons. The CO2 hydrogenation reaction over the Na–Fe3O4/zeolite multi-functional catalyst takes place in three steps: (1) the initial reduction to CO via RWGS, (2) the subsequent hydrogenation of CO to α-olefins intermediate via FT, and (3) the formation of gasoline-range hydrocarbons via acid-catalyzed oligomerization, isomerization, and aromatization reactions (Reproduced from ref. 327 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). (b) Influence of the integration manner of the active components (In2O3/HZSM-5 mass ratio = 2:1) on the catalytic behavior under the same conditions. (c) Schematic of the hydrocarbon-pool mechanism for CH3OH conversion into hydrocarbons inside HZSM-5. (d) Schematic for the formation of CH3OH from CO2 at the oxygen vacancy sites on the In2O3 catalyst surface, which involves four major steps: (1) CO2 adsorption at the oxygen vacancy sites, (2) sequential hydrogenation of the adsorbed CO2 species to CH3OH, (3) CH3OH desorption, which leads to a surface without oxygen vacancies, and (4) hydrogenation of the surface to regenerate the oxygen vacancy sites. (e) Schematic for hydrocarbon formation from CH3OH at the acidic site inside the pores of the HZSM-5 catalyst via the hydrocarbon-pool mechanism (Reproduced from ref. 328 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). 

3.2. Photochemical conversion of CO2
Photochemical CO2 conversion is viewed as a clean and environment-friendly technology, utilizing one of the most viable and widely abundant renewable energy sources, i.e., solar energy. With the constantly increasing concentration of CO2 in the atmosphere, directly converting the energy from the sun into chemical fuels (e.g., CH4, C2H6, CH3OH, C2H5OH, and other longer chain hydrocarbons) using CO2 as the feedstock has gained significant momentum.332,333 In addition, the advantage of photochemical CO2 conversion driven by the inexhaustible solar energy source is complemented by the simplicity and cost-effectiveness of a photocatalytic array, thus encouraging the further development of this technology. Given that the reduction of CO2 by water to form chemical fuels is a highly endothermic process (+802 and +726 kJ mol−1 for methane and methanol, respectively),334 these fuels can be stored and used upon demand, overcoming the intermittency of sunlight while generating electricity. In diverging from the use of fossil fuel resources, this approach could contribute to creating a new CO2 economy, based on the realization of sustainable CO2 conversion systems.

3.2.1 Basic principles of photocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
Unlike thermal CO2 conversion, the electrons that participate in the photochemical CO2 reduction reaction originate from the photoexcitation of a photo-absorber (commonly known as a photo-catalyst). During photo-catalysis, CO2 reduction occurs in concert with the oxidation reaction, ideally, water oxidation in a vapor-based or aqueous-phase environment (a hole scavenger is often used in place of water to improve the overall performance).335 The reduction and oxidation reactions are triggered by photo-generated electrons and holes from the conduction and valence bands of the photo-catalyst, respectively. As illustrated in Fig. 46a, the charge kinetics during photocatalytic CO2 reduction comprise three key steps: (1) charge generation upon illumination, promoting the excitation of electrons and holes to the conduction and valence bands of the material, respectively; (2) charge separation/migration from the bulk to the surface of the material; and (3) charge consumption by redox reactions on the surface of the photo-catalyst to invoke CO2 reduction and water oxidation.336
 Fig. 46  (a) Schematic illustration of three key charge kinetic steps during the photochemical conversion of CO2 and (b) valence and conduction band potentials of selected common photo-catalysts in relation to CO2 reduction, H2 reduction, and water oxidation potentials. 
The rate of photocatalytic CO2 conversion strongly depends on the charge dynamics of the photo-catalyst. In particular, the amount of charge generated in Step 1 relies on the light intensity and the band energy/HOMO–LUMO levels of the photo-absorber. Considering the fact that the solar radiation spectrum comprises of approximately 43% visible light, selecting a visible-light activated CO2 reduction photo-catalyst with a bandgap energy smaller than 3.0 eV is crucial. Charge excitation within the bulk photo-catalyst is followed by migration of the charge (Step 2) to the photo-catalyst surface. Charge migration is often hindered by intrinsic surface and bulk charge recombination, particularly in the presence of defects or impurities that act as charge trapping and recombination centers.337,338 Various strategies have been employed to improve the overall charge separation efficiency (by suppressing the charge recombination rate), such as metal deposition, heterojunction formation, and the integration of carbon-based materials.339,340 Improved charge separation can then facilitate efficient charge consumption (Step 3) by CO2 and water on the catalyst surface. The conduction and valence band potentials play a critical role in regulating the potential reaction pathways. For instance, selected visible-light active photo-catalysts (e.g., Fe2O3 and WO3) are unable to produce chemical fuels from CO2 due to the less energetic conduction band potential relative to the CO2 reduction potential (Fig. 46b).341 In contrast, TiO2, Cu2O, and C3N4 have been regularly applied to the photocatalytic CO2 reduction reaction due to their suitable conduction band potential.
The slow kinetics of the water oxidation reaction (similar to that experienced during photocatalytic water splitting342) deploying a hole sacrificial agent as the electron donor has gained favor. As a consequence, the current focus has been primarily directed towards investigating the CO2 reduction half reaction. From the viewpoint of artificial photosynthesis, the ideal photocatalytic CO2 reduction reaction should accompany O2 evolution via water oxidation.343 However, H2 evolution (via proton reduction) is more favorable than CO2 reduction in the presence of H2O as the reactant because of the need for multi-electron transfer during CO2 reduction. Utilizing H2O as the electron donor for O2 evolution as well as suppressing the H2 evolution therefore represents one of the most critical issues in photocatalytic CO2 reduction, and considerable research effort has been devoted for constructing photocatalytic systems towards efficient CO2 conversion together with water oxidation.344–346
Another important issue in photocatalytic CO2 reduction is the product selectivity. There exist various reaction pathways that can generate a selection of products. The most common products are CO, CH4, and CH3OH, although the capacity to photo-chemically convert CO2 into C2-based solar fuels such as C2H4, C2H6, and C2H5OH has also been demonstrated. The formaldehyde, carbene, and glyoxal reaction pathways are most favored for C1 product generation. The formaldehyde and carbene pathways are illustrated in Fig. 47 and the multi-electron nature of the reactions is highlighted as mentioned above (i.e., the production of CO and HCOOH required 2e−, HCHO needs 4e−, CH3OH and CH4 need 6e− and 8e−, respectively), irrespective of the reaction pathways. A combination of these pathways in conjunction with dimerization facilitates the formation of C2 hydrocarbons.347,348 Simultaneous H+ ion production from the oxidation half reaction is also essential for producing the methane, aldehyde, and alcohol species. However, the complexities of photocatalytic CO2 reduction still require further unravelling, where deeper understanding and knowledge of the processes will benefit both the activity and selectivity of the system.
 Fig. 47  Potential carbene and formaldehyde pathways of photocatalytic CO2 reduction to CH4 on the surface of a photo-catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 348 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2020). 


3.2.2 Progression and current challenges. 
Some of the earliest publications on photochemical CO2 conversion involved catalysis by a metal complex in a homogeneous system, including W2(CO)102− complexes, [Ru(bpy)2(CO)2]2+ complexes, and nickel(II) complexes, which were then followed by the development of Re-, Fe-, and other transition metal complexes.349,350 Although using metal complexes is known to invoke high selectivity and high quantum yield for CO production, metal complexes are typically expensive and difficult to synthesize, especially at a larger scale. In addition, the potential re-oxidation of the products by the metal complexes means that the production of methane or longer chain hydrocarbons in a homogeneous photocatalytic CO2 conversion system is challenging.
The research on heterogeneous photocatalytic CO2 reduction in a liquid–solid system was first reported by Inoue et al.351 in 1979. Here, TiO2 was shown to deliver the best CO2 reduction performance amongst the assessed photo-catalysts (i.e., TiO2, ZnO, CdS, GaP, SiC, and WO3). Over the past four decades, significant effort has been devoted to establishing effective gas and liquid phase heterogeneous photocatalytic CO2 reduction systems. Gas phase systems offer the distinct advantage of reduction in the competition between CO2 reduction and H2 evolution reactions. The saturated CO2 gas existing in the system only needs to compete with a comparatively small number of protons from the introduced water vapor. The advantage of a liquid phase system, where the photo-catalyst is suspended in a CO2-saturated solution, is the greater exposure of the catalyst surface to incident light.
Despite the apparent benefits of heterogeneous photocatalytic CO2 reduction systems, a significantly low yield and poor selectivity are the key barriers that hinder the effective uptake of the approach. Designing a photo-catalyst that is visible-light active while simultaneously possessing the desired properties for selectively reducing CO2 into the desired chemical fuels continues to be a challenge. In principle, in relation to the basic charge kinetics highlighted in Fig. 46a, an ideal photo-catalyst should possess: (1) a relatively small bandgap to effectively harness solar energy; (2) a crystal structure that promotes high intrinsic carrier density, charge mobility, and a long charge lifetime; and (3) conduction/valence band potentials and surface properties that drive the reaction towards a preferable reaction pathway. The greatest challenge that remains in photocatalytic CO2 reduction research is the scarcity of highly active, stable, cheap, and abundant photo-catalysts that meet all these criteria. The relatively low product yield from photocatalytic CO2 reduction (especially for longer-chain ‘C2/C3’ carbon products, which are typically produced in the nmol h−1 g−1 range) has led to the debate that whether the products are generated from CO2 or other surface impurities.352 The presence of carbon residues on a photo-catalyst surface (particularly alkoxides, which may have been used as precursors for catalyst preparation) have shown to contribute to the carbon product yield via a reverse Boudouard reaction.353 Using carbon-based photo-catalysts (e.g., g-C3N4, graphene, reduced graphene oxide, or MOFs) will further compromise the reliability of the results if proper control experiments (e.g., in the absence of CO2 or in the presence of 13C-labelled CO2) are not performed.
Rapid developments in photochemical CO2 conversion have seen significant improvements in solar fuel (i.e., CH4) production rates of up to 2.2 mmol h−1 g−1 (∼97% selectivity towards methane production) using the Au/Cu-deposited TiO2 photo-catalyst.354 In comparison, the highest methane production rate was less than 10 μmol h−1 g−1 a decade ago.347 Despite the material advances, the low solar fuel production level continues to be a significant issue from a practical standpoint. Other aspects that require consideration, including the smaller band gap of the visible-light active photo-catalyst, is often at the expense of the conduction band energy/potential, which may in turn not meet the high driving force required to reduce CO2. Furthermore, loading a noble metal on the photo-catalyst to promote CO2 reduction might increase the cost to a point where large-scale production is unfeasible. In terms of the selectivity, a mixture of chemical fuels in the product stream due to the lack of control over the reaction pathways necessitates a complicated separation process, which is also technically impractical. Finally, photo-stability issues that have been frequently reported in other photocatalytic studies (particularly for copper-based or sulfide semiconductors) should not be neglected.337,355


3.2.3 Strategies to improve the performance. 
The above-mentioned challenges define the research domains that require advanced strategies to deliver improved overall CO2 reduction performance. The well-established photo-catalyst, TiO2, continues to be the most commonly studied material for photochemical CO2 conversion, despite a wide band gap (∼3.2 eV) that only permits the absorption of UV light. High photo-corrosion resistance, non-toxicity, low cost, and abundance are the primary reasons that make TiO2 favorable. Along with the discovery of other photo-catalysts (e.g., Cu2O, C3N4, and photoactive perovskite materials) that are suitable for photochemical CO2 conversion, a range of strategies have been developed to enhance the photo-activity and selectivity, including doping, integration of secondary components (i.e., co-catalyst deposition, heterojunction formation, incorporation of carbon-based materials, and photosensitization), morphology or crystal facet tuning, as well as defect engineering. Fig. 48 summarizes the commonly-employed material design strategies that have been adopted to improve photochemical CO2 conversion. Each strategy introduces one or more ideal characteristics (e.g., good light absorption capacity, high charge separation efficiency, and enhanced surface properties) into the photo-catalyst, which promotes photochemical CO2 conversion. This section will build on the review articles on photocatalytic CO2 reduction published over the past few years by presenting a clear and concise evaluation of the latest progress in the above-mentioned techniques.347,356,357 Note that only reliable literature that performed appropriate control experiments proving the generation of solar fuels from CO2 instead of other carbon sources is included here.
 Fig. 48  Strategies to advance photocatalytic CO2 reduction performance by improving the light absorption ability, charge separation efficiency, and surface reactions. 
Metal and non-metal dopants can induce different conduction or valence band energy levels within a semiconductor, thus narrowing the band gap for visible light excitation. This strategy has been widely employed in various photocatalytic or photo-electro-catalytic applications, such as water splitting, organic degradation, photo-reforming, and CO2 conversion. Given that doping introduces distortion within the crystal structure and commonly increases the charge carrier density (at a low dopant concentration), the intrinsic charge separation/migration efficiency will also be affected. Metals such as Ag, Cu, Fe, Pt, and Au have been doped into the TiO2 lattice to reduce the band gap, making it visible-light responsive for photocatalytic CO2 reduction. For non-metal doping such as nitrogen, the introduced N 2p states often form a mid-gap level that reduces the band gap of the semiconductor and allows visible light absorption. For example, (g-C3N4)–N–TiO2 has displayed improved CO2 conversion to CO,358 while P-doped C3N4 also exhibited enhanced photo-activity and selectivity towards CO2 reduction due to better light absorption capacity and CO2 adsorption ability.
Co-catalyst loading and heterojunction formation (particularly type II heterojunction and direct Z-scheme systems) are known to be the most effective and widely implemented strategies for improving photocatalytic CO2 reduction.339,357,359 As illustrated in Fig. 48, these techniques enhance all three of the key photo-catalyst aspects and can potentially enable the realization of a highly active and selective photo-catalyst. In addition, there exist many simple and effective synthetic methods to produce co-catalyst-loaded or heterojunction-based photo-catalysts, such as photo-deposition, impregnation, and one-pot hydrothermal method. Integrating a plasmonic co-catalyst (e.g., Ag, Au, and Cu) or a narrow-bandgap photo-catalyst with the primary photo-catalyst material are well-established strategies to deliver visible-light active photo-catalyst arrays for photochemical CO2 reduction.360,361 These secondary components facilitate charge separation to suppress charge recombination and also change the surface active sites, thereby improving the surface reaction and potentially tuning the product selectivity. For instance, Neatų et al.354 achieved a high level of methane production using an Au/Cu alloy on the surface of the TiO2 photo-catalyst, demonstrating the important role each component played in improving the overall performance (Fig. 49a). The enhanced CO2 reduction capability was attributed to the plasmonic properties of Au, which facilitated visible light absorption, while CO adsorption was preferred on Cu. A reaction mechanism explaining the highly selective CH4 production was proposed (Fig. 4b), whereby CO2 was first reduced to CO and then CH4 in the presence of photo-generated electrons and H+ ions, following the carbene pathway.
 Fig. 49  (a) The TEM image and (b) the proposed rationale based on the influence of irradiation wavelength range on the product distribution by the Au/Cu alloy deposits on the TiO2 photo-catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 354 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2014). (c) Comparison of the photocatalytic production of CH4 using CdS, TiO2, and direct Z-scheme TiO2/CdS system. (d) Schematic illustration of the charge migration mechanism according to type II heterojunction and direct Z-scheme of TiO2/CdS (Reproduced from ref. 362 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2018). (e) Production rate during photocatalytic CO2 conversion for different ratio of g-C3N4/Bi4O5I2 under visible light illumination (Reproduced from ref. 363 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2016). (f) Production rate during photocatalytic CO2 conversion for different ratios of Bi2WO6/RGO/g-C3N4 under visible light irradiation (Reproduced from ref. 364 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
The Z-scheme approach has attracted considerable attention for photocatalytic CO2 reduction. The combination of two narrow-bandgap semiconductors with suitable conduction and valence band potentials can effectively enhance visible light utilization, and the Z-scheme configurations promote charge separation and accomplish CO2 conversion together with water oxidation.357 There exist two discrete Z-scheme photocatalytic systems (direct and indirect) with the difference defined by the absence/presence of an electron mediator. While direct Z-scheme charge transfer occurs in the absence of an electron mediator (the two photo-catalysts are in direct contact), it needs to be clearly distinguished from a type II heterojunction. Recently, Low et al.362 systematically differentiated direct Z-scheme charge migration within TiO2/CdS from a typical type II heterojunction using in situ XPS so as to probe the charge transfer pathway upon illumination, as illustrated in Fig. 49c and d. A Z-scheme SiC@MoS2 structure achieved the overall conversion of gas phase CO2 and H2O into CH4 and O2 with an apparent quantum efficiency of 1.75% at 400 nm.346 In the case of an in-direct Z-scheme system, a liquid-phase redox couple (e.g., I3−/I− or Fe2+/Fe3+) or a solid-phase conducive material (e.g., graphene or reduced graphene oxide) are employed as an electron mediator. The Z-scheme structure of g-C3N4/Bi4O5I2 in a suspension containing I3−/I−,363 the combination of (CuGa)1−xZn2xS2 and BiVO4 in a [Co(tpy)2]3+/2+ redox couple solution, Bi2WO6/RGO/g-C3N4,364 and CuGaS2/RGO/BiVO4365 have shown to successfully convert CO2 into CO and CH4 using H2O as an electron donor (Fig. 49e and f). The simultaneous deposition of a co-catalyst on the heterojunction photo-catalyst also provided favorable results. A recent study employing the g-C3N4/Au–TiO2 Z-scheme system displayed promising photo-activity and selectivity towards CO2 reduction under visible light illumination.366 Overall, the selection of an appropriate co-catalyst and semiconductor combinations is crucial for imparting high efficiency and selectivity during photochemical CO2 reduction.
There are intensive studies that have examined the potential of graphitic carbon nitride (g-C3N4) for photocatalytic CO2 conversion.367 This emerging metal-free photo-catalyst shows the benefits of being visible-light active, photostable, and possesses a very energetic conduction band that permits the conversion of CO2 into various chemical fuels. The effective tuning of the product selectivity has also been demonstrated by computational DFT calculations, where Pd/g-C3N4 was shown to preferentially convert CO2 into HCOOH, while Pt/g-C3N4 produced more CH4.368 Given that g-C3N4 is a carbon-based semiconductor, extra precautions and comprehensive control experiments confirming the origin of the products should not be neglected. With the exception of g-C3N4, integrating secondary carbon-based materials on a photo-catalyst has emerged as a popular strategy due to the extensive possibilities for improving the charge transfer efficiency and surface adsorption of CO2. The possible contribution of carbon products originating from the integrated carbon species needs to be carefully evaluated. In most cases, graphene or reduced graphene oxide was employed369 due to their high conductivity and flexibility with morphology control, which leads to improved photo-activity.370 Recently, the development of MOFs as photo-catalysts or to fabricate photo-catalyst composites has attracted wide interest in photo-catalysis, predominantly for application in photocatalytic CO2 reduction. Sophisticated MOF structures could be rationally designed and finely tuned to suit the specific reaction pathway for enhanced photo-activity and selectivity. For instance, the construction of a modified ZIF on TiO2/carbon displayed almost complete selectivity (99%) towards CO production.371 Although implementing MOFs in photochemical CO2 conversion remains limited by the photo-stability concerns (where unstable MOFs will result in misinterpretation or overestimation of products formed) and high material costs, a wide range of interesting properties derived from their structural controllability is yet to be thoroughly explored.372
Over the past decade, faceted photo-catalysts have shown to provide excellent performance towards photocatalytic CO2 reduction. Facet control imbues improved charge transfer efficiency and reactant adsorption ability, which is attributed to the preferred surface electronic and atomic structures, respectively.356 The CO2 adsorption capacity and geometry on the photo-catalyst surface are crucial in determining the conversion efficiency and selectivity. Different exposed facets and the associated surface atomic structure of TiO2 can significantly influence the CO2 adsorption geometry. Possible surface arrangements of the CO2 molecule on TiO2 are illustrated in Fig. 50a.373 Although CO2 adsorption is the initial critical step in the reduction process, the simultaneous adsorption of CO2 and water is also important when converting CO2 into chemical fuels. For example, (010) anatase TiO2 has been reported as an active facet for CO2 reduction (Fig. 50b), despite it being thermodynamically less stable than the (101) facet. This derives from the more energetic conduction band (Fig. 50c) and exposed Ti atoms on the high energy (010) facet, which improve the adsorption of CO2 and water.374 Ohno et al.375 also demonstrated that brookite TiO2 with a high aspect ratio of the (210) to (212) facets (Fig. 50d) produced a greater amount of CH3OH from photocatalytic CO2 reduction, as illustrated in Fig. 50e.
 Fig. 50  (a) Possible surface structures of CO2 adsorbed on TiO2 (Reproduced from ref. 373 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2014). (b) SEM images of microsized anatase rods with a dominant (010) facet, with the inset illustrating the facet arrangement. (c) Schematic electronic band structure of anatase rods with a dominant (010) facet.374 (d) Brookite TiO2 nanorods prepared in the absence of a polymer. (e) CH3OH production rate of several kinds of brookite TiO2 (Reproduced from ref. 375 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2014). 
Likewise, defect engineering is a surface modification technique that can enhance charge transfer in a photo-catalyst. By prolonging the charge lifetime and providing relevant surface-active sites for the reaction, defect engineering has been implemented to improve the photocatalytic CO2 reduction reaction.376 A predominance of research studies on oxide-based semiconductors have reported that surface oxygen vacancies (Ov) with low coordinative sites are beneficial for promoting charge separation, charge transfer, and the CO2 adsorption capacity. As demonstrated by Yin et al.,377 the presence of localized Ti3+ defects on a blue TiO2 photo-catalyst (Fig. 51a–d) both improved the charge migration to the surface for the CO2 reduction reaction and acted as surface active sites to promote CO2 adsorption and activation so as to provide high selectivity towards CH4 (see the proposed mechanism in Fig. 51e). If the defect layer was too thick (Fig. 51d), the resultant high defect concentration would impede the charge transfer, leading to a lower photo-activity. Recently, bismuth-based photo-catalysts (i.e., Bi12O17Cl2 and BiOI3)378,379 have illustrated the beneficial role of surface oxygen vacancies in accelerating charge transfer during photocatalytic CO2 reduction. A higher oxygen vacancy concentration in the Bi12O17Cl2 nanotubes when compared to bulk Bi12O17Cl2 accounted for the accelerated CO production rate.378 Similarly, oxygen defects introduced into BiOI3 nanostrips boosted the CO production rate.379 Defect formation could also be observed from the carbon-based material, such as g-C3N4. Akin to oxygen removal from an oxide material, g-C3N4 with nitrogen vacancies exhibited an approximately eight times higher CO2 conversion to CO when compared to that of pristine g-C3N4.380
 Fig. 51  (a–d) HRTEM images of pristine TiO2 and blue TiO2 with different thicknesses of the Ti3+ defect layer. The amorphous shell is illustrated by the dashed line and arrows. (e) Proposed reaction pathways for CO2 photo-reduction in the presence of H2O by the blue TiO2 photo-catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 377 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). 


3.2.4 Perspective and outlook for photocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
In the context of utilizing renewable resources, the photocatalytic conversion of CO2 into value-added chemical fuels using solar energy is very appealing. Photochemical conversion customarily occurs close to the ambient temperature and pressure, which renders it less energy consuming than other approaches. Given that a heterogeneous system is more promising in terms of future scale-up (including the system with a combination of complexes on a heterogeneous photo-catalyst), various strategies have been established to overcome the foremost challenges in designing photo-catalysts that possess both high thermodynamic and kinetic potentials toward CO2 reduction. An ideal photo-catalyst for photochemical CO2 conversion should possess selected key features. First, a narrow band gap that allows the harnessing of a larger portion of the solar spectrum. Second, good intrinsic charge properties that suppress charge recombination and promote charge migration. Finally, it should possess desirable surface properties that drive selected surface reactions. These include appropriate band potentials that minimize competition with the water reduction reaction (H2 evolution reaction) and good adsorption ability towards CO2 molecules. While a combination of the above-mentioned strategies has demonstrated the possibility to boost the overall conversion of CO2 and H2O upon illumination, it is also important to consider the product selectivity. The potential for the formation of a wide range of products (CO, CH4, C2H4, CH3OH, and C2H5OH) and a corresponding lack of understanding of the reaction mechanisms and pathways has yielded little control over its product selectivity. Consequently, the factors that regulate the reaction pathways and define product distributions from photochemical CO2 conversion are yet to be convincingly identified. Despite the comparatively low efficiency in relation to other approaches, there continues to be a clear interest in exploring and establishing promising photo-catalysts for enhanced CO2 photo-conversion. The current low yields of solar fuels from photocatalytic CO2 reduction are impractical in its present state. Thorough mechanistic studies, both experimental and first principles-based to unearth new photo-catalysts and provide a greater fundamental insight in effectively capturing the broad solar energy spectrum, are crucial. In fact, such new knowledge could be further extended into other catalysis modes, such as photo-thermal catalysis (as detailed in the ensuing section), to synergistically harness both light and heat from the sun.

3.3. Electrochemical conversion of CO2
Electrochemical CO2 reduction into fuels and chemicals provides an important way for a carbon neutral cycle and renewable energy storage and utilization. The activity and selectivity towards specific products via electrochemical CO2 reduction has proved to be dependent on selected electro-catalysts and the conditions of reactions. In the past few decades, researchers have made great efforts to develop novel heterogeneous and homogeneous electro-catalysts. Considering that typical homogeneous catalysts are difficult to be separated from the product mixtures, which hampers large-scale applications, herein, we mainly focus on the recent progresses in heterogeneous catalyst development. They have achieved high selectivity towards different products, especially for the 2e− transfer products (CO and HCOOH), the FE of which has reached nearly 100% today.381,382 However, the current densities regarding the CO2RR activity have always been lower than 40 mA cm−2 because of low CO2 solubility in aqueous solution (∼0.33 mol L−1 at 25 °C, 1 atm). Recently, numerous studies have focused on the large-scale production of various products for further industrial applications, aimed to achieve long-term reaction (>20000 hours) and large current density (>200 mA cm−2) in the future.383–385 The obstacle in technology development mainly includes two aspects: one is the catalytic property (over-potential, product selectivity, activity, and stability), the other is the construction of infrastructure (combination with CO2 capture, resistance in the cell, product separation, etc.). Herein, we have mainly summarized the outstanding works in the field of electrochemical CO2 reduction that focus on low-cost materials with prospects for the large-scale production of various products in the past three years.

3.3.1 Basic principle of electro-catalytic CO2 reduction. 
CO2RR involves a multiple-electron transfer process including 2e−, 4e−, 6e−, 8e−, 12e−, or even more electrons pathways to form a variety of products. In aqueous solution, a competitive reaction HER also occurs to form the H2 by-product. As shown in Table 4, the equilibrium potentials (vs. SHE) for the reduction half-reactions of CO2-to-various products in aqueous solution (pH = 7.0) has been summarized.386 Although some product formation pathways seem to be thermodynamically more favorable than HER, the kinetics of CO2RR is quite slow because of the standard redox potential of the intermediate radical CO2˙− species formation at about −1.9 V (Fig. 52).387 Subsequently, this intermediate is further reduced to ˙COOH or HCOO˙ through the protonation of its oxygen atom or carbon atom to produce CO and formate, respectively. These two transfer processes only involve two electrons; thus, CO and formate are mostly produced as the main products of CO2RR. In addition, CO can be further reduced to a series of different hydrocarbons through hydrogenation, C–O bond breakage, and C–C bond coupling.388,389

3.3.2 Recent progress in formic acid/formate selective formation. 
Formic acid is a latent liquid fuel and a common organic chemical material, which is easy to store and transport. The most widely studied catalysts for formic acid production are Sn, Bi, and In instead of other heavy metals due to their non-toxicity, environment-friendly properties, and good performance, which show great potential in further commercial applications.
3.3.2.1 Sn-Based catalysts. 
Sn-Based catalysts have been widely used for formate formation in the past few decades due to their excellent selectivity towards HCOOH and low cost. Previous work reported by Hori group391 indicated that the bulk Sn electrode was able to selectively convert CO2 to formate with a relatively large over-potential and low current density. Oxide-derived metals have gained great attention in electrolysis in recent years, especially for characteristic metals that are synthesized via in situ or ex situ reduction from the oxidized metal architecture. The morphology and/or chemical composition of the metal surfaces could be changed from the bulk form of the metals after the oxidation–reduction steps, thereby influencing the electro-catalytic performances.392–394 SnO2 has been typically prepared via different methods and then reduced to Sn0 with residual surface oxide, which could create more active sites for CO2RR. For example, SnO2 porous nanowire reduced by an electrochemical method was reported to reach a high HCOOH FE of up to 80% under an applied potential of −0.8 V vs. RHE due to the high density of the grain boundaries within the porous structure, as compared to that of commercial SnO2 nanoparticles.395 Moreover, Amal and her co-workers396 proposed a convenient and scalable strategy to develop efficient Sn-based electro-catalysts for CO2RR by manipulating defects generation. They applied an industrially adopted FSP technique to synthesize SnO2 nanoparticles for electrochemical CO2 reduction to formate. According to ex situ and in situ characterizations, the surface defects containing oxygen vacancies and oxygen hole centers in the obtained FSP-SnO2 catalysts could be altered by tuning the flame synthesis conditions, which play an important role in CO2 activation. FSP-SnO2-5 exhibited better performance for formate production with higher current density and selectivity compared to the other two catalysts, which could be attributed to a higher amount of defects, achieving a HCOO− FE up to 85% as well as a high total current density of −23.7 mA cm−2 at −1.1 V.
Apart from oxide-derived catalysts, sulfide-derived catalysts have also been intensively applied for efficient CO2 reduction. Li and co-workers397 reported a 2D SnS2/rGO composite for CO2-to-formic acid conversion, for which the highest selectivity of up to 84.5% along with a total current density of ∼14 mA cm−2 was obtained at −0.68 V. According to the characterization results, metallic Sn derived from SnS2 was considered to be the active site and the remaining SnS2 was found to improve the catalytic activity. Moreover, a sulfur-modulated tin (Sn(S)) catalyst on Au was prepared for formate formation, which was reduced from the atomic layer deposition of SnSx on Au needles.398 The detailed synthetic process is illustrated in Fig. 53a, suggesting that Sn(S) films were formed via the selective reduction of the SnSx slab on Au needles. XANES analysis illustrated in Fig. 53b indicated that the tin oxidation state in the Sn(S) catalyst was higher than that in the Sn NPs before and after reaction; thus, Sn(S) might have more under-coordinated Sn centers than Sn NPs. As illustrated in Fig. 53c, sulfur-modulated tin on Au needles (Sn(S)/Au) achieved a high total current density of 55 mA cm−2 at −0.75 V and the HCOOH FE was up to 93%, suggesting superior efficiency for HCOOH formation compared to the pure Sn NPs/Au (Fig. 53c). The existence of S atoms in the electro-catalysts’ surface was able to boost the under-coordinated sites, thus enhancing the activity for CO2-to-formate reduction.
 Fig. 53  (a) SEM images and schematics for the synthesis of Sn(S) nanostructured electrodes. (b) In situ Sn L3-edge XANES spectra and Sn K-edge of Sn(S) and the relevant controls. (c) Performance tests including polarization curves and potential dependence of the faradaic efficiencies and current densities in CO2-saturated 0.1 M KHCO3 (Reproduced from ref. 398 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017). (d) Schematic images for the synthesis of SnO2@N-rGO and SnO2@rGO nanocomposites, respectively. (e) HRTEM, TEM, and the corresponding elemental mapping images of SnO2/0.14@N-rGO. (f) Faradaic efficiency of CO, HCOOH, and H2 for SnO2/0.07@N-rGO, SnO2/0.14@N-rGO, SnO2/0.21@N-rGO, and SnO2/0.14@rGO, respectively (Reproduced from ref. 399 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
Integrating metal-based catalysts with carbon supports (e.g., CNT, carbon nanofibers, and graphene nanosheet) provides a useful way to boost the electrical conductivity and surface area, and to further improve their electrolytic performance. Besides, it also promotes metal nanoparticle dispersion and alleviates the agglomeration of particles during the electrochemical reaction. Recently, Zhang and co-workers399 prepared a hybrid catalyst containing ultra-small SnO2 nanocrystals deposited on N-doped graphene through the in situ transformation approach (Fig. 53d) and applied the electro-catalysts for CO2 reduction. The presence of SnO2 and N-doped rGO (SnO2@N-rGO) was proved by the TEM images (Fig. 53e). As shown in Fig. 53f, the optimized SnO2/0.14@N-rGO electro-catalyst showed an excellent performance towards formate formation with a high FE up to 89%, which was attributed to the ensemble effect between the well-dispersed SnO2 nanocrystals (Fig. 53e) and the N-doped graphene nanosheets (Fig. 53f).
3.3.2.2 Bi-Based catalysts. 
Bi is another earth-abundant, cost-effective, and environment-friendly catalyst comparable to Sn, which is adjacent to Sn and Pb in the periodic table and has similar electrochemical properties. Before 2017, most studies about Bi-based catalysts for CO2RR focused on selective CO formation in ionic liquids or aprotic electrolytes. Recently, its application in HCOOH production via CO2RR in aqueous solution has aroused intensive interest.400–403 Metallic Bi has a layered crystal architecture similar to black phosphorus, in which the layers interact with each other, mainly via weak van der Waals’ forces. Therefore, many strategies for 2D Bi preparation with atomic layers have been reported so as to expose more active sites for improved CO2RR performance.
Among the different strategies, the sacrificial template method is most commonly used due to the flexibility that various templates such as oxides,404 sulfides,405 and oxyhalides381,385 can be reduced to novel Bi-based catalysts with abundant exposed sites. Recently, Li et al.406 successfully in situ synthesized ultrathin Bi nanosheets derived from the BiOI nanosheets template for CO2 reduction to formate, and reached a maximum selectivity up to ∼100% as well as good stability of 10 h. Moreover, they combined the Bi nanosheets cathode with the Ir-based anode to establish a full cell driven by two alkaline batteries, and achieved an HCOOH FE of ∼95% and electrical energy conversion of 47%. Besides, they also used ultra-thin 2D Bi2O2CO3 nanosheets as a sacrificial template to obtain mesoporous Bi nanosheets with atomic layers for CO2RR, which showed an excellent selectivity for formate (∼100%) with great durability.381 They further proposed that the structural defects in the Bi nanotubes derived from the Bi2O3 nanotubes played a significant role in the catalytic performance.404 As illustrated in Fig. 54a and b, the obtained catalyst consists of a distinct double-layer tubular architecture and a highly fragmented outer surface. The tubular nanostructure could maintain its original form even after the Bi2O3 nanotubes were reduced to metallic Bi under the cathodic potential. According to DFT calculations (Fig. 54c and d), the presence of defective Bi sites was favorable for the stabilization of the *OCHO intermediate and thus improved the CO2RR performance. The nanotube-derived Bi catalyst exhibited an outstanding performance towards CO2 reduction to HCOOH, suggesting a high CO selectivity that approaches 100% within a broad potential range (Fig. 54e) and an excellent formate partial current density of about 60 mA cm−2 at −1.05 V vs. RHE, which is superior to most reports related to CO2-to-formate reduction. Besides, the catalysts loaded on a gas diffusion layer reached an excellent current density of 136 mA cm−2 at −0.86 V vs. RHE in 1 M KHCO3, and ∼288 mA cm−2 at −0.61 V vs. RHE in 1 M KOH within a flow cell (Fig. 54f and g), which showed great potential for commercialization. Similarly, Sargent group385 applied BiOBr as a template for the in situ preparation of petal-shaped Bi nanostructure with prior exposure of Bi(110) facets so as to enhance the electrochemical activity. They also operated the CO2RR within a flow cell under a high total current density over 200 mA cm−2 in 2 M KHCO3 along with a FE of about 90% towards formate formation.
 Fig. 54  (a) HAADF-STEM image. (b) Schematic image of Bi2O3 NTs configuration with fragmented outer walls. (c) Optimized configurations of the *OCHO intermediates adsorbed on different Bi(001) surfaces. (d) Gibbs free energy diagrams for CO2-to-formate conversion. (e) Potential-dependent faradaic efficiencies of different products. (f) Polarization curves of nanotube-derived Bi in 1 M KHCO3 or 1 M KOH in a flow cell, (g) schematic image of the flow cell configuration (Reproduced from ref. 404 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2019). 
Electrodeposition is another facile method to deposit metals on some substrates from the metal ion-containing solution. Carbon materials are commonly composited with metal-based catalysts to improve the electrical conductivity. For example, carbon-supported BiOx NPs (BiOx/C) were prepared through a facile solvothermal approach for CO2 reduction, which exhibited a superior selectivity of 96.0% with a total current density of 12.5 mA cm−2 towards formate formation in 0.5 M NaCl electrolyte.407 Liu et al.408 composed Bi2O3 nanosheets with NGQDs and the obtained electro-catalyst achieved an excellent selectivity of 95.6% towards HCOOH in a wide potential range due to the enhanced binding energy of CO2ads and OCHO* intermediates.
3.3.2.3 In-Based catalysts. 
Similar to Sn and Bi, In is also non-toxic and environment-friendly; however, its cost is higher than that of Sn and Bi, which remains the main obstacle in its industrial implementation. Compared to bulk Sn, the bulk In electrode showed higher selectivity towards formate of ∼95% under the same conditions.391 Nevertheless, the current density of CO2RR on the In electrode is needed to be further improved. Therefore, nanostructured In catalysts were synthesized by different strategies. For example, a 3D hierarchical porous In electro-catalyst was prepared through the template-free electrodeposition on Cu meshes.409 A high HCOOH selectivity of ∼90% as well as a high current efficiency were obtained with an unprecedented production rate of 1.14 mmol cm−2 h−1 at −1.2 V. The DFT results revealed that the porous In surface stabilized the *OCHO intermediate for the formate pathway.
Likewise, oxygen and sulfur residues on the In surface also demonstrated improved electrolytic performance. Ma et al.410 reported a sulfur-doped In catalyst derived from the electroreduction of S-doped In2O3 nanoparticles for enhanced CO2RR. The optimized S2-In electro-catalyst with an S content of 4.9 mol% achieved a high formate selectivity of 85% for a wide range of current densities (25–100 mA cm−2) in the KHCO3 electrolyte. Moreover, in the CsHCO3 electrolyte, it showed a higher value of 93% for HCOOH selectivity and a total current density of 84 mA cm−2 with a production rate of 1449 μmol h−1 cm−2 (Fig. 55a). Based on the DFT calculation results, they proposed that the adsorbed S2− ions on In, which interacted with the hydrated metal cations (e.g., K+ or Cs+) in the double layer, could help H2O to dissociate and form the H* species instead of directly activating CO2, thus stabilizing the formation of the HCOO* intermediate (Fig. 55b and c). The surface S2− species could anchor with the K+(H2O)n cation to keep them close to the In surfaces in the double layer through Coulombic interactions (Fig. 55d). The chemical coupling interaction between the porous In2O3 nanobelts and rGO was also investigated by Zhang and co-workers411 to enhance the CO2 electroreduction. The formate FE and current density of the obtained In2O3–rGO hybrid electro-catalyst were 1.4-fold and 3.6-fold superior to the catalyst prepared via the physical mixing of In2O3 and rGO at −1.2 V, respectively. Based on theoretical analysis, the enhancement in the electro-catalytic activity was attributed to the increase in the electrical conductivity and the stabilization of the key intermediate HCOO−* (Fig. 55e and f).
 Fig. 55  (a) Effect of alkali metal cations (Na+, K+, and Cs+) on the current density and formate FE. (b) Gibbs free energies for CO2-to-HCOOH conversion on In(101) and S–In(101) surfaces. (c) Gibbs free energies for the HER pathway on pure In(101), In/S sites of the S–In(101) surfaces. (d) Schematic image for the role of S2− in facilitating water dissociation and H* formation for CO2 electroreduction to formate (Reproduced from ref. 410 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2019). (e) The differential charge diagram of the In2O3–rGO hybrid electro-catalyst. (f) Gibbs free energy diagram for CO2-to-formate conversion on different catalysts (Reproduced from ref. 411 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). 
3.3.2.4 Other metal-based catalysts. 
Normally, Pb is not regarded as a promising candidate like Sn, Bi, and In for industrial CO2RR applications due to its high toxicity, even though it shows good performance towards CO2 electroreduction. Nevertheless, the fundamental study on Pb for CO2RR still makes sense for comparison with other main group metals. Recently, Guay and co-workers412 prepared honeycomb porous Pb films with dendrite-shaped secondary architecture via dynamic hydrogen bubble templating strategy for efficient electrochemical CO2 reduction, which grew along the [100]-axis. The thickness and the electrochemically active surface area of the obtained porous Pb films could be manipulated by the current density and the deposition time.
