It was observed that an optimized porous Pb film deposited at −4.0 A cm−2vs. 40 s exhibited the best performance, suggesting a formate FE up to 97% along with a partial current density of −7.5 mA cm−2 at an overpotential of −0.99 V after long-term electrolysis of 6 h, which could be attributed to the increased fraction of the [100] surfaces. Furthermore, the morphology effect on the Pb catalyst activity for the CO2-to-formate conversion was studied by Yeo et al.413 They synthesized SD-Pb and OD-Pb electro-catalysts from electrochemically-reduced PbS and PbOx precursors with different morphologies. The obtained SD-Pb catalyst with a wafer structure exhibited a formate FE of 88% and a geometric partial current density approaching 12 mA cm−2 at −1.08 V. Besides, the formate partial current density normalized to the ECSA of the SD-Pb was significantly higher than those of the OD-Pb and polished Pb disks, suggesting a higher activity towards formate formation. The unique morphology was responsible for the improved activity. Therefore, tuning the morphology of the Pb cathodes is an effective way to enhance the CO2RR activity.
In the periodic table, the position of Sb is close to that of Sn and Bi but there are few studies about Sb-based electrodes for CO2 electroreduction.414 For instance, Zhang and co-workers414 reported 2D “few-layer” antimony (Sb) nanosheets prepared by cathodic exfoliation for active CO2RR, which suggested better performance than bulk Sb due to the larger amount of active edge site exposure. The obtained Sb/graphene composite showed a maximum formate FE up to 88.5% with the partial current density approaching 8 mA cm−2 due to the combination of higher density of active sites and the strong electronic interaction between graphene and Sb.


3.3.3 Recent progress in selective CO formation. 
CO is gaseous under the ambient conditions and can be easily separated after electrolysis. It is widely used in chemical, metallurgical, and medicine industries and is the principal raw material for Fischer–Tropsch process for the synthesis of hydrocarbons. The process of CO formation by CO2RR only requires two-electron transfer and the pathway is not as complicated as that for other products’ formation. Therefore, electrochemical CO2 reduction to CO is most likely to be implemented for industrial applications, and thus, has attracted intensive attention in the past few years. CO2-to-CO conversion mainly involves three steps: first, CO2 molecule is adsorbed on the electrode surface to form the *COOH (* refers to the surface-adsorbed species) intermediate through the H+/e− transfer process, then the adsorbed *COOH intermediate further reacts with an electron-proton to generate *CO, and finally, *CO is desorbed from the electrode surface to form the CO product. Hence, the key to designing an efficient electro-catalyst for CO formation is to facilitate the adsorption energy for both CO2 activation and *CO desorption. Noble metals such as Au,392,415–417 Ag,418–420 and Pd421,422 have been extensively studied to exploit novel approaches for excellent performance and to investigate the fundamental mechanisms of CO2RR, which have been proved to be highly active for selective CO production due to their position in the Trassati's volcano curve. These different strategies of improving noble catalysts could also be extended to other non-noble metals. Based on thorough studies on these noble metals for CO formation, researchers have started to shift their interest to non-noble metal-based electro-catalysts and carbon-based electro-catalysts so as to substitute noble metals in the recent years, which are considered as ideal candidates for the further commercialization of CO2-to-CO conversion.
3.3.3.1 Non-noble metal-based catalysts. 
Zn is considered as a promising substitute to noble metals due to its effective CO2-to-CO conversion, low cost, and non-toxicity. Similar to other metal-based catalysts, the effect of surface morphology,423 exposed facets,424 and oxidation states on the CO2RR catalytic performance have been systematically investigated. Recently, a report proposed that Zn nanosheets covered with a porous ZnS sub-nanometer layer could reach an outstanding performance comparable to those of noble metal-based catalysts due to the lower energy barrier for *COOH formation.425 Meanwhile, a porous Zn catalyst was obtained by applying a facile electrodeposition approach so as to improve its CO2RR performance.426 The porous Zn catalyst reached a remarkable CO selectivity up to 95% with a total current density of about 27 mA cm−2 at −0.95 V in an H-type cell. The boost in the activity towards CO production correlated with the increased electrochemical surface area and the high local pH effect in the porous construction. Moreover, the porous Zn catalyst deposited on GDE was applied for CO2RR as well in a flow cell, which obtained a superior current density of 200 mA cm−2 with a CO selectivity up to 84% at −0.64 V, which is superior to most other non-precious metal-based electro-catalysts for CO2RR. These achievements in the exploitation of Zn-based electro-catalysts show tremendous potential for the application of low-cost metals such as Zn to substitute precious metals for further practical CO2 electroreduction to CO with high efficiency.
In addition to Zn, Cu is also capable of reducing CO2 to CO or further reducing CO to hydrocarbons. Cu is favorable for hydrocarbon production, leading to relatively low CO selectivity. In addition, Cu-based alloys such as CuPd, CuIn, and CuAu have shown excellent performance for CO formation due to the break in the scaling relations through the adjustment of the binding energies for the key intermediates.427,428 Besides, Cu-based hybrid catalysts such as Cu–SnO2 with a core–shell structure,429 SnO2-modified CuO nanowires,430 and hybrid Cu/Ni(OH)2 nanosheets431 have been studied as well, which successfully reduced CO2 to CO with a superior selectivity of more than 90%. Furthermore, another non-noble metals such as Bi, which prefer to produce formate in aqueous solutions, resulted in a completely different product orientation by employing ionic liquids as the promoter or electrolyte, which indicated high CO selectivity towards CO2RR.432,433
3.3.3.2 Metal-free carbon-based catalysts. 
Carbon is commonly used as a catalyst support in the field of electrocatalysis owing to its high SSA, electrical conductivity, low cost, and earth abundance. Besides, heteroatom doping is an efficient approach to introduce active sites into the inert carbon matrix, which is able to modify the electron structure of the carbon surface so as to enable it to absorb the CO2 molecule and stabilize the reaction intermediates during CO2RR. In the past few years, various carbon materials such as CNT, graphene, and carbon fibers have been employed as supports to synthesize N-doped carbon electro-catalysts for CO2RR.434,435
Researchers have also found that the type and concentration of N in the carbon matrix can influence the CO2RR performance, wherein pyridinic N is regarded as one of the most functional active sites.434,436,437 For instance, Liu et al.435 synthesized 3D N-GRW via the adjustment of N dopants, obtaining a high CO selectivity of 87.6% at a relatively low overpotential of 0.49 V. The pyridinic N sites in the catalyst were found to be the active sites for CO2RR, exhibiting a linear relationship with the current density (Fig. 56a). In addition, DFT calculations further suggested that the pyridinic N was favorable for the transformation of CO2 to *COOH and *CO desorption during the CO2RR (Fig. 56b). Moreover, detailed DFT calculations of various active sites in the N-doped graphene matrix (Fig. 56c) for CO2RR were employed to investigate the activity trends of these different sites.437 As shown in Fig. 56d, the binding energies of COOH* and CO* intermediates in the N-doped graphene did not maintain linear scaling relationships like the transition metals. Notably, many active sites suggested a lower required binding energy for COOH* formation than that of Au in the thermodynamic activity volcano image (Fig. 56e), thus demonstrating higher efficiency for CO2-to-CO reduction. Pyridinic-N (Fig. 56c (14)) showed a low *COOH binding energy at low overpotentials, suggesting high activity for CO formation.
 Fig. 56  (a) The relation between the CO partial current density and the pyridinic N content in various N-GRW catalysts at 0.5 (left) and 0.7 V (right) versus RHE. (b) Illustration of the CO2-to-CO conversion processes on the N-GRW catalysts and the free energy diagram for CO2-to-CO conversion on various N-GRW catalysts (Reproduced from ref. 435 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2018). (c) Optimized structures of different examined carbon structures with and without N-doping, where LD stands for line defect. Here, 5, 6, 7, and 8 refer to the pentagon, hexagon, heptagon, and octagon in the defect configuration. (d) Activity volcano for CO2-to-CO conversion. (e) Free energy diagram of CO2-to-CO conversion on different examined active sites displayed in panel (a) at an equilibrium potential of −1.2 V vs. RHE (Reproduced from ref. 437 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2017). (f) Schematic illustration showing the synthetic processes of the NS-C layers. (g) Free-energy diagrams of CO2RR at an electrode potential U = 0 V for graphitic-N, pyridinic-N, S-containing graphitic-N, and S-containing pyridinic-N (Reproduced from ref. 438 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2019). 
However, these N-doped carbon catalysts still suffer from low current density and poor efficiency at a low overpotential. Recently, other heteroatoms such as sulfur have been introduced as dopants into the N-doped carbon matrix to form a dual-doping catalyst so as to further improve the CO2RR performance.438 Li and coworkers438 reported an N-doped carbon catalyst with sulfur addition (Fig. 56f) to boost the activity and selectivity towards CO production. The obtained N,S-co-doped carbon catalyst reached an excellent CO selectivity up to 92% with a partial current density of 2.63 mA cm−2 at a relatively low overpotential of 0.49 V, which is superior to that of the S-free N–C catalyst. The introduction of S into N–C resulted in the formation of a higher concentration of pyridinic N, which facilitated CO2 activation. Moreover, the S dopants reduced the binding energy barrier of *COOH, thereby significantly improving the activity of the pyridinic N and graphitic N sites towards CO formation (Fig. 56g).
3.3.3.3 Metal–nitrogen–carbon materials. 
To further improve the performance of carbon-based electro-catalysts towards CO formation, the introduction of transition metals into N-doped carbon have been paid much attention to for efficient CO2RR.439–443 The metal- and N-co-doped carbon catalysts consist of atomically-dispersed transition metals and the N-doped carbon matrix, referred to as M–N–C electro-catalysts (M = Fe, Co, Ni, etc.). The single atom carbon-based catalysts have shown outstanding performance for CO2-to-CO conversion due to their maximum atomic utilization, tunable coordination environments, and unique electronic properties. In 2017, Strasser et al.439 reported a series of transition metal- and N-co-doped porous carbons containing atomically dispersed M–Nx centers (M = Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu) for the effective CO2 electroreduction to CO and studied their intrinsic catalytic reactivity and selectivity (Fig. 57a), in which the M–Nx centers worked as the active sites. The theoretical and experimental results both proved that Fe–N–C and especially Ni–N–C displayed comparable CO2RR performance to that of Au- and Ag-based electro-catalysts. Moreover, Ni–N–C achieved a maximal selectivity of CO at a relatively high potential while Fe–N–C produced more CO at lower potentials (Fig. 57b). A similar conclusion was reported by Li and coworkers444 that Fe and Ni were found to be more active than other metals in the M–N–C structure for CO2 electroreduction to CO. Fe–N–C achieved the lowest onset overpotential of 0.19 V while Ni–N–C reached the highest CO selectivity of up to 96% at a moderate overpotential of 0.65 V (Fig. 57c). Mechanistic investigations shown in Fig. 57d indicated that for M–N–C (M = Fe, Cr, Mn), the production of a *COOH intermediate during CO2RR was the rate-determining step, while for M–N–C (M = Ni, Co), the formation of a *CO2˙− species through the first electron transfer process was crucial.
 Fig. 57  (a) CO faradaic efficiencies (FE) and CO partial currents (mass activity) vs. the applied potential for the five M–N–C catalysts compared to the state-of-the-art Au catalysts (Reproduced from ref. 439 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). (b) The summary of maximum CO FEs and CO TOFs at the corresponding overpotentials for M–N–C. (c) Proposed CO2RR mechanisms on M–N–C (Reproduced from ref. 444 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). (d) Optimized atomic structures of different Ni–N structures with Ni atoms coordinated with 4N atoms (NiN4), 3N atoms (NiN3 and NiN3V), 2N atoms (NiN2V2), and free energy diagrams for CO2-to-CO with different Ni–N structures.445 (e) CO and H2 faradaic efficiency and (b) CO partial current densities of Fe3+–N–C in an H-cell (red) and on a GDE (blue), and of Fe2+–N–C in an H-cell (black) (Reproduced from ref. 442 with permission from American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2019). 
Apart from the type of metal centers, metallic states including the coordinative environment and the oxidation state also influenced the catalysts’ activity.381,383,441 Coordinatively saturated M–N4 moieties in heterogeneous M–N–C have been widely studied and demonstrated to be effective for heterogeneous CO2 electroreduction to CO.446–449 In 2017, Wu et al.446 first proved that exclusive Ni–N4 sites exhibited unprecedentedly high activity and selectivity for CO formation, which could be assigned to the lower formation energy of COOH* relative to that for N–C. Besides the typical M–N4 structure, other coordinatively unsaturated M–Nx (x = 1–3) centers for CO2 electroreduction to CO were also comparatively studied. For example, a series of Ni–N–C catalysts containing coordinatively unsaturated Ni–N sites doped within the porous carbon were prepared by the pyrolysis of Zn/Ni bimetallic ZIF-8, which exhibited high CO selectivity from 92.0% to 98.0% in the broad potential range from −0.53 V to −1.03 V (vs. RHE) and reached an outstanding current density of about 71.5 mA cm−2 at −1.03 V.445 DFT calculations indicated that CO2RR was more favorable than HER over the coordinatively unsaturated Ni–N sites, and the binding energies for *COOH formation on the coordinatively unsaturated NiN3, NiN3V, and NiN2V2 moieties were significantly lower than those on NiN4, leading to high current density and faradaic efficiency. Moreover, the effect of the oxidation state of the metal centers on the CO2RR performance was also examined. A catalyst containing dispersed single Fe atom sites was reported for efficient CO production,442 exhibiting a very low overpotential of 80 mV along with a CO partial current density of up to 94 mA cm−2 at an overpotential of 340 mV. The active sites were found to be Fe3+ ions coordinated with pyrrolic N atoms within the N-doped carbon matrix, and the +3 oxidation state was maintained during CO2RR. The Fe3+ sites possessed better performance than the conventional Fe2+ sites due to faster CO2 adsorption and weaker CO absorption.


3.3.4 Recent progress in the selective formation of C2+ products. 
Typically, C2+ products such as ethylene, ethanol, and n-propanol are more desirable than C1 products owing to their higher energy densities and highly commercial values. For example, ethylene can be employed as a raw material to produce polyethylene, ethylene oxide, and diesel. However, the conversion of CO2 to C2+ products is not as easy as C1 products formation because of the large obstacle for C–C coupling activation and the competition between the C–C bond and the other bonds’ (C–O and C–H) generation. For C2+ products formation, the binding energy of *CO on the electrode surface should be strong enough for further C–C coupling, which would inevitably lead to increased activation barriers. Therefore, it is necessary to optimize the interaction for selective C–C coupling and to exploit novel electro-catalysts. Among different metal-based catalysts, copper has been intensively studied as the most active electro-catalysts for C2+ products formation due to its moderate adsorption energy towards *CO.450 However, most C2+ products formation catalysts still suffer from high overpotentials and relatively low selectivity, which are far from industrial applications.
In the past few years, various modification methods on Cu structures have been reported, including nanostructured Cu, oxide-derived (OD) Cu, Cu alloys, and supported Cu electro-catalysts, as summarized in previous reviews.451 Electrocatalytic CO2RR on single crystal Cu facets demonstrated that single crystal Cu(100) possessed higher electivity towards C2+ products formation than Cu(111).449,452,453 Recently, DFT calculation results (Fig. 58a) demonstrated that the Cu(100) and stepped (211) facets were more favorable for selective C2+ products formation than Cu(111).454 Besides, the Cu nanocube catalyst showed a six-fold enhancement in the ratio of C2+ to C1 product formation, reaching a maximum C2+ FE of more than 60% and a corresponding C2+ partial current density of up to 40 mA cm−2, which was consistent with the theoretical result.
 Fig. 58  (a) DFT simulations of C–C coupling on the Cu facets (Reproduced from ref. 454 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018). (b) Schematic of the electro-growth process and the SEM images of ERD-Cu under CO2RR with time resolution. (c) DFT studies of C1 and C2 electro-production as a function of the metal oxidation state (Reproduced from ref. 455 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018). (d) Schematic illustration of the Cu2S–Cu–V CSVE electro-catalyst design for the production of multi-carbon alcohols from CO2 reduction. (e) Simulated result of the positron density distribution in the pure Cu2S bulk (left graph) and the schematic representation of trapped positrons of copper vacancies in the CSVE catalyst (right graph) (Reproduced from ref. 456 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018). (f) Schematic of the cathode portion of a gas diffusion electrode for CO2. (g) Schematic illustration and cross-sectional SEM image of the graphite/carbon NPs/Cu/PTFE electrode (Reproduced from ref. 457 with permission from American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2018). 
OD-Cu possesses boosted activity and selectivity towards C2+ products formation.455,458 Various fabrication strategies including annealed/OD-Cu,459 electrodeposited/OD-Cu,454,455 Cl−/OD-Cu, plasma/OD-Cu,460 and in situ/OD-Cu461 for OD-Cu catalysts’ synthesis have been developed, leading to various product distributions. However, the actual active sites in these OD-Cu materials for electrochemical CO2 reduction are still controversial. Some reports proposed that the grain boundaries393 and under-coordinated atoms462,463 generated from the electroreduction of the copper oxide catalyst worked as the actual active sites for CO2RR. Besides, Cu0 is also regarded as an actual electro-catalyst due to the driving force for oxidized Cu reduction under negative potentials during electrolysis.464,465 In contrast, some groups demonstrated that the Cu+ site remaining on the electrode surface under reducing potentials played a key role in the boosted activity and C2+ products selectivity during CO2RR.455,460,466 Moreover, sub-surface oxygen in the OD-Cu catalysts was also found to be functional in causing a synergy effect between the surface Cu+ and the surface Cu0 sub-oxide species.458 Recently, ERD-Cu was reported for the enhanced electrochemical CO2 reduction by the simultaneous control of the morphology and oxidation state during the synthetic process (Fig. 58b).455 The oxidation state of Cu was monitored via in situ soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy during CO2RR with time resolution, which is indicative of a stable state of Cu+ under negative reduction potentials. The sharp structures kinetically suppressed CH4 production via the influence of a high local pH while Cu+ existence could stabilize the OCCOH* intermediate along the C2H4 pathway (Fig. 58c). Consequently, ERD copper established a superior C2H4 partial current density of 161 mA cm−2 within a flow cell at −1.0 V with a high formation ratio of C2H4 to CH4 (up to 200).
Introducing heteroatom dopants into Cu-based catalysts has also been considered as a useful way to adjust the surface electron structure and the Cu oxidation state for improved electrolytic performance. Recently, Sargent's group467 employed boron to tune the ratio of the Cuδ+ to Cu0 active sites with enhanced efficiency and durability towards C2 products formation. Tuning the average oxidation state of Cu could manipulate the CO adsorption and dimerization, achieving a highly selective C2 products formation by CO2RR. The boron-doped Cu catalyst exhibited a high C2 selectivity of ∼79% and maintained a stable activity during electrolysis for 40 h. Besides, copper sulfide-derived catalysts have also attracted much attention for the generation of stable surface defects and for tuning the concentration of surface vacancies.468 A series of CSVE electro-catalysts with S atoms in the nanoparticle core and Cu vacancies in the shell (Fig. 58d) was prepared for efficient CO2RR,456 which favor the production of liquid alcohols. Besides, the catalyst reached a C2+ alcohol partial current density of ∼126 mA cm−2 with a corresponding FE of ∼32%. The simulated results (Fig. 58e) of the positron density distribution suggested a higher vacancy density for the CSVE NPs catalyst compared to the pure Cu2S bulk. Typically, Cu foil/plate/mesh/foam or FTO is employed as a substrate for Cu-based electro-catalysts for CO2RR, and glassy carbon or carbon cloth/paper is often applied for Cu powder electro-catalysts. Recently, more researchers have applied highly efficient GDE in flow cells for C2+ products formation. The GDE can enhance the activity of the corresponding deposited Cu-based electro-catalysts due to their gas/electrolyte penetrability. Sargent's group457 fabricated Cu electro-catalysts supported on polymer-based GDL (Fig. 58g) for CO2-to-ethylene reduction in an alkaline electrolyte within a flow cell architecture (Fig. 58), which reached an unprecedented C2H4 selectivity of 70% at an applied potential of −0.55 V vs. RHE and an extremely high ethylene partial current density of 473 mA cm−2 at a relatively low potential of −0.67 V. Abundant hydroxide ions close to the Cu surface derived from high KOH concentrations reduced the activation energy barriers for C–C coupling. The introduction of a polymer-based GDL sandwiched between the separated hydrophobic and conductive supports helped the electrode to maintain stable electrolysis for 150 h.


3.3.5 Summary and outlook of electrocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
Recent progress in the development of novel electro-catalysts for selective products formation (formate, CO, and C2+ products) by CO2RR have been summarized and the merits have been systematically assessed including current density, faradaic efficiency, and electrolysis durability. Sn-, Bi-, and In-based electro-catalysts for CO2 electroreduction to formate, non-noble metal-based catalysts and carbon-based catalysts for CO formation, and Cu-based catalysts for C2+ products formation have attracted much attention in the past three years and have shown great potential for further industrial application due to their low cost, earth abundance, environment-friendly property, as well as the high electrolytic efficiency. Moreover, various, strategies have been undertaken to prepare unique metal-based catalysts, including nanostructured metals, oxide-derived metals, sulfur-derived metals, metallic alloys, and metal@carbon composites, tuning the adsorption energy energies of the CO2 and CO2 intermediates with enhanced reactivity and selectivity towards electrocatalytic CO2 reduction. The introduction of heteroatoms and transition metals into the carbon-based catalysts is usually considered as an effective approach to improve their CO2RR performance. Therefore, it is crucial to develop a controllable synthetic approach to generate active sites with suitable adsorption energies for reaction intermediates to selectively produce various products via different CO2 reduction pathways. Moreover, the development of electro-catalysts loaded on gas diffusion electrodes applied in flow cells is also very crucial for the realization of large-scale production.

3.4. Photoelectrochemical conversion of CO2
Photoelectrocatalysis has shown enormous potential in water splitting driven by a broad solar spectrum but it is still in its infancy for CO2 reduction. In recent years, the PEC conversion of CO2 in aqueous environments has become appealing as it enables the generation of fuels or chemicals using renewable solar energy. Compared to photocatalysis and electrocatalysis, the photoelectrochemical reduction of CO2 can integrate their advantages to simultaneously achieve improved catalytic efficiency and decreased electric energy input. Unlike the electrolysis process during which CO2 is only reduced by external electricity, photogenerated electrons and holes in the PEC system react with CO2 molecules and H2O on the catalysts’ surface to form reduced products and oxygen, respectively. Moreover, compared with photocatalytic CO2 reduction, the PEC system could exhibit a higher efficiency for CO2 reduction due to more efficient charge separation assisted by external electricity. Based on different photoelectrodes, the PEC CO2 reduction system can be commonly divided into three categories using semiconductors as photocathodes, photo-anodes, or both the photo-anode and photocathode (Fig. 59).
 Fig. 59  Schematic diagrams for PEC CO2 reduction in water using a semiconductor as (a) a photocathode, (b) a photo-anode, and (c) both photo-anode and photocathode. (d) Schematic diagram for the device combining a photovoltaic cell with an efficient electrochemical catalyst for CO2 reduction (Reproduced from ref. 469 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018). 

3.4.1 Basic principle of photoelectrocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
Similar to eletrocatalytic CO2 reduction, the PEC system consists of two half-reactions, for which CO2 electroreduction takes place in the cathodic side while OER takes place in the anodic side. The main reaction products include CO, HCOOH, methanol, and CH4 as well as long-chain hydrocarbons with H2 as the by-product from water. Notably, it is difficult to select an original semiconductor material with a suitable band energy location for both CO2RR and OER. Therefore, different types of PEC systems with the combination of a semiconductor and a metal or two semiconductors were developed (Fig. 59). In terms of each configuration, the selection of photoelectrode materials combined with the electrolyte is of significant importance, leading to various product distributions. Typically, n-type semiconductors, having more positive valence band maximum than water oxidation potential, are employed as the photo-anode and p-type semiconductors with the conduction band minimum higher than the CO2 reduction potential are employed as the photocathode. When the photo-anode is used as a light absorption electrode to generate electron–hole pairs, the photogenerated holes would be supplied for the oxygen evolution reaction and the generated electrons would migrate to the dark-cathode for CO2RR with the assistance of the applied bias potential. In terms of the photocathode–anode system, electron–hole pairs are formed by the light absorption of the photocathode and charge separation is driven via the applied bias potential. The generated electrons would be consumed by CO2RR on the photocathode surface and OER would simultaneously proceed on the surface of the anode. Moreover, a photo-anode connected with a photocathode is able to form the Z-scheme conversion of CO2 and H2O so as to establish a single photoelectrode-counter electrode system. The applied bias potential is not necessary for the two photoelectrodes system if the stable current–potential curves of each photoelectrode are intersected from the onset position. The energy levels of VB and CB as well as the external applied electricity are significantly crucial for products distribution. A schematic energy level graph of PEC water oxidation and CO2RR with a CO2 activation catalyst for the photo-anode–cathode system is presented in Fig. 60.
 Fig. 60  Schematic representation of the energy level diagram of PEC water oxidation and CO2 reduction reaction with CO2 activation catalyst on the thermodynamic and kinetic aspects (Reproduced from ref. 470 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2019). 


3.4.2 Recent progress in photocathode-dark anode system. 
Photocathodes made of p-type semiconductors combined with a dark anode (such as carbon material or Pt) are the most widely used configuration for PEC CO2 reduction (Fig. 59a). Various p-type semiconductors have been developed, such as metal oxides,471,472 nitrides,473 and phosphides.474 In 1978, a pioneering study on PEC CO2 reduction was first published by Halmann,475 in which the p-GaP semiconductor was employed as a photocathode with a carbon rod as the anode and an external electrical bias was applied for the system. As a result, the following products, HCOOH, HCHO, and CH3OH, were obtained. Recently, Persson et al.476 reported the largest CO2 photocathode examination that covered 68860 candidate materials through a computational screening approach to evaluate the synthesizability, corrosion resistance, visible-light absorption, and compatibility of the electronic structure with fuel preparation (Fig. 61a). It was proved that only 52 materials met the strict CO2 photocathode requirements. However, these pure semiconductor photocathodes always possess low activity towards CO2 reduction due to inefficient light harvesting and the lack of active sites for CO2 activation. Therefore, many strategies including nanostructure formation, heteroatom doping, heterojunction construction, and co-catalyst decoration have been deployed to enhance the reaction efficiency on the photocathodes.
 Fig. 61  (a) The selection criteria, as well as the number of materials that satisfy the criterion, are shown for each tier (Reproduced from ref. 476 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2019). (b) Schematic diagrams for a typical structure heterojunction and photocathode components (Reproduced from ref. 469 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2018). (c) Layer-by-layer molecular p–n-junction assembly formed by Zr(IV)–phosphonate bridging. (d) n-GaN nanowires on the silicon substrate with the surface-immobilized molecular assemblies (Reproduced from ref. 477 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2019). 
3.4.2.1 Unique structural design of the photocathodes. 
Considering the low efficiency of light absorption and the slow electron transfer process for conventional photocathodes, unique structures of photocathodes have been developed to enhance light harvesting and to improve the charge kinetics. Integrating semiconductors into heterojunctions provides an efficient way to enhance the light absorption, steer the charge kinetics, and improve the durability. A typical structure of the heterojunctions is shown in Fig. 61b, where the photogenerated electrons are transferred from a higher CB-containing semiconductor to a lower one, and the photogenerated holes are collected at the semiconductor with a higher VB after light harvesting. Subsequently, these accumulated electrons on the lower CB-containing semiconductor would participate in CO2RR, while the holes would migrate to the other side. For example, Cu2O is a typical p-type semiconductor, which is promising for photoelectrochemical CO2 reduction. Integrating Cu2O and other semiconductors such as CuO into heterojunctions proved to be efficient for photoelectro-catalysis since they can match well at the interface.478 Moreover, ZnO@ZnSe and ZnTe/ZnO479 have also been reported for efficient PEC CO2 reduction in the past few years.
In addition, semiconductor photocathodes with heterojunctions further combined with molecular assemblies have also showed great feasibility to boost the system efficiencies, avoiding the shortcomings of two optical conversion cells. Recently, Thomas and co-workers477 synthesized a series of photocathodes with a layer-by-layer structure (Fig. 61c and d) through the binary p–n junction approach for highly active CO2-to-formate conversion. Light irradiation on the photocathodes produced redox separated states, which generated an intermediate state with remotely-separated electrons and holes at the catalyst and the semiconductor, respectively. The function mechanism of the photoelectrode was derived from the dynamic interaction between the molecules and the p–n junctions, in which two photosensitive cells harvested visible light in a complementary way and produced electrons to activate the complex for CO2-to-formate conversion. As a result, the obtained photocathodes enabled formate formation with a maximum FE of up to 64% and a steady photocurrent density of about −1.1 mA cm−2 during 20 h of photoelectrolysis.
3.4.2.2 Co-catalysts anchored on photocathodes. 
Some highly-efficient electro-catalysts for CO2RR are also effective cocatalysts for photocathodes to boost the activity and selectivity towards desirable products. These CO2RR co-catalysts can be classified into four categories: noble metal-based, non-noble metal-based, metal-free, and biological co-catalysts. In the past few decades, noble metal-based co-catalysts have been most extensively reported due to their outstanding performance. For example, Au has aroused much attention owing to its high activity towards CO production by electrochemical CO2 reduction. Pure nanoparticles of Au or Au alloys have been combined with various p-type semiconductors such as p-Si, p-GaN, and ZnO, displaying enhanced activity and selectivity towards CO formation.479–481 Besides, noble metal complexes have also been widely applied for PEC CO2 reduction, facilitating charge separation by trapping the photo-generated electrons from the photocathodes and the reduced metal centers can act as active sites for CO2 conversion.
Considering the scarcity and high cost of precious metals, non-noble metal-based co-catalysts have been intensively investigated for further large-scale application. A series of low-cost and earth-abundant metals including Cu,430,482 Sn,483 and Co484 in the form of nanoparticles or complexes have been used as co-catalysts on photocathodes to reach a high efficiency for the production of various products by the PEC CO2 reduction. Recently, a noble metal-free molecular catalyst-based photocathode was reported for efficient CO2 conversion in aqueous solution, which consisted of a p-Si semiconductor modified with a mesoporous TiO2 layer and adsorbed Co catalyst.484 The obtained hybrid photoelectrode reached a high turnover number of ∼330 and a high selectivity of about 75% towards CO production, and maintained an excellent stability exceeding 24 h in both organic solution and pure aqueous solution, which is comparable to those of noble metal-based co-catalysts.
In addition, metal-free co-catalysts including organic molecules, conductive polymers, and enzymatic biocatalysts have been applied for PEC CO2 reduction in the early years. During photo-electrolysis, the organic molecules usually work as mediators to migrate electrons and protons to the carbonaceous reactants. Besides, the conductive polymers possessing rich functional groups can be bonded with reaction molecules and change the adsorption constitution. In particular, biological co-catalysts anchored onto photocathodes have attracted much attention and showed enormous potential in industrial application due to their abundant active sites for CO2 reduction, which led to improved performance. For example, Park et al.485 developed a tandem PEC cell with an integrated enzyme-cascade setup by employing an Rh complex and three dehydrogenases, which migrated the photogenerated electrons to a multi-enzyme cascade for biocatalyzed methanol formation. During photo-electrolysis, NADH reacted with dehydrogenases to form NAD+, then the generated NAD+ was converted to NADH through the Rh complex and electrons for efficient regeneration.


3.4.3 Recent progress in ohoto-anode–dark cathode system. 
Another promising way for bias-assisted PEC CO2 reduction is to combine an effective cathode for electrocatalytic CO2 reduction with a photo-anode to oxidize water under irradiation, providing electrons and protons for the cathodic side (Fig. 59b). As the light absorber, the photo-anode can be assembled with earth-abundant, low-cost, and stable n-type metal oxide semiconductors (e.g., TiO2, BiVO4, and Fe2O3).486 TiO2 is one of the most widely applied n-type semiconductors for photocatalysis, which has been extensively employed as a photo-anode to absorb light, combined with a cathode for PEC CO2 reduction.487,488 For instance, Pt-modified TiO2 nanotubes and P-modified rGO on Ni foam were used as the photo-anode and cathode, respectively, for PEC CO2 reduction, during which CO2 was reduced into a series of liquid products, including CH3OH, C2H5OH, HCOOH, and CH3COOH.487 Apart from metal/carbon cathodes, metal oxides have also been considered as promising candidates for efficient CO2 reduction. Recently, a photoelectrocatalytic system consisting of nanosheet-like CoOx as the cathode and TiO2 nanotube arrays as the photo-anode was employed for PEC CO2 reduction.488 The cathode was obtained by the electrodeposition of the nanosheet-like CoOx onto a copper foam, which exhibited a maximum FE of 60.9% towards HCOOH formation for electrochemical CO2 reduction. Compared with the electrocatalytic system, the PEC system combining CoOx and TiO2 nanotubes exhibited a high HCOOH yield of 56.6 μmol cm−2 h at a low applied potential. Besides, CoOx coated with a thin ZnO layer was prepared through the atomic layer deposition approach for protection so as to improve the durability of the cathode. Furthermore, a (040)-facet engineered BiVO4 plate was employed as a photo-anode combined with a Cu cathode for PEC CO2 reduction (Fig. 62a), formatting rich protons to convert CO2 into various liquid products by tuning the reduction potential.489 As a result, a high selectivity of 62% towards HCOOH production and a FE of 85% for formaldehyde, 8% for MeOH, and 6% for EtOH were obtained at various potentials. The outstanding photo-anodes in water splitting systems can be further used for the PEC CO2 reduction setup with efficient cathodes in the future and an improved performance can be expected.
 Fig. 62  (a) Schematic illustrations of 040-BVO-driven PEC CO2 reduction (Reproduced from ref. 490 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). (b) CO2 reduction mechanism over the photocathode and the schematic representation of the two-electrode PEC CO2 reduction (Reproduced from ref. 491 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). (c) Schematic illustration for the solar-driven CO2 reduction device (Reproduced from ref. 430 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). 


3.4.4 Photocathode–photo-anode and photovoltaic tandem electrocatalytic devices systems. 
The combination of a photo-anode with a photocathode provides an approach to maximize the light harvesting and minimize the external electrical bias of PEC CO2 reduction, thus reducing the energy input (Fig. 59c). Within this system, an n-type semiconductor with an adequately positive VB position is used as the photo-anode for water oxidation, while a p-type semiconductor with an adequately negative CB position is used as the photocathode for CO2RR. Besides, the CB position of the photo-anode should be higher than the VB position of the photocathode to allow the Z-scheme charge transfer. The selected photo-anode and photocathode should be extremely stable in the respective electrolyte and the band gaps of both the electrodes should be suitable for visible light harvesting. Recently, a hybrid photocathode consisting of ruthenium complex with a multi-heterojunction architecture (TiO2/N, Zn–Fe2O3/Cr2O3) and a reduced SrTiO3 photo-anode (Fig. 62c) was applied for PEC CO2 reduction.491 A steady photocurrent of 150 μA cm−2 for formic acid and CO formation under 1 sun irradiation at −0.1 V vs. RHE was obtained. The system exhibited superior energy conversion efficiency of 0.15% for solar to chemical transformation. With the absence of an external bias, the excellent performance was attributed to the functional combination of the photo-anode and the photocathode through suitable band arrangement and heterojunctions in the photocathode.
In addition, a novel PEC set up coupling a photovoltaic cell with an active electro-catalyst was invented for CO2 reduction (Fig. 59d), demonstrating a promising approach for high energy conversion efficiency. Photovoltaic cells can be well matched to the solar spectrum to maximize the light absorption, resulting in high incident photon–electron conversion efficiency, which provides sufficient current density for the chemical reaction. Meanwhile, a strong voltage was supported by connecting the photovoltaic cells in series to meet the thermodynamic redox potential of CO2 reduction and water oxidation. Recently, an earth-abundant SnO2-modified CuO catalyst prepared through atomic layer deposition was employed as both the anode and the cathode for complete CO2 electrolysis due to its efficient activity towards CO2RR and oxygen evolution reaction.430 In the solar-driven CO2 reduction device, a bipolar membrane was used to divide the anodic and cathodic electrolytes, and a GaInP/GaInAs/Ge photovoltaic cell was employed for CO2 splitting into CO and O2 driven by solar energy, which reached a high efficiency of 13.4% and selectivity over 80% towards CO production.


3.4.5 Summary and outlook of photoelectrocatalytic CO2 reduction. 
Photoelectrocatalysis integrates photocatalysis with electrocatalysis and combines their advantages, thus enabling higher energy efficiency. The photocathode–anode system has been widely investigated and great efforts have been devoted to modifying the photocathodes made from p-type semiconductors, including enhancing the light utilization efficiency and constructing active sites with high activity. Establishing unique structures of photocathodes via heterojunction formation and anchoring co-catalysts have been carried out to effectively improve the light utilization efficiency and facilitate CO2 activation and reduction, respectively. Within a photo-anode–cathode PEC system, the photo-anode consisting of n-type semiconductors is applied for light harvesting independently, while the cathode served as the active site for CO2 reduction reaction, for which the separation configuration has the flexibility to optimize the performance. The combination of photo-anode with photocathode to establish a Z-scheme photocatalytic setup can minimize the consumption of external electricity and extend the lifetime of the semiconductors, which is an ideal candidate for PEC CO2 reduction as it can be operated without external electricity input; however, it still needs further exploration. In addition, integrating a photovoltaic solar cell to an electrocatalytic system is also appealing due to the high efficiency. Despite the above-mentioned advancement achieved in the recent years, the development of PEC CO2 reduction is still in its infancy and stays far from satisfactory. The activity, durability, and selectivity towards specific products needs to be enhanced, and the ambiguous reaction mechanism needs to be further studied through some in situ technologies. Besides, the cell design for PEC CO2 reduction with high mass transfer efficiency is highly warranted for practical applications.

3.5. Photothermal conversion of CO2
The idea of “photothermal CO2 reduction” arose under the backdrop of increasing carbon dioxide emissions (global warming) and fossil fuel over-exploitation (energy crisis), and has evolved in the anticipation of renewable energy, such as solar energy, being utilized/coupled to power CO2 conversion to value-added chemicals or fuels. From a practical perspective, solar energy is transformed and stored in the CO2-derived products, which could then be used as an active “working carbon” for the supply of essential chemicals or as an alternative energy resource in our daily life. Such a practice mimics the natural photosynthesis process and would deliver a man-made solar-powered “carbon cycling” concept.492,493 In addition, the quantum efficiency and reaction rate/energy efficiency of photothermal CO2 catalysis is estimated to be higher than that of conventional semiconductor-based CO2 photocatalysis494 and thermal catalysis,489 respectively. As an added benefit, the lower operating temperature during photothermal CO2 catalysis can suppress unwanted side reactions and improve the long-term stability of the catalyst.495–498 From a mechanistic perspective, photothermal CO2 catalysis conversion relies on the capability of a catalyst to synergistically incorporate heating (thermochemistry) with light–matter interactions (photochemistry) in the gaseous CO2 reduction reaction.499,500 To clarify, “photochemistry” in the context of this review differs from that of a traditional photochemical process, which involves an electronic transition from the ground state to an excited state upon light absorption by a molecule.501 In photothermal studies, photochemistry has been associated with the generation of electron–hole pairs by an optically active catalyst, which activates adjacent molecules/intermediates for catalytic conversion.502 Despite its similarity, photochemistry is not interchangeable with photocatalysis, which is conducted at room temperature. The coupling of photochemical processes with conventional thermal activation mechanism offers opportunities to address the slow reactivity and/or low selectivity during CO2 reduction by targeting the high-activation-barrier/kinetically-relevant intermediary steps.503,504

3.5.1 Fundamental understanding of photothermal CO2 catalysis. 
3.5.1.1 LSPR excitation and its subsequent dephasing mechanisms. 
Seen as one of the most important mediators for the conversion of solar energy to chemical energy, LSPR excitation in plasmonic metallic nanostructures and its subsequent dephasing processes have aroused significant interest in the domain of catalytic chemical synthesis and transformation.505,506 LSPR excitation is achieved when a metal with a high free electron mobility interacts with photons that match the resonant frequency of the electrons oscillating against the restoring force of the positive nuclei.507 Many studies have shown that, in addition to conventional plasmonic metals (Au, Ag, and Cu), other metallic or metal-like nanostructures can exhibit variable plasmonic properties.508–511 Following the excitation of the LSPR in plasmonic nanostructures, the collective electron oscillation non-radiatively dephases and generates a non-thermal electron distribution, normally in a very short time scale (1–10 fs).512 The electron distribution rapidly thermalizes to a Fermi–Dirac distribution via electron–electron or electron–phonon scatterings, which give way to a high lattice temperature (Fig. 63a). This pathway acts mainly as a heating source from light–matter interaction. The dephasing process becomes more complicated in the presence of electron-accepting orbitals of a surface adsorbate or an electronically interacting semiconductor (Fig. 63b).506 In addition to the generation of a non-thermal electron distribution (also known as Landau damping), hot electrons with a sufficiently high kinetic energy have a chance to be injected into the unoccupied states of adjacent objects. A direct electron transfer mechanism (known as CID) was proposed to describe the interaction of the excited energetic electrons with unpopulated adsorbate orbitals and was recently confirmed by time-dependent DFT calculations for Ag/H2O513 and Ag/CO.514 A similar phenomenon was also confirmed when the adsorbed molecule was replaced by a semiconductor cluster (Cd33Se33).515 The electron transfer processes are always accompanied by thermalization and back-transfer processes, as depicted in Fig. 63b. The excitation of the LSPR and the subsequent electronic excitations are powerful for promoting reactant activation or modulating reaction pathways (referred to as hot electron photochemistry506) and can increase the CO2 conversion rate451,516 or invoke greater selectivity504,517 of the thermocatalytic process.
 Fig. 63  (a) Electronic energy distribution upon LSPR excitation and its relaxation processes (Reproduced from ref. 512 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2015). Initially, the electrons follow a Fermi–Dirac distribution at the thermal temperature of the system. The photoexcitation of the electrons from the filled states to the unfilled states generates a non-thermal electron distribution (0–10 fs). The electron distribution thermalizes to an elevated electron temperature through electron–electron scattering (500 fs). The hot electron distribution cools over time as it couples with the substrate phonons (1 ps). (b) Plasmon-induced hot carrier generation and hot electron transfer/back-transfer processes in clean metal, metal/adsorbate, and metal/semiconductor systems. In each case, the left component (relative to energy y-axis) displays the Fermi level (EF) of the plasmonic metals and the right component (relative to energy y-axis) illustrates the HOMOs and LUMOs for the adsorbates, or the CB and VB positions for the semiconductors. The gray regions represent the population of the electronic states (Reproduced from ref. 506 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018). 
3.5.1.2 Driving forces in photothermal CO2 catalysis. 
Considering the published systems, two commonalities are apparent during photothermal catalysis, namely, “light–matter interactions” and “thermal activation”, which could be referred to as the origins of “photo” and “thermal”, respectively. Fig. 64 details the driving forces behind the thermal/photochemical contributions to photothermal catalysis and their respective underlying principles. The thermal contribution stems from either external physical heating or, ideally, the photothermal heating effect in the plasmonic nanostructures. It plays a role in thermally activating the reactants and intermediates (overcoming activation energy, Ea) with the assistance of surface functional sites (path a in Fig. 64). The photochemical contributions can originate from photocatalysis by a semiconductor or plasmonic photocatalysis by a metallic nanostructure. The gaseous photocatalytic reduction of CO2 over a semiconductor with the aid of appropriate heating has been recently described as a potentially economic and scalable (non-metal component) solar-driven CO2 conversion technology.518 The mechanism involves a redox reaction initiated by the electron–hole pairs (path f in Fig. 64) but under thermal activation.519 Plasmonic photocatalysis initiated by LSPR excitation in metallic nanostructures is experiencing increasing interest in traditional gaseous catalytic reaction systems due to its promotional role in well-known surface catalytic processes and distinctive activation mechanisms.520–522 Physical plasmonic photocatalytic mechanisms can be classified into one of the following two systems: (i) direct plasmonic photocatalysis on a metal–adsorbate array, and (ii) indirect plasmonic photocatalysis on a semiconductor in conjunction with energy transfer from the metal to the semiconductor. In detail, direct plasmonic catalysis is initiated by either (i) non-radiative Landau damping (where electrons are initially generated in the metal and are subsequently transferred to the adsorbate), or (ii) chemical interface damping (where electrons are instantaneously injected into the unoccupied orbitals of the adsorbates)505 (path b and c in Fig. 64). Two pathways are also available for indirect plasmonic catalysis: (i) DET via electron injection from the metal nanoparticles to the semiconductor, or (ii) RET from the metal nanoparticles to the semiconductor (near-field electromagnetic or phonon scattering mechanisms), and the generation of electron–hole pairs in the semiconductor507,523 (path d and e in Fig. 64). The net effect of the energy transfer processes is the generation of more electrons in the semiconductor. It is highly dependent on the catalyst architecture and components as to whether the above-mentioned processes can occur. In addition, light excitation in a single system, for instance, broad range light illumination in a composite catalyst system, can induce multiple phenomena, which can interact beneficially with each other.496,497,524
 Fig. 64  Driving forces behind photothermal catalytic CO2 reduction, including: (a) thermal activation, (b) Landau damping,525 (c) chemical interface damping (Reproduced from ref. 525 with permission from The American Association for the Advancement of Science, copyright 2018), (d) direct energy transfer,523 (e) resonant energy transfer (Reproduced from ref. 523 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2012), and (f) electron–hole pair generation. 
3.5.1.3 Classification of photothermal CO2 catalysis. 
Two types of photothermal CO2 catalysis can be defined (Fig. 65). The first is the case where photochemistry seldom contributes and thermally-driven processes govern the surface catalytic reaction. Catalyst performance originates from a localized photo-heating effect on the nanostructure526 to power conventional thermal CO2 catalysis.527,528 This type of reaction experiences no benefits from photocatalysis and is subject to the same intrinsic catalytic parameters (activation barrier/selectivity/T-dependent kinetics) as that of conventional thermocatalysis529 and is suggested to be termed as “PTH” CO2 catalysis. The second type of photothermal CO2 catalysis involves the application of light-induced chemistries (for example, hot electron photochemistry) to promote/tailor CO2 catalysis with the assistance of heating. A unique feature of this situation is that it draws on both thermal and photochemical contributions in a single system500,530 and is suggested to be termed as “PTD” CO2 catalysis. Both PTH and PTD CO2 catalysis have attracted extensive attention and appear to be promising approaches for the solar-assisted chemical transformation of CO2. The ensuing review primarily focuses on the mechanistic understanding and experimental progress in the PTD approach as it offers greater flexibility in integrating photochemical contributions with conventional thermal CO2 catalysis.
 Fig. 65  Graphic depicting photothermal CO2 catalysis classifications. 
In a system powered by both thermal and photochemical activation (for example, hot electron chemistry), one effect may dominate catalytic conversion while the other assists the process. Consequently, PTD catalytic systems can be divided into two groups: (i) light-assisted thermocatalysis and (ii) heat-assisted photocatalysis. The former case has been mainly observed for gaseous CO2 reduction catalysts where plasmonic metals are present. Without LSPR excitation, CO2 catalysis can continue to proceed at a remarkable reaction rate, driven by the thermal activation pathway. Light acts as a promoter to extend the capability of the reaction via photochemistry effects. The latter case involves photocatalytic CO2 reduction (predominantly under visible light) operating with the assistance of heat. Here, photogenerated carriers are the main driving force for the CO2 reduction reaction, while the thermal contribution (i.e., in reactant cleavage,524 activation barrier overcome, population of excited vibrational states,520 and species desorption518) plays an important role in maintaining an acceptable reaction rate. Removing the thermal contribution may result in a sharp decline in the activity (several orders of magnitude lower than that with heating); despite this, the operational heating condition (<150 °C) is typically insufficient to solely drive the CO2 catalytic reduction at a significant rate.


3.5.2 Latest progress in photothermal CO2 reduction. 
Early examples of light-assisted CO2 thermocatalytic reactions can be found almost two decades ago, in a study by Grätzel and co-workers in 1987 (over TiO2-supported Ru/RuOx using a xenon lamp as the excitation source).283 An inspiring work in 2001 by Ljungström and co-workers stressed on the potential of using non-thermal and selective excitation (for example, by forming transient radicals) to invoke low-temperature catalysis.495 However, applying light-induced chemistries to thermal catalytic reactions only found significant traction once reports suggesting plasmon-generated hot electrons on plasmonic nanostructure can interact with surface adsorbates directly (via electron injection from metal to adsorbates520,521) or indirectly (via hot electron transfer on metal/semiconductor heterostructure506) began to surface. The first instance where the photothermal approach was used to realise the complete reduction of CO2 to solar fuels (CH4) involved a Ni–silica–alumina catalyst in 2014.531 The system, in essence, represented direct plasmonic photocatalysis by a Ni nanostructure with the assistance of heat (T ≈ 150 °C). Meanwhile, a single-component semiconductor photo-catalyst (driven by the bandgap excitation of In2O3−x(OH)y) for CO2 reduction in the gas-phase under UV-visible light with moderate heating (T ≈ 150 °C) was reported.518 The reports illustrate two classic examples of heat-assisted photocatalysis. At around the same time, two early studies of LSPR-enhanced thermocatalysis (light-assisted thermocatalysis) were reported over Au nanoparticles for CO2 reduction by methane532 and RWGS reaction.533
3.5.2.1 CO2 hydrogenation via the photothermal route. 
CO2 hydrogenation is regarded as a key option in terms of approaches for converting CO2 into chemical necessities, especially when advances in producing green hydrogen are realised.534 Thermodynamic and kinetic considerations of the governing reactions and reaction intermediates are vital for further improving the catalytic performance for CO2 hydrogenation. The choice of temperature and pressure conditions relies mostly on the thermodynamic features and overall gas volume change during CO2 hydrogenation: CO2 methanation (exothermic), CO2-to-methanol (exothermic), and RWGS (endothermic). The use of high pressures and low temperatures should be favorable for the CO2 methanation and methanol-production processes. At milder and more economically favorable temperatures, CO2 methanation and methanol-production become kinetically limited, while thermodynamics limits the RWGS process. The kinetic limitations are highly relevant to the elevated activation barrier of the intermediary steps, e.g., HCOO* hydrogenation in methanol production over a Cu–ZnO catalyst and C–O cleavage of HCO* species in methane production on a Rh surface.504 Increasing the temperature can help to overcome the intermediary activation barriers, despite the expense of selectivity, catalyst stability, and lifetime.495 In this regard, light-induced chemistries on plasmonic nanostructures serve as a promising approach to tackle thermodynamic and kinetic limitations of thermally-driven systems so as to achieve low-temperature and solar-assisted catalytic CO2 reduction.530,535,536
As mentioned before, LSPR excitation in metallic nanostructures and the subsequent dephasing processes serve as a promising approach to extend the capability of conventional CO2 catalytic reaction systems. Along with the discovery of the stimulative role of Ni531/Au532,533 LSPR excitation in CO2 hydrogenation, the optical excitation of Al,517,520 Pd,498,537 Ru,489,538 Ag,524 and Cu539 nanostructures with a plasmonic resonance (Fig. 66) in the visible light range have been successively reported for enhanced CO2 hydrogenation. The plasmonic excitation of Al enhancing the catalytic CO2 hydrogenation to CO on an adjacent semiconductor was recently reported by Halas's group (Fig. 67a–c).517 They found that the photogenerated hot electrons in the Al could deposit their energy into the vibrational mode of C–O, causing vibrational excitation and C–O bond elongation, following transfer to the adjacent Cu2O semiconductor. An overlap of the Al optical plasmonic absorption spectra and the measured EQE strongly indicated that the improved catalytic performance was derived from the excitation of the Al quadrupolar plasmon mode. They also described an ingenious antenna-reactor nanostructure where the photon energy could be assimilated (antenna), transferred (interface), and utilized (reactor) for chemical conversion in the form of energetic electrons. Other studies demonstrated that the excitation of the plasmonic components in the commonly-studied methanol catalysts Pd/ZnO537 and Cu/ZnO could facilitate methanol production. In the case of Cu–ZnO, a wavelength-dependent behavior observed during photothermal methanol synthesis (Fig. 67d and e) revealed a synergism between the pivotal adsorbed intermediate species (e.g., HCOO* and H*) under UV-vis light illumination at the interfacial perimeter.496 Rh nanoparticles possessing a controlled size and shape, which enabled a strong LSPR absorption peak at 334 nm (3.71 eV), were studied as plasmonic photo-catalysts for CO2 hydrogenation.504 It was demonstrated that photo-generated hot electrons on the Rh nanostructure, upon illumination by either ultraviolet (365 nm) or blue (460 nm) LED light sources, could transfer into the C–O π* anti-bonding orbitals with a surmountable energy barrier and matched orbital levels, thus selectively enhancing CO2 methanation over the CO-production pathway (Fig. 67f–h). The optical excitation of Ru (which exhibits weak intra-band and inter-band absorption) was also reported to promote CO2 activation on its surface.489 After comparing different metal systems, which were characterised by different band structures (HOMO–LUMO gap) of adsorbed CO2, it was concluded that, irrespective of the absorption property of clean metal particle, hot electron generation in the system is relevant to the HOMO–LUMO energy gap of the CO2 adsorbed on the metal surface. To confirm this, in the in situ UV-vis spectrum, an absorption peak at 528 nm following CO2 adsorption on the catalyst surface was identified (with no similar absorption peak on a clean Ru metal surface). This molecular activation mechanism likely falls into the scope of direct intramolecular excitation of HOMO-to-LUMO transfer induced by the photoexcitation of the Ru–CO2(ads.) hybrid orbitals.540
 Fig. 66  Optical absorption spectra of metallic nanostructures in the visible light range. Experimental and theoretically (Mie's theory) calculated optical absorption spectra of embedded (a) copper and (b) silver nanoclusters in soda-lime glass (Reproduced from ref. 541 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2003). (c) Extinction spectra of Rh nanocubes in ethanol solution (black) and on a porous Al2O3 support (blue) (Reproduced from ref. 504 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). (d) Pd nanocubes with average sizes of 25 and 50 nm (Reproduced from ref. 510 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2005). (e) Normalized extinction spectra of 200 nm diameter nickel disks (left), of elliptical nickel disks with polarization along the short axis (b = 190 nm, center), and of elliptical nickel disks with polarization along the long axis (a = 300 nm, right) (Reproduced from ref. 509 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, copyright 2011). (f) UV-vis tuning of aluminum plasmons (left). Experimental dark-field spectra of individual nanodisks with D = 70, 80, 100, 120, 130, 150, 180 nm (center). SEM micrographs of the corresponding nanodisk structures. Scale bar is 100 nm (right). Simulations of the nanodisk spectra, assuming a 3 nm surface oxide and an SiO2 substrate (Reproduced from ref. 508 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2014). 
 Fig. 67  LSPR-assisted CO2 hydrogenation reaction performance over the Rh/Al2O3 (a–c), Al@Cu2O (d–f), and Cu/ZnO/Al2O3 (g and h) catalysts. (a) Rates of CH4 (green) and CO (black) production at 623 K on Rh/Al2O3 (solid lines) and Al2O3 (dotted lines) under dark and ultraviolet illumination at 3 W cm−2. CH4 production is strongly and selectively enhanced by ultraviolet light on the Rh photo-catalyst. (b) Selectivity towards CH4 of the thermo- (black circles) and photocatalytic reactions under ultraviolet (365 nm, red squares) and blue (460 nm, blue triangles) illumination as a function of temperature under H2-rich (CO2:H2 = 1:5.5, solid symbols) and H2-deficient (CO2:H2 = 1:3.1, open symbols) conditions. (c) LDOS for adsorbed CHO on C(pz), O(pz), and Rh(d) orbitals. The Rh(100) surface is perpendicular to the x direction and the C–O bond is along the y direction. Major bands are identified as (1) C–O p bonding band (6.5 eV) with C(pz) (black) and O(pz) (red) interactions; (2) C–O p* anti-bonding band (1–3 eV, mode around 2 eV) with C(pz) and O(pz) interactions (Reproduced from ref. 504 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). (d) Numerically calculated local electric field strength |E(r)|2 in the Al core (left axis) and the Cu2O shell (right axis). (e) MeOH selectivity/space time yield (STY) in the range of 200–300 °C under dark and 350–800 nm light irradiation (red dotted box indicates the highest photo-enhancement at 225 °C). The error bars indicate the deviation among three independent experiments; (f) time-on-stream MeOH yield under different light irradiation conditions (350–800 nm, 200–500 nm, and 420–800 nm, which corresponds to the excitation of Cu, ZnO, and Cu + ZnO, respectively). P = 21 bar, T = 225 °C, light intensity = 600 mW cm−2 for all the spectral ranges (Reproduced from ref. 496 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2020). (g) Typical gas chromatogram of the chamber output during light (7 W cm−2) and thermally-driven (350 °C) rWGS on Al@Cu2O. (h) The measured EQE as a function of illumination wavelength for oxide-supported Al@Cu2O compared to Al vs. illumination wavelength (Reproduced from ref. 517 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). 
Beyond the LSPR-induced chemistries, efforts have also been devoted to the study/improvement of photocatalytic gas-phase CO2 reduction on semiconductors at an elevated temperature, where the electron–hole pairs from bandgap excitation (usually with visible light illumination) drive the reaction. For instance, the strategy used to promote heat-assisted photocatalytic CO2 reduction by defective In2O3 by doping an optimum amount of Bi3+ into the In2O3−x(OH)y lattice. The increased Lewis basicity and acidity of FLPs served to increase the splitting of H2 and the RWGS reaction rate.542 To induce better light absorption by indium oxide, black 2D In2O3−x nanosheets prepared by photoinduced defect engineering were reported to possess excellent light harvesting and CO2 adsorption abilities, delivering 103.21 mmol gcat.−1 h−1 CO yield in a 300 W Xe lamp-powered batch reactor system (350 °C achieved without external heating).543 Similarly, a recent study showed that the thermal hydrogenation treatment of pale yellow In2O3 at 400 °C could transform it into non-stoichiometric black In2O3−x, where a three order enhancement in the performance magnitude was achieved (relative to stoichiometric In2O3)494 (Fig. 68). The presence of oxygen vacancies and dynamic amorphization processes were confirmed by Raman spectroscopy, in situ XPS, and in situ HRETEM (Fig. 68a–f). Under photothermal conditions, a substantial enhancement in the CO production rate (approximately 417%) under illumination was achieved at 300 °C compared to the dark (non-illuminated) reaction conditions (Fig. 68g). The elevated heat-assisted photocatalytic performance was attributed to the higher population of oxygen defects, serving as unsaturated states for trapping photo-generated electrons/holes (Fig. 68h). The long-term stability of the photo-catalyst performance under light illumination was examined where there was no significant drop in the CO generation rate over 70 h on stream (Fig. 68i). Another successful example of utilizing a semiconductor for photothermal CO2 hydrogenation was demonstrated for silicon-based nanostructures. In addition to being a single-component photo-catalyst (hydride-terminated silicon nanocrystals544), the photogenerated electron–hole pairs in Si have also been used to thermally or photochemically facilitate CO2 reduction on the hybrid structures (Ru/Si,497 RuO2/Si,545 and In2O3/Si546). Other semiconductors, such as WO3547 and MoO3−x,548 have also been appraised for photocatalytic CO2 reduction at an elevated temperature. The introduction of oxygen defects was confirmed to be pivotal for both an extended absorption of visible (to IR) light and CO2 activation/reduction.
 Fig. 68  Non-stoichiometric black In2O3 as a catalyst for the heat-assisted photocatalytic RWGS reaction. In situ high-resolution images of a stoichiometric In2O3 nanocrystal at 400 °C (a) under an N2 atmosphere and then switched to an H2 atmosphere for the following times: (b) 5 min, (c) 10 min, and (d) 20 min. Scale bar in d applies to all the images; green squares indicate the formation of an amorphous phase. Graphical representation of the (e) original and (f) treated In2O3, where the blue region, pink dots, yellow dots, and yellow circles represent the amorphous phase, In atoms, O atoms, and [O], respectively. (g) Catalytic performance for S4 in a flow reactor at different temperatures, both with and without light irradiation; inset is the enlarged view of the catalytic performance for 200, 225, and 250 °C. (h) The In2O3−x/In2O3 heterostructure showing the In(III)′, [O] electron-trapping, and O′ hole-trapping mid-gap energy states near the CB and the VB edges. (i) Stability test for a hydrogenated sample (at 400 °C) in a flow reactor at 300 °C with light irradiation for 70 h (Reproduced from ref. 494 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2020). 
It is worth mentioning that a range of unique nanostructures that favor light-to-heat transfer and heat-retention have been appraised for photothermal heating CO2 catalysis. An early example demonstrated the ability of Group VIII nanocatalysts in converting CO2 into the solar fuel with high selectivity and conversion (both >90%) (under a 300 W Xe lamp). After that, catalysts, such as the Al2O3-supported CoFe alloy,529 Ru-loaded ultrathin layered double hydroxides,549 and Pd@Nb2O5,528 have exhibited both a good photothermal heating effect (achieving over 300 °C) and a high CO2 hydrogenation activity under illumination by a 300 W Xe lamp. Li et al.548 recently demonstrated a light absorber-assisted, sunlight-driven CO2 methanation array over a Y2O3-supported single-atom Ni catalyst (SA Ni/Y2O3). Combining the excellent thermocatalytic activity of SA Ni/Y2O3 (with a Tlight-off = 180 °C) with the sunlight-selective light absorber (AlNx/Al foil), the system provided an impressive ∼100% methanation selectivity and ∼87% CO2 conversion at 240 °C under only 0.52 to 0.7 kW m−2 of light intensity.
3.5.2.2 Dry reforming of CO2 and methane via the photothermal route. 
As a high operating temperature (700–1000 °C, intensive energy input) is typically required for any appreciable activity by the DRM reaction, the notion of integrating solar energy into the system is highly attractive. Introducing plasmonic (unreactive) gold metal deposits into the Rh/SBA-15532 and Pt/SiO2550 catalysts facilitated a 1.2–1.7 times improvement in the CO2 conversion for the DRM reaction upon visible light illumination at a comparatively lower reaction temperature (400–500 °C). A similar observation was reported for a functionalized monometallic plasmonic Ni catalyst supported on mesoporous silica.551 It was claimed that the rate-determining step for CO2 activation was improved by energetic electrons from the LSPR and the activation barrier was greatly lowered.550 This implies that the electronic excitation induced photochemical/photocatalytic contribution may pave the way for further mitigation of the temperature requirements by DRM reaction. This was also echoed in the bandgap-excitation-driven heat-assisted photocatalytic DRM system over the Pt/black TiO2 catalyst.552 Under simulated solar light illumination, the catalytic activity was observed at 350 °C, where at 550 °C, appreciable CO2 conversion (158 mmol h−1 gcat.−1), which is three orders of magnitude larger than that of the reported photocatalytic DRM system, was apparent. Strong visible light absorption by the black TiO2 was ascribed to be responsible for the photocatalytic contribution to the system.


3.5.3 Summary and outlook of photothermal CO2 catalysis. 
In summary, the physical understanding of LSPR excitation and its subsequent dephasing processes, which serve as one of the important mediators for chemical utilization of solar energy in CO2 catalysis, was initially discussed. Subsequently, to provide a clear demonstration of the motivations behind and the classifications of photothermal CO2 catalysis, different driving forces for photothermal CO2 catalysis were summarized and classified into one of the two groups (PTH and PTD), depending on whether photochemical contributions are involved or not. PTD CO2 catalysis was then further classified into light-assisted thermocatalysis and heat-assisted photocatalysis. Finally, the recent progress in photothermal CO2 catalysis (including CO2 hydrogenation and DRM reaction) from the perspective of materials engineering and mechanistic elucidations was reviewed. Broadly, the studied photothermal CO2 reduction catalysts are mainly derived from catalysts that have been previously considered for conventional thermal-catalytic CO2 reduction, such as Ni/Cu/Pd/Ru/Rh and defective-rich In2O3,553 which could also absorb visible light. Within these catalysts, hot electrons in the metallic component are generated upon light illumination, which can potentially accelerate the rate-determining steps or alter the energetics of intermediate reactions (where excited state species are involved). Heat-assisted photocatalysis using active narrow-bandgap semiconductors such as surface modified MoO3,548 WO3,547 or silicon nanocrystals544 were also studied. Overall, the design of plasmonic metal/semiconductor hybrid materials with tailor-made architectures is a promising approach for photothermal CO2 reduction catalysis with multiple benefits: (i) moderate photothermal heating from the metal is sufficient for activating important molecules (i.e., H2) with localised and fast heat-up;524 (ii) interaction between the semiconductor and the strong SPR-induced electric fields close to the metallic nanostructures increases the electron–hole pair formation for photocatalytic CO2 reduction; (iii) generated hot electrons accelerate the intermediary reactions on metal surfaces/interfaces496,504via the direct plasmonic photocatalysis phenomenon; (iv) the generated hot electrons accelerate the intermediary reactions on the semiconductor surface517via plasmon-induced interfacial charge-transfer transition (indirect plasmonic photocatalysis approach). Strategies such as alloying a metal catalyst with a plasmonic metal, defect engineering, and the creation of surface functional groups have been employed to further improve the photothermal catalysis of the CO2 reduction reaction.
Importantly, to rationally design or tailor the catalyst properties, it is mandatory to develop a fundamental understanding of the currently-observed experimental phenomena comprising (i) photothermal heating effect and hot-electron-induced non-thermal contribution; (ii) light-induced chemistries behind the observed photo-enhancement over supported plasmonic nanostructure catalysts; (iii) origins of the temperature-dependent photo-thermal performance. In particular, the non-thermal effect lowers the apparent Ea of the CO2 reduction reaction and exhibits a linear to superlinear relation between the AQE (rate performance) and the photon flux.520 In contrast, the catalyst temperature increases from photothermal heating, which usually follows a sublinear relation with the illumination intensity.554 It is thus practical to differentiate the contributions from the plots of rate vs. photon flux. In addition, a reactor system that can eliminate heat accumulation and favors heat dissipation (i.e., a flow reactor, small amount of catalyst, and low illumination intensity) is normally dominated by the non-thermal contribution. Li et al.538 recently developed a direct and indirect illumination technique to extract the contribution of the non-thermal plasmonic effect. Knowing that a non-thermal contribution is involved, ascertaining in which way hot electrons are influencing the surface catalytic CO2 reduction reaction is essential. Existing observations pointing towards three mechanisms: reactant activation, accelerating a certain intermediary step (RDS-relevant), or regulating surface chemistry. For example, on the Ru metal surface, the cleavage of CO2 into CO* was verified (via DRIFTS technique) to be promoted within the considered temperature range upon LSPR excitation.489 The increased CH4 production on Rh under illumination was ascribed to the plasmon-enhanced selective C–O bond weakening within the CHO* species (supported by DFT calculations).504 Beyond this, light-induced variations in the physicochemical properties of the catalyst (i.e., defects555 and carbon deposition556) could provide another explanation for enhanced catalytic performance (i.e., rate and stability). Finally, temperature-dependent behaviors during photothermal CO2 reduction, such as the linear relation between non-thermal AQE and catalyst temperature538 or the decreased rate enhancement factor at higher temperatures,533 have been observed.
