The former effect has been rationalized by an increase in the relative population of the adsorbate-excited vibrational states, resulting in less energy to overcome the energy barrier for plasmonic photocatalysis (non-thermal contribution) at higher temperatures, which enables a linear relation between non-thermal AQE and temperature.538,554 In the latter case, the decreased contribution of the non-thermal effect can be correlated with the dominance of the thermocatalytic process and decreased coverage of the surface intermediates at a higher temperature.496 Further experimental evidence is needed to support these inferences and to establish more comprehensive and robust understandings of the cooperative role of heat and light.557 Heterogeneous CO2 catalysis with a single activation mechanism is already a complicated process; the coupling of multiple driving forces adds more complexity to physical studies.496,524 Ensuing studies should place emphasis on unveiling the dynamic atomic catalyst surface variations/molecular-level surface catalytic processes and interplays between different activation mechanisms with the assistance of in situ/operando characterization techniques.

3.6. CO2 as a soft oxidant
CO2 utilization has been focused on its use with a reductant, mostly hydrogen. However, the current major source of hydrogen highly relies on the reforming of methane, naphtha, and alcohols with low carbon numbers and oxygenates such as dimethylether, resulting in the co-production of CO. This makes the conversion of CO2 into chemicals and fuels uneconomical if renewable H2 is not used. It has been proposed that CO2 reduction could be possibly achieved not only by using hydrogen but also by saturated hydrocarbons, such as light paraffins and alkylaromatics. Simultaneously, these saturated hydrocarbons could be converted into the corresponding olefinic compounds, which are basic industrial chemicals such as ethylene, propylene, butenes (including butadiene), and styrene, which are produced mainly through cracking and simple dehydrogenation or oxidative dehydrogenation using molecular oxygen. However, these olefins were proposed to be generated by oxidative dehydrogenation using CO2, during which CO2 plays a role as an oxidant to directly abstract hydrogen from the saturated bond and/or capturing hydrogen molecules, which are produced through simple dehydrogenation as follows: CnH2n+2 + CO2 → CnH2n + CO + H2O(5)or CnH2n+2 → CnH2n + H2(6) CO2 + H2 → H2O + CO(7)
CO2 utilization, as a soft oxidant, enhances the catalytic oxidative transformations, and is enjoying rapid growth in industrialization, and could greatly contribute to carbon emission mitigation and economical energy efficient fabrication of several chemicals.558,559 CO2 plays an important role in hydrogen abstraction from functionalized or simple hydrocarbons via CO2 catalytic activation to form oxygen species and CO (Fig. 69).560,561 The economic efficiency, non-toxicity, mild oxidizing properties, and abundant availability of CO2 have led to the intensive investigation on its utilization as a soft oxidant in various catalytic reactions, including the oxidative dehydrogenation of alkyl aromatics and alkanes as well as methane oxidative coupling.562,563 Notably, Park and co-workers first reported the idea of CO2 utilization as a soft oxidant for the oxidative dehydrogenation of ethyl benzene to styrene, with solely CO2 instead of steam over the oxygen-deficient Fe-based catalysts, resulting in both the enhancement in the activity and selectivity together with high stability.564 The oxidizability of CO2 has been rarely studied. To the best of our knowledge, the first report on carbon oxidizability reported the order O2(105) ≫ H2O(3) > CO2(1) ≫ H2(0.003) at 800 °C and 0.1 atm for carbon oxidizability.565 Since then, CO2 has been coined as a mild oxidant, non-traditional oxidant, unusual oxidant, and an oxygen transfer agent.566
 Fig. 69  (a) The role of CO2 in abstracting hydrogen from functionalized or simple hydrocarbons via CO2 catalytic activation to form the oxygen species and CO (Reproduced from ref. 567 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2016); (b) styrene yield in ethylbenzene dehydrogenation reaction over the Ce–K–Fe2O3 catalyst using CO2 and steam as the oxidants. The thermodynamic equilibrium was referred to from the work of Mimura.568 
It is of significant importance to utilize CO2 in catalysis with superior merits, including reaction rate acceleration, total oxidation suppression, chemical equilibrium alleviation, product selectivity enhancement, and hot spot prevention on the surface of the catalyst.569,570 The diversities in the catalytic performance shed light on the behavioral aspects of CO2 and its activation, the importance of surface oxygen species, the surface properties of catalysts, the role of supports, and the distinct mechanistic pathways that form the evolution basis of the oxidation reaction (depicted in eqn (7)–(10)), which are of vital importance for CO2 utilization as a soft oxidant.571,572 In 2012, Park and co-workers presented a systematic review tracing the emergence, development, and industrialization of the scheme based on CO2 as a soft oxidant.558 Due to its lower oxidization ability, CO2 suppresses the unwanted total oxidation products, poisons the non-selective catalysts sites, and thus results in improved selectivity.573 CO2 acts as a gas-mediated modification of the bulk composition and/or catalyst surface in various catalytic reactions, affecting the electronic, structural, diffusion, acid–base, redox, and adsorption properties.574 CO2 + MO(x−1) → MOx + CO(8) H2 + MOx → MO(x−1) + H2O(9) CO2 + H2 → H2O + CO(7) C + CO2 → CO(10)
The lattice/surface oxygen contributes to oxygen donation from the metal oxide lattice, reducing the higher valence states to lower valence oxides with water generation so as to abstract hydrogen from the substrate in oxidative dehydrogenation. Subsequently, CO2 re-oxides the lower valence metal oxide back to its original state. Yao and co-workers investigated the surface properties that govern the selectivity of Mo2C catalysts with CO2 as a soft oxidant in ethane oxidative dehydrogenation. Oxygen modification is essential to inhibit the C–C bond cleavage in ethane and to enhance ethylene production.575 Nowicka and co-workers synthesized an Al, Zr, and Ce-containing mixed oxide support for propane oxidative dehydrogenation with CO2 as a soft oxidant, which possessed both basic and acidic sites with superior selectivity and long-term stability to propylene. The dissociation of CO2 over the reduced catalyst leads to the formation of selective oxygen species and CO, regeneration of selective oxygen species, and equilibrium shift for alkane dehydrogenation via hydrogen consumption through the RWGS reaction.576
Acid–base bifunctionality is of significant importance in CO2-mediated oxidative dehydrogenation. The basic properties are favorable for hydrogen atoms and CO2 activation, whereas the acid sites present on the catalysts play a predominant role in substrate activation. Kainthla and co-workers employed a novel MoO3/TiO2–Al2O3 catalyst using CO2 as a soft oxidant for ethylbenzene oxidative dehydrogenation to styrene (Fig. 70), which exhibited superior activity for 40 h with a maximum styrene selectivity of ∼97% and ethylbenzene conversion of ∼70%. Surface acidic sites, strong interaction with TiO2–Al2O3 highly dispersed active species of MoO3, and excellent physical properties greatly contributed to the remarkable activity.577
 Fig. 70  Pictorial representation of the plausible reaction pathway for the dehydrogenation of ethylbenzene to styrene over the MoO3-based catalyst (Reproduced from ref. 577 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017). 
The redox properties of the catalyst as a mediator for oxygen transfer from CO2 to hydrocarbon in oxidative dehydrogenation are of paramount importance. Ren and co-workers systematically prepared and evaluated MnOx catalysts with CO2 as a soft oxidant for ethylbenzene oxidative dehydrogenation, exhibiting the highest ethylbenzene conversion of 60%. The redox mechanism involves lattice oxygen. The SSA and the amount of mobile oxygen species are key factors for determining the catalytic activity.578
The support providing high SSA, porosity, and immobilization with highly dispersed surface-active sites with large fractions, which reactants have easy access to for adsorption is ideal for overcoming the diffusion problems. Wang's group fabricated a flower-like Al2O3 support for the iron oxide catalyst using CO2 as a soft oxidant for ethylbenzene oxidative dehydrogenation. The high concentration of O2− anions on the flower-like support surface resulted in abundant highly-dispersed FexOy species on the support, which exhibited superior activity.579 Li and co-workers investigated oxide-supported NiFe bimetallic catalysts using CO2 as a soft oxidant for the oxidative dehydrogenation of n-butane to 1,3-butadiene. The FeOx/Ni(111) promotes the CeH bond cleavage while Ni(111) favors CeC bond scission. The highly reducible supports, such as CeO2–ZrO2 and CeO2, enhance the production of 1,3-butadiene.580 The utilization of CO2 as a soft oxidant for olefins is depicted in Fig. 71.
 Fig. 71  CO2 uses as a soft oxidant for olefinic basic chemicals. 

3.6.1 Oxidative dehydrogenation of light hydrocarbons. 
CO2 has been found to reduce carbon deposition and assists in the rapid desorption of ethylene from the catalyst surface. The enhancement in the ethylene productivity by CO2 was also observed over a wide range of bulk metal oxides (e.g., Ga2O3, Cr2O3, and V2O5). Park et al.581 proposed that the promotional effect of CO2 on the catalyst stability could be enhanced using cerium–zirconia oxide as a support due to its higher CO2 adsorption capability due to its surface basicity. However, it is still a challenging task to design catalytic systems with high product yield and better catalytic stability so that the oxidative dehydrogenation of ethane with CO2 will go beyond the current steam cracking technology.
For propane dehydrogenation, usually the non-oxidative path is followed, which results in the quick deactivation of the catalyst and the loss of valuable products. The challenge is the selection of such a catalyst and an oxidant that leads to enhanced catalytic activity and propylene selectivity, and to avoid coke formation. To overcome these limitations, CO2 is considered as a much safer, weaker, and easily available soft oxidant that can replace oxygen.582,583 For instance, Hutchings et al.576 illustrated that CO2, in the presence of the Ga2O3/TiO2 catalyst, contributes mainly to the RWGS reaction, which leads to enhanced dehydrogenation activity, while utilizing CO2 with the Ga2O3/ZrO2 catalyst system leads to coke removal (through reverse Boudouard reaction) and increased catalyst stability.
Butadiene is one of the basic petrochemical products that are widely used in the production of butadiene–styrene rubber and plastics with special mechanical properties. In 1980, most of the butadiene was produced via steam cracking from naphtha but catalytic dehydrogenation has recently attracted much attention as an alternative route. n-Butane is a relatively strong reducing agent than C1–C3 hydrocarbons, which might prevent the re-activation (i.e., re-oxidation) of the catalyst by the CO2 oxidant. Instead, the addition of a small amount of CO2 into the O2 atmosphere dramatically enhanced the selectivity towards butadiene with a slight loss in butane conversion in the n-butane dehydrogenation reaction over the titanium pyrophosphate catalysts. This is attributed to the competitive adsorption of O2 and CO2 on the catalyst surface and the decrease in the amount of adsorbed O2, which might be responsible for the over-oxidation reaction.


3.6.2 Oxidative dehydrogenation of alkylaromatics with CO2. 
Initially, CO2 utilization in ethylbenzene dehydrogenation was limited because CO2 was commonly known as a catalyst-poisoning agent. Since the 1990s, however, several research teams have demonstrated the enhancement effect of CO2 on the catalytic performance in the ethylbenzene dehydrogenation reaction. CO2 could be activated by the basic catalyst to react with hydrogen, which shifted the dehydrogenation reaction to the right side. Carbon dioxide functioned as a soft oxidant in the redox cycle of the metal oxides to retain their highest oxidation states. Therefore, styrene yield could be improved 2.5 times by the addition of CO2. Another role of carbon dioxide is to enhance the equilibrium conversion of ethylbenzene by combining CO2 reduction with styrene oxidative dehydrogenation reaction.
It has been proposed that the dehydrogenation of ethylbenzene using CO2 oxidants may proceed via two pathways, i.e., one-step and two-step routes. In case of the one-step route, CO2 directly interacts with ethylbenzene to abstract hydrogen from the ethyl moiety with the concomitant formation of CO and H2O, as shown in the following eqn (11). (11)
In the case of the two-step route, ethylbenzene is first converted to styrene and hydrogen, and the formed hydrogen then reacts with CO2via the RWGS (eqn (12)). (12) (13)
Generally, both mechanisms participate in the dehydrogenation reaction, and enhanced activity and selectivity are obtained through the utilization of CO2 towards oxidative dehydrogenation by the simultaneous activation of both paraffin and CO2 as well as the removal of the H2 by-product through RWGS with CO2. Ge et al. studied the roles of CO2 in the oxy-dehydrogenation of ethylbenzene to styrene on the CO2(111) surface by DFT, claiming that the two pathways have almost the same activation energy (Fig. 72).584
 Fig. 72  The potential energy profile, the configuration of initial (IS), transition (TS) and final (FS) states of (a) the oxo-insertion pathway, (b) CO2 reaction with H via the RWGS pathway on the CeO2(111) surface (Reproduced from ref. 584 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2018). 
Since ethylbenzene could be dehydrogenated without the addition of CO2, eqn (6) should occur. CO2 addition accelerates the styrene formation rate either because the one-step route becomes accessible or because of the shifting of the equilibrium towards the product side via the RWGS (eqn (13)). The former case produced no hydrogen during the dehydrogenation reaction, while the latter pathway generated H2. In general, both the routes may occur simultaneously and the relative contribution of the two pathways is difficult to separate.
In spite of dedicated research, only few processes at present based on the utilization of CO2 as a soft oxidant have been implemented at a technical scale.585 CO2-mediated oxidative dehydrogenation has emerged as an environment-friendly alternative to the conventional dehydrogenation processes, the recent progress was summarized by Mukherjee and co-workers in 2016.567 Numerous studies on CO2-mediated oxidative dehydrogenation have mainly focused on redox and acid–base catalysts and are restricted to the bench-scale. The primary challenges for commercial implementation involve the enhancement in the thermal stability of the catalyst, improvement in the catalytic performances per pass, and the increase in CO2 conversion with minimal coke deposition.567 Current research utilizing CO2 as a soft oxidant at the laboratory scale can be regarded as a foundation for future technologies to present chances and bring changes in the world economy.558,586 Park et al.587 proposed the process, ISOCO2, with the concept of retrofitting of the current simple dehydrogenation of ethylbenzene with the minimal process modification for replacing steam with CO2 (Fig. 73).
 Fig. 73  The schematic illustration of the ISOCO2 process.587 


3.6.3 Perspectives on CO2 as an oxygen source. 
The concept of soft oxidant is also extended to other reactions such as dehydrogenative polymerization, for example, the hydrosilane oxidation to silanol using CO2 as a soft oxidant or CO2-mediated dehydrogenative polymerization of hydrosilane to form polysilanes,588 and glycerol dehydration.589 In addition, other molecules such as sulfur, in the case of coupling of methane, give less activation energy and coke with stronger metal–sulfur bonding (for example, Pd16S7), which suppresses the over-oxidation of methane (2CH4 + S → C2H4 + 2H2S).590,591 N2O was also investigated to be effectively utilized as a soft oxidant in the oxidative dehydrogenation of ethylbenzene over alumina-supported iron oxide catalysts, in which the improved reducibility of iron oxide and the increased dispersion of the iron active species were ascribed to the presence of N2O.592 In the conversion of n-butane to 1,3-butadiene on Ni–Mo/Al2O3, N2O is demonstrated to be a soft oxidant such as CO2, which limits the formation of the electrophilic oxygen species, thus promoting the oxidation of alkenes to carbon oxides on the surface of the catalyst with increased selectivity towards butadiene.593
There is a study on the great challenge in both the activation of CO2 and CH4. CO2 activation on the catalyst surfaces is an essential elementary step in oxidative reactions. With an optimum ratio of substrate to CO2, alkali or alkaline earth metals could improve the catalytic activity as promoters in oxidative dehydrogenation reactions. The simultaneous C–H bond activation of CO2 and CH4, both with high stabilities, is regarded as one of the toughest obstacles in the field of catalysis. Park and co-workers successfully synthesized acetic acid over Cu-loaded M+-ZSM-5 catalysts through the concurrent feed of CH4 and CO2 (Fig. 74), with a remarkably rapid formation rate. The basic cation greatly contributes to the surface-activated CO2, and therefore, gave rise to the high insertion activity.594
 Fig. 74  Direct synthesis of acetic acid by the simultaneous co-activation of methane and CO2 over the Cu-exchanged ZSM-5 catalysts (Reproduced from ref. 594 with permission from Elsevier, copyright 2017). 
These approaches of utilization of CO2 as an oxidant, therefore, appear to be very promising from various viewpoints, such as being energy efficient by alleviating the equilibrium, allowing easy retrofitting modification of the current industries for commercialization, and the considerable consumption of CO2 with the production of CO as a by-product due to oxidative conversion. Moreover, these utilizations could be applied for the production of industrially valuable basic chemicals, such as olefinic monomers. The utilization of CO2 as an oxidant would be applicable more broadly for the formation of oxygenates and aromatics through oxygen insertion, aromatization, and dehydrogenation as well.
A summary that compares the many technologies with emphasis on the energy source, reducing agent, conversion conditions, representative catalyst, main products, as well as the performances is presented in Table 5. Currently, CO2 utilization using concentrated CO2 as a source can gain high selectivity, especially towards CO and HCOOH formation via two-electron transfer processes. The thermochemical conversion of CO2 as a conventional and mature technology could obtain a relatively high conversion ratio, while there is still room for other emerging processes to develop with future breakthroughs in reaction equipment optimization and advanced catalyst synthesis, thus showing great potential with the integration of renewable energy sources.

4. Process and economic aspects
Currently, the global CO2 emission is around 42 Gt per year.595 The latest released IPCC report on global warming of 1.5 °C has emphasized the urgency of addressing anthropogenic climate change.596,597 The world would have to curb CO2 emission by 49% of the 2017 levels by 2030.598,599 An annual investment of $640 to $910 billion for energy demand measures and $1.46 to $3.51 trillion towards energy supply is necessary between 2016 and 2050 to achieve carbon neutrality by 2050.600 The CCU process has emerged to be promising and attractive for mitigating CO2 emissions. It offers an alternative as a carbon source to generate carbon-containing valuable chemicals.601 Therefore, the exploration of the possibility for CO2 utilization as carbon feedstocks for conversion into value-added products or fuels has attracted much attention in recent decades.602–604 In 2020, Deng and co-workers presented a novel process of COG + BFG to MeOH process from steel refineries, which could produce about 179 kt of MeOH per year, achieving a total carbon efficiency of 72%. This efficient process leads to a net reduction in the CO2 emissions of 228 kt per year and fixes it in the conversion into methanol of 246 kt CO2 per year. The net present values of the latter process could be potentially increased by up to $54 million, compared with the current operating procedures. Overall, the production of MeOH is highly recommended for China, USA, and Mexico from both environmental and economic perspectives.605 Later on, Tian's group put forward a novel thermochemical calcium-looping methane reforming CCU scenario to realize the in situ coupling of CO2 capture and conversion in one integrated chemical process, leading to lower energy consumption than that of the traditional process.268 Zhao and co-workers proposed a novel simultaneous CO2 capture and utilization process with the deployment of chemical looping reforming for syngas production. The energy consumption is reduced by 68.6% and the CH4 conversion rate is over 92.1%, compared with that of the conventional processes.606
Recently, a collaborative research project aiming at the effective recycling of CO2 emitted from the Tomakomai refinery is commissioned by NEDO in Japan, which is about to start its use in February 2021.607 The integration of the CCU process is expected to combine the captured CO2 with H2 generated either from water electrolysis or as a by-product from refineries within the existing CCS facilities, which is favorable for enhancing the interoperability of both the facilities and sharing the CO2 recovery functions.608 The high economic consumption for the CO2 capture technique poses a severe challenge in the advancement of the CCU technology.10 The emission sources of CO2 could be divided into two categories: one is of high concentration (coal chemical industry, refinery, natural gas purification plant), and the other is of low concentration (coal-fired power plant, steel plant, cement plant). The capture cost for high concentration CO2 is largely lower than that for the opposite source. Post-combustion process, which is applied mainly for low CO2 partial pressure capture, has been a rather popular technology and has practically applied. The CO2 capture cost is about 300 RMB per t for the project implemented by Huaneng Power Corporation. The economic input for the carbon capture test platform started in 2019 of the Huarun Power Corporation is 500 RMB per t. The small scale and pilot tests of the oxy-fuel combustion process for coal-fired power plants were executed with a cost of 900 and 780 RMB per t at the Huazhong University of Science and Technology, respectively.
Recently, the U.S. DOE's Office of Fossil Energy made up to $131 million available to capture CO2 from industrial sources.609 On April 24th, 2020, the U.S. Department of Energy decided to fund multiple CCUS research projects. The fundamental purpose is to accelerate the regional deployment of the CCUS projects. The U.S., as a global leader in the fields of CCUS implementation, possesses 10 large-scale CCUS projects and captured more than 2500 t CO2 per year by the end of year 2019. The “Illinois Storage Corridor” project permits to store a combined 6.5 million metric tons of CO2 captured annually (DOE: $18 million; total: $25 million). The “San Juan Basin CarbonSAFE Phase III” project is about to accelerate the deployment of integrated CCS technology at the San Juan Generating Station, a nearby 847 megawatt coal-fired electricity generation plant. Approximately 6 to 7 million metric tons of CO2 will be captured per year, of which 2 million metric tons per year will be stored at a site approximately 20 miles from the station (DOE: $17.5 million; total: $22 million). The “Project ECO2S” will transport CO2 from Plant Daniel and Plant Miller, totaling 22.5 million metric tons per year (DOE: $17.5 million; total: $23.6 million). The “North Dakota CarbonSAFE Phase III” project will store over 3 million metric tons of CO2 per year from the Minnkota plant's 455 megawatt unit 2 (DOE: $17 million; total: $25 million). The “Wyoming CarbonSAFE” project utilizes Basin Electric's Dry Fork Station, a novel commercialized coal-fired power plant in the U.S., to source 2.2 million metric tons of CO2 per year for storage at three nearby sites within the same storage complex (DOE: $15 million; total: $19 million). The total investment of the above-mentioned projects is $85 million, as charged by the U.S. National Energy Technology Laboratory.609
Promising technologies and future plants for CCUS are summarized in Table 6. In general, the experiment and demonstration of CCUS in China are still at the early stages. The carbon capture sources are mainly focused on the coal-fired power generation and coal chemical industry. Tanker transportation remains the dominant mode of transport and there is seldom a project of pipeline transport, which is basically in the planning stages or under construction. In terms of carbon utilization and storage, the EOR is the research hotspot. The overall size of the experiment and the demonstration of CCUS in China is small with a relatively high cost. Only 3 of the completed CCUS demonstration projects in China have the ability to capture more than 100 thousand tons of CO2 per year, while the capture capacities of the other projects are normally less than 10 thousand tons CO2. Currently, most of the CCUS projects have a relatively high increment cost. For instance, the coal-fired power generation project would increase by 25–90% and 5–12% in terms of investment and operation and maintenance cost, while for the coal chemical industry, these are 1–1.3% and 7.5–8%, respectively. Most of the CCUS demonstration projects in China were set up after 2008 and put into use after 2010. The longest operation period of the demonstration project is only 10 years, and thus the demonstration experience is relatively limited. A newly-built gasification plant with the CO2 capture process is more cost-effective than a coal plant with post combustion capture. For most combustion power plants, CO2 capture requires large energy penalty and a high cost of technology. Thus, to reach affordable prices for the market, the CCU technology requires major breakthroughs in CO2 capture to reduce the overall cost of the system.16,610 CO2 conversion to carbon-neutral fuels at deployable scales requires significant development in the process design and catalysis. The production of renewable H2, as the primary cost source in CO2-to-fuel scenario, is of significant importance for cost reduction in order to move CCU forward.

5. Summary and future perspectives
Carbon capture and utilization has great potential for reducing carbon emissions. This review presented an authoritative, critical, and comprehensive review regarding the sustainable development for advanced improvements in CO2 capture and utilization, which has received tremendous attention due to its high potential towards intensifying global warming. For CO2 capture, pre- and post-combustion processes have attracted worldwide attention and emerged as two major techniques. CO2-EOR utilization and CO2 hydrate are useful techniques for CO2 utilization.
CCU shows technical potential to cut down the annual CO2 emission by up to 3.5 Gt CO2-eq. in 2030.275,580,612 Targeted subsidy policies for CO2 utilization should be formulated by the government.613 Despite the large gap existing between the low carbon credit price and the high CO2 abatement cost, the incorporation of the power plant with the CCU technology into the emission trading system could effectively ease the financial subsidy pressure.610 The Shanghai United Assets and Equity Exchange carried out the evaluation of national carbon emissions trading systems construction and announced that the trial operation conditions have been provided for the national carbon market. According to the project issued publicly (by the Chinese government, not only the world view or examples), the national carbon market is about to be put into operation in 2020, the first stage of which would mainly concentrate on power generation industries, including the cogeneration supply of electricity and heat energy and captive power plant.
The CO2 hydrogenation process could be carried out either by electrolytic hydrogenation in oil refineries or through the utilization of residual gas in coking plants. Without the wasted hydrogen, it could also combine with the renewable energies. The surplus energy could be directly deployed for electro-catalytic CO2 conversion and water electrolysis for hydrogen production. The generated renewable hydrogen energy could be further put into use. To realize highly effective carbon emission reduction, the carbon market and its mobility have become an active domain of academics and engineering all over the world. The exploration of carbon allowance as a direct reward to climate-friendly enterprises has demonstrated the value for the improvement of the high-quality low-carbon economic development. The companies with the carbon credit award are forced to deliver certain quotas for sale on the carbon market on a daily basis to boost the liquidity of the market. In the course of resourceful CO2 utilization, especially CO2 hydrogenation for the production of chemicals, the development of cost-effective carbon-free hydrogen generation technology is of significant importance. Except for H2 production by water electrolysis with a high cost, the other current industrial hydrogen production processes, such as the technology of hydrogen production from coal and natural gas, emit a large amount of CO2. To obtain inexpensive, carbon-free hydrogen sources, the most recent studies have focused on solar photocatalytic hydrolysis hydrogen production, solar energy photoelectric catalytic hydrolysis hydrogen production, the decomposition of hydrogen sulfide for H2 production, and so on. CO2-ECBM and CO2-EOR are two dominant procedures for utilizing CO2, hence demonstrating the significant importance of achieving stable and increased yield of most of the oilfields and the enhancement in the exploitation and utilization volume of coal bed methane. The extensive deployment of CO2-EOR could achieve substantial carbon storage and boost oil production, which is favorable for improving the economic benefits of the oil and gas industry and to mitigate the energy challenge to the security resulting from the rising external dependence on oil.608
The cost for the CCUS demonstration project is relatively high, which greatly hinders the development of the CCUS process. The investment of the current CCUS demonstration project is typically about hundreds of millions RMB and the introduction of carbon capture would increase the extra operation cost of 140–600 RMB per (CO2)t. Given the current CCUS technologies, the implementation of CCUS would increase the primary energy consumption by 10–20% with great efficiency lost, which is one of the major obstacles in achieving the wide applications of CCUS. The full process flow, integration, and scalable technology demonstration of CCUS technology could be progressively realized by stages and technology demonstration in a variety of fields. The accumulated experience obtained via widely executed experiments and identified key techniques would gradually accelerate the standard improvement and the cost reduction of the CCUS technology, which is essential for achieving the long-term commercial deployment of CCUS. The demonstration projects combined with lost-cost carbon capture and EOR are recommended to be widely deployed for coal chemical industries. Oil and gas enterprises are encouraged to take the initiative to start the CO2-EOR demonstration so as to enrich the engineering experience. The related strategic planning and system design of the CCUS should be strengthened to improve the policy support and economic motivation so as to progressively form a policy guarantee system that is beneficial for the virtuous development of CCUS, and to lay a solid foundation for CCUS to play a key role in long-term carbon emission reduction. The control of the total carbon emission should be intensified and the quantification of the constrained objectives for various industries should be established and proposed so as to boost the consciousness of low-carbon development and initiative desire of CCUS deployment for enterprises. Innovative incentive policies such as tax exemption and differentiated subsidies that benefit the development of CCUS are encouraged to be explored and executed. The CCUS commercialized investment and financing mechanism with the integration of the government and the market is well explored to support the CCUS demonstration project with the positive utilization of various modes as green finance, climate bonds, low-carbon fund, etc. In all, the outlook of future research and progress in CCU are presented in Table 7.

Table Content:

Wanlin Gao
	Wanlin Gao is currently a PhD candidate at the College of Environmental Science and Engineering, Beijing Forestry University in China under the supervision of Prof. Qiang Wang. Her research interests include the fabrication and application of intermediate-temperature solid CO2 adsorbents for sorption-enhanced water gas shift reaction and the deployment of in situ and operando analysis for CO2 capture mechanism study.

Qiang Wang
	Professor Qiang Wang received BSc (2003) and MSc (2005) from Harbin Institute of Technology in China, and PhD (2009) from POSTECH in South Korea. In 2009–2011, he worked as a research fellow at the Institute of Chemical and Engineering Sciences under A*STAR, Singapore. In 2011–2012, he worked as a postdoctoral associate at the Department of Chemistry, University of Oxford. Since 2012, he holds a full professor position at the College of Environmental Science and Engineering, Beijing Forestry University. He serves as the section editor (capture, storage, and chemical conversion of carbon dioxide) of the Journal of Energy Chemistry and the editorial boards of several scientific journals. His current research interests include environmental functional nanomaterials for air pollution control and CO2 capture and utilizations (CCU).

Anne-Cecile Roger
	Professor Anne-Cécile Roger obtained her PhD from the University of Strasbourg in 1995 in the field of heterogeneous catalysis (oxidative coupling of methane). She obtained her Accreditation to Direct Research in 2003 around the direct and indirect valorization of natural gas. She got her first position as an Assistant Professor in 1996 and became full professor in 2011 at the same university. She is the deputy director of ICPEES (Institute of Chemistry and Processes for Energy, Environment and Health, UMR CNRS 7515) since 2018. After a strong experience in Fischer–Tropsch synthesis for almost 10 years, she has been, since more than ten years, involved in the development of mixed oxide-type catalytic materials for CO2 hydrogenation (methane, methanol, DME, higher alcohols) and Power-to-X technologies.

Rose Amal
	Professor Rose Amal is a Scientia Professor in the School of Chemical Engineering, UNSW Sydney. Her current research focuses on designing nanomaterials for solar to chemical energy conversion applications (including photo and electrocatalysis for water and air purification, water splitting, CO2 reduction) and engineering systems for solar-induced processes. Prof. Amal is a Fellow of the Australian Academy of Technological Sciences and Engineering (FTSE), a Fellow of Australian Academy of Science (FAA). She has received the nation's top civilian honour – the Companion of the Order of Australia – as part of the 2018 Queen's Birthday Honours for her eminent service to chemical engineering, and was named 2019 NSW Scientist of the Year.

Hong He
	Prof. Hong He is the Member of the Chinese Academy of Engineering, the Deputy Director of the Research Center for Eco-Environmental Sciences (RCEES), Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS). Prof. He received his doctorate (PhD) from the University of Tokyo in 1994. He studied and worked in Japan, the United States, and Canada for 11 years, and thereafter joined RCEES through the “Hundred Talents Program” of the CAS in 2001. His research has mainly focused on fundamental and applied research in environmental catalysis and heterogeneous atmospheric chemistry, with a series of achievements in the areas of diesel emission control technologies, catalytic indoor air purification, and haze formation mechanisms. Prof. He was awarded the National Technology Invention Award (China) in 2011, the National Science and Technology Progress Award (China) in 2014, the Ho Leung Ho Lee Foundation Prize for Scientific and Technological Innovation in 2017, and the National Natural Science Award (China) in 2019.

Sang-Eon Park
	Prof. Sang-Eon Park is the Emeritus Professor of Inha Univ., and has been the director of the Laboratory of Nano-Green Catalysis and Nano Center. He has a PhD (1981) from KAIST and experience from Chon Engrg. Co. for 6 years with a post-doc at Texas A. & M. He worked as a principal investigator in KRICT for 16 years. Now, he is the Editor-in-Chief of the Journal of CO2 Utilization and also serves as the Honorary Prof. of several Universities, the editorial boards of several scientific journals, and as an advisory member of several leading international conferences including ICCDU. He has published more than 350 scientific articles and 80 patents, with 7 industrialized projects. His current research interest involves carbon dioxide utilization as an oxidant coined as Soft Oxidant, Promoter, and Enhancer.

 	Fig. 63  (a) Electronic energy distribution upon LSPR excitation and its relaxation processes (Reproduced from ref. 512 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2015). Initially, the electrons follow a Fermi–Dirac distribution at the thermal temperature of the system. The photoexcitation of the electrons from the filled states to the unfilled states generates a non-thermal electron distribution (0–10 fs). The electron distribution thermalizes to an elevated electron temperature through electron–electron scattering (500 fs). The hot electron distribution cools over time as it couples with the substrate phonons (1 ps). (b) Plasmon-induced hot carrier generation and hot electron transfer/back-transfer processes in clean metal, metal/adsorbate, and metal/semiconductor systems. In each case, the left component (relative to energy y-axis) displays the Fermi level (EF) of the plasmonic metals and the right component (relative to energy y-axis) illustrates the HOMOs and LUMOs for the adsorbates, or the CB and VB positions for the semiconductors. The gray regions represent the population of the electronic states (Reproduced from ref. 506 with permission from American Chemical Society, copyright 2018).

CH4 production is strongly and selectively enhanced by ultraviolet light on the Rh photo-catalyst. (b) Selectivity towards CH4 of the thermo- (black circles) and photocatalytic reactions under ultraviolet (365 nm, red squares) and blue (460 nm, blue triangles) illumination as a function of temperature under H2-rich (CO2:H2 = 1:5.5, solid symbols) and H2-deficient (CO2:H2 = 1:3.1, open symbols) conditions. (c) LDOS for adsorbed CHO on C(pz), O(pz), and Rh(d) orbitals. The Rh(100) surface is perpendicular to the x direction and the C–O bond is along the y direction. Major bands are identified as (1) C–O p bonding band (6.5 eV) with C(pz) (black) and O(pz) (red) interactions; (2) C–O p* anti-bonding band (1–3 eV, mode around 2 eV) with C(pz) and O(pz) interactions (Reproduced from ref. 504 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2017). The error bars indicate the deviation among three independent experiments; (f) time-on-stream MeOH yield under different light irradiation conditions (350–800 nm, 200–500 nm, and 420–800 nm, which corresponds to the excitation of Cu, ZnO, and Cu + ZnO, respectively). P = 21 bar, T = 225 °C, light intensity = 600 mW cm−2 for all the spectral ranges (Reproduced from ref. 496 with permission from Springer Nature, copyright 2020). CO2 chemical utilization	Production of a series of chemicals such as organic carbonates, methanol, degradable polymers, etc.
(2) Breakthroughs in key capture technologies	Post-combustion CO2 capture	Integration of advanced absorbents, highly-efficient reactor, and novel energy-efficiency technologies.
Pre-combustion CO2 capture	Chemical engineering kinetics co-generation of coal gasification and flue gas decarbonization, development of advanced adsorbents, and technologies.
(3) System integration of the whole-process and large-scale demonstration	Large-scale CCUS system	Integration and optimization of CO2 capture and emission source, pre-/post-combustion capture facilities of capturing level of million tonnes CO2 per year.
CO2 enhanced oil recovery and storage, storage capacity and utilization potential, characterization and site selection, etc.
